104  A MADCAP CRUISE
105  
106  
107  
108  
109  Chapter One
110  
111  THE CARDINAL POINTS
112  
113  
114  "It strikes me," said Jerrold Taberman, "that we are booked for
115  everlasting fame, win or lose. We'll either sail down the ages as a
116  brace of heroes, or as the most egregious pair of donkeys that ever
117  botched a job."
118  
119  "Well, Jerry," returned his companion, smiling, "you've as much to do
120  with making the thing a success as I have. I hope you realize the
121  responsibility."
122  
123  The young men chuckled in concert at the thought of all that was
124  involved in this remark, although they looked, not at each other, but
125  out over the sea.
126  
127  It was early twilight in the last week of the month of May. The two
128  speakers were standing on a little jetty that ran out into a small and
129  all but landlocked harbor of an island in East Penobscot Bay. Both were
130  evidently in the earlier twenties, both were dressed in such canvas
131  working-suits as are worn by the sailors in our navy, and both were, at
132  half a glance, gentlemen.
133  
134  The second speaker, John Castleport, was tall and dark. His face, with
135  its prominent features and keen brown eyes, was rather striking than
136  handsome. He stood looking southward to where, in the fading light, the
137  Atlantic shouldered away to the west as if with a hidden purpose of its
138  own. In his hand he held a pair of powerful binoculars, and despite his
139  smile he had the air of being pretty seriously in earnest.
140  
141  Taberman contrasted curiously with his host. He was short and thickset,
142  with blue eyes and fair hair which showed a tendency to curl. As he
143  stood with shoulders turned to the wind, the square collar of his canvas
144  jumper was blown against his round pate, and made a background for his
145  tanned face. He held a briar drop-pipe between his teeth, and his hands
146  were thrust deep into his trousers pockets. Working his pipe into the
147  corner of his mouth, he spoke again.
148  
149  "Hope this breeze won't trouble the old gentleman," he remarked, casting
150  a glance at the billowing double-headers that were driving by aloft.
151  
152  The wind shrilled by the watchers on the jetty, clear, strong, and
153  salt.
154  
155  "Guess not," replied Castleport; "anything short of a hurricane's a
156  sailing-wind for him. He's a mettlesome old chap."
157  
158  "That's right enough. Can't have him spoiling our game by being late,
159  you know. Let's go up; it's getting beastly chilly."
160  
161  They turned and walked along the pier. At the point where it met the
162  shore stood a small boathouse. Thence the ground, covered with a stunted
163  growth of spruce and fir, and the inevitable New England boulders, rose
164  abruptly. Directly in the line of the jetty the shingled roof of a small
165  house showed above the trees. To the westward, in the dimming afterglow
166  of the sunset, the Camden Hills stood out luminous, purple, yet rimmed
167  with a thread of golden fire. Away to the east, clad in soberer colors,
168  rose Mt. Desert, a mass of shadowy greens and blues. The steepness of
169  the path they were ascending soon cut off from the view of the young men
170  these beauties and grandeurs, which, however, they were probably not in
171  a mood to dwell upon; and a minute's walking brought them to the door of
172  the house, a small affair with high-pitched roof and broad veranda. Its
173  shingles were almost the color of the dark evergreens that encircled
174  the clearing in which it stood; its windows reflected with a vacant and
175  glassy stare the fast-fading light. Castleport opened the door for his
176  guest, and followed him into the living-room.
177  
178  The darkness seemed the greater from its contrast with what light yet
179  remained outside, and not until Taberman had put a match to the pile of
180  old shingles and light driftwood in the wide fireplace could they see
181  fairly. The crimson glow showed a room some twenty feet square, with
182  windows on two sides,--the south and east. The joists and sheathing were
183  of planed spruce, left unpainted. The big Mexican fireplace of brick
184  occupied the northwestern corner; in the middle of the room stood
185  conspicuously a round deal table, covered with a litter of pipes,
186  tobacco, magazines, and nautical hardware; between the two eastern
187  windows, below a box-like cabinet which was attached to the wall, was a
188  smaller table with a square top, piled with books and charts. Beneath
189  the southern windows was placed a heavy desk with a faded baize top, the
190  cloth ink-stained and full of holes due to moths and carelessly handled
191  cigars. Of the happy-go-lucky assortment of chairs which completed the
192  furniture of the room, no large portion was in an entirely unbroken
193  condition, but all evidently were meant for service and ease. The walls
194  of the room were decorated with devices in scallop-shells and a few
195  unframed water-colors of the impressionist type. A large chart of
196  Penobscot Bay was tacked to the inside of the door, and a venerable
197  flintlock musket hung below a battered quadrant over the chimneypiece.
198  Everything was simple almost to rudeness, yet the place gave at once and
199  most strongly the impression of comfort and good-fellowship.
200  
201  Castleport laid his binoculars on the desk, and, stepping to a door on
202  his right, opened it and called out:--
203  
204  "Oh, Gonzague?"
205  
206  "Sair?" promptly replied some one from beyond the short passage into
207  which he looked.
208  
209  "Dinner when you're ready, Gonzague."
210  
211  "A' right, sair."
212  
213  Taberman had seated himself by the fire, and here Castleport joined him.
214  Each filled and lighted a pipe, and together they stared at the flames
215  roaring up the wide chimney. The smaller sticks already began to fall
216  apart, pitching outward or dropping between the dogs, and for some
217  moments the young men watched them in silence. At length, as Taberman
218  flung a fresh stick into the flames, Castleport spoke, half to himself.
219  
220  "What a lesson it'll be to the old chap! My aunt! He'll grind his teeth
221  to powder!"
222  
223  "Tooth-powder, eh?" queried the other with a grin. "But we must be sure
224  we have the laugh on the right side. It isn't merely the getting away
225  with the Merle that's the joke; it's the hanging on to her and bringing
226  her back safe."
227  
228  "That's true enough," assented Castleport; "but with pluck and luck and
229  an eye to the three L's, we ought to manage."
230  
231  "You'd better go over the whole plan for me, Jack; you haven't given me
232  half the details, and I'd like to know the latest version. It's
233  certainly important to have everything perfectly understood beforehand."
234  
235  "All right; I'll go over the whole business after dinner, old man. We
236  will act the conspirators rehearsing their villainy; but let's wait for
237  food. I hate discussions on an empty stomach."
238  
239  "Correct; here's Gonzague now."
240  
241  A tall, gray-haired man, with a much-bronzed face, came in and began to
242  clear away the litter on the round table. He had a rugged,
243  weather-beaten countenance, with prominent features and luminous black
244  eyes. Beneath his big, hooked nose a large white mustache, stiff and
245  curled like that of a walrus, half hid a firm, full-lipped mouth. A
246  native of Provence,--soldier, sailor, cook, and deck-hand,--old Gonzague
247  Mairecalde had led sixty-odd years of exciting and polyglot existence,
248  the last three of which had been spent in Castleport's service. Dressed
249  in blue flannel trousers and an immaculate white jacket, the old man
250  moved noiselessly about, swiftly disposing of the things on the table.
251  He seemed to have a place for everything, and the lightest tread and
252  deftest hands imaginable. Having cleared away, he went out, and soon
253  reappeared with linen and service. In a short time the table was ready
254  for the bringing in of the food.
255  
256  "A' ready, sair?" asked Gonzague, tugging at his mustache with his bony
257  fingers.
258  
259  "Two minutes," answered Jack. "Come on, Jerry; let's scrub up."
260  
261  In ten minutes they were seated before a dinner plain but hearty, well
262  cooked and appetizingly served. They were apparently not at all troubled
263  by any incongruity between their rough and not over-fresh sailor clothes
264  and the snowy napery and the silver on which the fire threw dancing and
265  wavering lights. On the walls opposite the fireplace mute, shadowy
266  grotesques helped each other to huge supplies from dishes of vague
267  outline and uncertain size, plied dark forks and spoons with ogre-like
268  gusto, or with heads thrown back and crooked elbows drank like trolls
269  from enormous tankards.
270  
271  After dinner the table was cleared, a jug of ale was placed upon it,
272  with a plate of ship-biscuit and a supply of tobacco. It was the theory
273  of Castleport that the climate of the Island was English enough to
274  warrant this nightly attack upon the October, of which his uncle, who
275  owned the Island, kept always a butt in the cellar. In truth, the fresh
276  coolness of the air at night, the pleasant blaze of the fire, the
277  agreeable scent of burning tobacco, made a tankard or two of ale seem
278  hardly to need an excuse of any sort.
279  
280  With the table pulled forward so that its edge came between them, their
281  pipes lit, their feet stretched out comfortably toward the hearth, the
282  pair of friends smoked for a time in silence, until at last Jack, after
283  refilling and relighting his pipe with great deliberation, broke into
284  speech.
285  
286  "Before I go into the details of this job," he observed, "there's one
287  thing I have to say. It's a waste of breath for me to talk until I know
288  you're with me. I haven't done anything more than to ask you off-hand,
289  old man; now I'd like you to say seriously whether you'll come on this
290  cruise with me or not. I hate to be so horribly businesslike, Jerry,
291  especially in the matter of a lark; but in--er--larking on this scale,
292  things have got to be put on a definite basis,--be perfectly understood,
293  as you said before dinner."
294  
295  Taberman gave his companion a sidelong glance, and began to smile. The
296  smile grew into an audible chuckle; and this in its turn developed into
297  a laugh increasing to a jovial roar.
298  
299  "You solemn old pirate," he cried, "what sort of a quitter do you take
300  me for? I'll give you any kind of a promise you like, provided--_semper
301  more equitis_, you know--Can't bind myself to cut throats, scuttle
302  ships, fly the jolly roger, et cetera. What's your form of oath, eh? Do
303  we drink each other's blood out of a skull, or what?"
304  
305  There was a boyish exuberance about Jerrold Taberman, a debonair
306  abandon, which he never could outgrow. It accorded well with his
307  youthful face and careless mien, which made him so marked a contrast to
308  his friend. Castleport, although impulsive and disposed to jollity as
309  only a hale and hearty young man of twenty-two can be, was, on the
310  whole, of a temperament the reverse of boisterous. He responded frankly
311  to Jerry's outburst.
312  
313  "Well, old man," said he, "there's nothing more needed than your word
314  that you'll go, and stick it out to the end. I knew you would, Jerry.
315  Confound it, give us your flipper!"
316  
317  In his enthusiasm he caught Taberman's hand and wrung it heartily, being
318  evidently moved more by some inner consciousness of the weighty nature
319  of the scheme he was about to outline than by anything that had actually
320  been said between them. Jerry laughed, and returned the grip with
321  interest.
322  
323  "And now," continued Castleport, "I'll let you have particulars galore.
324  I'll tell you the beginning of it first: how the idea came to me. About
325  three weeks ago I decided I'd go abroad,--I wrote you, you remember.
326  Well, I went to Uncle Randolph, and asked him for a letter of credit.
327  That's what comes of the pleasant arrangement by which all my property's
328  in trust till I'm twenty-five! Beastly nuisance!"
329  
330  "Of course it is," assented his companion. "It's queer your father made
331  such a will. However," he added, as if with the feeling that he was
332  perhaps touching upon delicate ground, "that's neither here nor there.
333  Heave ahead."
334  
335  "You know why I wanted to go," Jack went on, "and so"--
336  
337  "Slow up a bit," interrupted the other, mischief shining in his eyes;
338  "why should you want to go particularly?"
339  
340  "Confound you!" retorted Castleport. "You know perfectly well! Do you
341  think it's any fun to be here when--when"--
342  
343  "When Miss Marchfield's on the other side," finished Jerry, with the air
344  of enjoying a huge joke.
345  
346  Jack shifted uncomfortably in his seat, leaned forward to rap the ashes
347  out of his pipe on the firedog, and then looked at his friend seriously.
348  
349  "I won't be roughed, Jerry," he said. "You know perfectly well I'm dead
350  in earnest about her, and I'll thank you to let up."
351  
352  "All right, Jack; I beg your pardon; but I would like to ask one thing.
353  It's not exactly my business, of course, but really it's something I'd
354  like to know in connection with this scheme."
355  
356  "Fire away," Castleport said rather grimly.
357  
358  "Well, then, what I want to know is why the President's so set against
359  your marrying Katrine Marchfield?"
360  
361  "It isn't time to talk of marrying," Jack returned somewhat stiffly.
362  "She may have something to say to that."
363  
364  "Of course, old fellow; but you know what I mean. What's his objection
365  to your trying?"
366  
367  "I don't see how that affects the cruise, exactly, but I don't mind
368  telling you; only of course I shouldn't want it talked about. It's so
369  unreasonable, and honestly I should hate to seem to be giving Uncle
370  Randolph any sort of a black eye."
371  
372  "I shouldn't repeat it, Jack; but you needn't say anything if you'd
373  rather not."
374  
375  "It's only that it looks as if Uncle Randolph was infernally obstinate
376  and cranky, and he really isn't. He hadn't any reason to give me, that
377  amounted to anything. He talked about Katrine's not having any money;
378  but of course that's all poppy-cock. I've got a good bit myself when I
379  come into it, and he's always told me I should have all his. Of course
380  Katrine hasn't much, though she'll have something, I suppose, from her
381  aunt."
382  
383  "Aunt?"
384  
385  "Why, Mrs. Fairhew. Katrine's traveling with her now. She's the only
386  near relative Katrine has."
387  
388  "But if it isn't money"--
389  
390  "No, it isn't that. The truth is--I heard it from Mrs. Fairhew once; I
391  wasn't sure then, and I'm not now, whether she knew quite how much she
392  was telling me, and meant it for a warning, or not. I'm half inclined to
393  think she did."
394  
395  "But what was it?" inquired Jerry, as Jack paused to meditate, with his
396  eyes fixed earnestly on the fire.
397  
398  "Oh, Uncle Randolph had some sort of a row with Katrine's father when
399  they were young men. I fancy it was about a girl, for I know there was
400  one somewhere along about that time. I've heard father speak of it, and
401  say it altered Uncle Randolph's whole life. Anyway, there was some sort
402  of a scrap, and Uncle Randolph never forgave it."
403  
404  "Humph!" was Taberman's comment. "It's rather crotchety of him to vent
405  his spite on Miss Marchfield."
406  
407  "Of course it is," Castleport answered, "but he's not so bad as it
408  looks. He's been awfully good to me all my life."
409  
410  A brief pause followed, in which both were probably reflecting upon the
411  character of Randolph Drake, one of Boston's prominent men, president of
412  one of the largest banks, and trustee of a dozen important corporations;
413  a man whose chief aim in life was, apparently, making money, whose
414  amusement was yachting. It was in connection with this sport that he had
415  a few years before bought the island and put up the house in which his
416  motives were now being discussed. The place served as a shooting-box or
417  as a base of supplies, and was provided with a trig little harbor
418  exactly adapted for the accommodation of the President's yacht, the
419  Merle.
420  
421  "After all," Jack said at length, "Uncle Randolph really cares more for
422  me than he does for anything else in the world."
423  
424  "And so when he suspected that you were going abroad to try to marry the
425  daughter of his old enemy, he wouldn't supply the funds."
426  
427  "He can't seem to get it into his head that I am grown up, anyhow,"
428  grumbled Jack. "I've made up my mind now that I'll convince him that I
429  am."
430  
431  "Why in the world didn't you borrow the money, Jack? That would have
432  been easy enough."
433  
434  "Well, when I came of age I made Uncle Randolph a sort of a promise that
435  I wouldn't borrow. He put it that it would be evading the intent of my
436  father's will; and of course it would. Anyway, Uncle Randolph himself
437  put a bigger idea into my head. It took me one day and two nights,
438  mostly without sleep, to think it out, and then I got hold of you."
439  
440  "How did he suggest it?"
441  
442  "He was really sorry for me; I could see that. Only he had the air of
443  feeling I was so young that any other cake would do as well as the one
444  I wanted. The very day that he refused to let me go abroad, he suggested
445  that I come down here with Gonzague and some friend or other. He thought
446  that if I fooled round the bay until he came to pick me up on the Merle,
447  I should get over my wish to go abroad. He said I was run down, needed
448  change, and so on. He's coming June 5, and plans to go on down to the
449  Provinces. Then he said that after he had had his cruise on the Merle I
450  might perhaps like to have her a week or two myself. It was a mighty
451  great concession, let me tell you. When I think of taking the boat, I'm
452  half ashamed of myself, the old gentleman's so rum fond of her."
453  
454  "And that put the notion into your head?"
455  
456  "Yes, only not at the moment. I said to myself that if I was going to
457  cruise in the Merle I'd like to go across in her; but it wasn't till
458  that night, just as I was turning in, that the idea of getting her now
459  and running off came to me. It fairly bowled me over!"
460  
461  "I should think it might!" laughed Taberman.
462  
463  "At first it seemed the easiest thing in the world. Then I began to
464  think of objections, and as fast as I got one out of the way another
465  popped up. I've worked at it like a prize puzzle. I've got my crew
466  picked out, I've planned how to get possession of the yacht and to get
467  rid of her old crew; and then--Hurrah for the Mediterranean!"
468  
469  "Oh, Jacko, you devil!" cried Taberman. "I wouldn't have believed you
470  had it in you! Do you really think we can do it?"
471  
472  "Do it! Of course we'll do it. Didn't I tell you I'd got my crew
473  already? Ten strappers, not counting Gonzague."
474  
475  "Did Gonzague kick?"
476  
477  "Gonzague? Did you ever consider, Tab, those eyes of his, with that nose
478  and mouth?"
479  
480  "No," Jerry responded, "I've never given his features any especial
481  critical overhauling."
482  
483  "_Saracen!_" Jack said, lowering his voice. "When you see that
484  combination in a Spaniard or a Provençalese, it spells Moorish marauder
485  every time. He doesn't know it, I fancy; but there's good old ripe
486  Moorish pirate blood in him, and it came sizzling to the top the moment
487  I broached the scheme. Besides, Gonzague would have his throat cut for
488  me any time."
489  
490  "That's so, but he's as honest an old soul as there is above ground."
491  
492  "Of course I told him, and I told the crew, that it was a lark. You
493  know I've knocked about Penobscot Bay ever since I got out of the
494  nursery. Everybody knows me, and at Isle au Haut I've been so much that
495  I'm almost like one of their own pals to the natives. I got hold of my
496  men pretty easily. Of course they look on me as the same as the
497  President's son; and they were willing enough to leave the fishing for
498  better wages than they could earn anywhere else. They all like me, and
499  so of course they all take advantage of me in the way of wages."
500  
501  "I confess I don't see where your economy comes in, Jacky," observed
502  Taberman, giving a poke to the wasting fire. "I don't know much about
503  expenses, but I should think it would cost as much to hire a crew as to
504  go without one."
505  
506  Castleport grew grave and moved a little impatiently.
507  
508  "There's a question for a casuist," he said. "I'm taking these men off
509  on the trust that Uncle Randolph will let me pay them when I get home.
510  It's a deuced sight more like borrowing than I wish it were, though of
511  course my allowance comes in; but I'm bound that he shall get it into
512  his head that I'm no longer in leading-strings, and"--
513  
514  Taberman looked at him affectionately and comprehendingly.
515  
516  "That'll be all right, old man," he said consolingly. "We'll get out of
517  that somehow. I'd like to see the President's face when he finds he's
518  left high and dry down here and the Merle has flitted across the
519  Atlantic without him."
520  
521  "Oh, he won't be here. We'll capture the yacht at North Haven. I'll show
522  you the whole scheme to-morrow on the chart. I've brought down more than
523  a thousand for this coast and the Mediterranean! Now let's get to bed.
524  It's only a week or so that we have left to sleep with a clear
525  conscience."
526  
527  Taberman rose from his seat, then without warning suddenly slapped his
528  knees with his hands and burst into a roar of laughter.
529  
530  "Oh, by George," he cried, "what a jolt it'll be for Uncle Randolph!"
531  
532  "That's the cream of the whole thing," responded Jack, joining in the
533  laugh. "He'll be so surprised to find out that I'm grown up."
534  
535  [Illustration: Decoration]
536  
537  
538  
539  
540  Chapter Two
541  
542  THE FOG COMES IN
543  
544  
545  The Casino at North Haven is a curious little box, known
546  locally--possibly from its situation at the end of a fairly long
547  wharf--as the "Fo'c'sle." It has but one room, paneled with imitation
548  Japanese carvings, and having an attractive divan-like seat in a wide
549  bay-window, where one may lounge and watch the vessels passing through
550  the Thoroughfare. Outwardly the building is very plain, its two
551  prominent features being the bay-window, which looks south, and a flight
552  of outside stairs on the west which lead to a little nest of a balcony
553  half hidden under the gable-end of the roof above this window.
554  
555  The balcony is so covered by the peak of the roof that its interior is
556  not visible from the wharf, and a person sitting on the settle at the
557  back of it can be seen only from a boat some distance out on the water.
558  
559  The Casino is little used, and although the caretaker unlocks the door
560  each morning, the place is more generally deserted than not. The
561  subscribers who come down to the wharf to start for rowing or sailing
562  sometimes step in, wait for friends, or use the place as a storage for
563  extra wraps; sometimes a riotous group of children holds brief but noisy
564  possession; but after sunset the solitude is generally unbroken until
565  ten o'clock, when the caretaker comes to lock up for the night. If the
566  weather be bad, it is not unusual for the Casino to remain unvisited for
567  the entire day. It affords a convenient shelter when it is needed,
568  however, and its wharf, with a float on either side, makes a good
569  landing-place; and it is, in a word, one of the numerous class of things
570  which in this world are not constantly in demand, but which, when they
571  are wanted at all, are wanted badly.
572  
573  Here, on the evening of the fourth of June, Jerrold Taberman, wrapped in
574  a shapeless ulster,--for a thick fog was driving in from the
575  southeast,--sat awaiting his friend. Half an hour earlier Jack had gone
576  to get something to eat, and Jerry had agreed to meet him here. Taberman
577  was somewhat tired to-night, and beginning to feel the strain of three
578  crowded and exciting days in which he had had little time for anything
579  but action and sleep. The young men had completed their arrangements at
580  the Island, had left Gonzague in charge there, had notified the future
581  crew to report to the Provençalese on the evening of the third, and to
582  hold themselves in readiness to sail immediately on the arrival of the
583  Merle. The pair had then taken the big market-boat, a whitehall used for
584  bringing supplies from Isle au Haut, and with a couple of the most able
585  of the Isle au Haut men, selected beforehand, had sailed over to an
586  unfrequented cove in Vinal Haven, on the south side of the Thoroughfare.
587  There they encamped in hiding. They had reached their place of
588  concealment by night, and next afternoon had the satisfaction of seeing
589  the Merle come in from the westward and drop anchor just inside the
590  channel, off the "Fo'c'sle."
591  
592  "By Jove, isn't she a fine sight!" Castleport exclaimed
593  enthusiastically; and Jerry assented no less warmly.
594  
595  The Merle ran in under full sail, with a quartering breeze. Her clean
596  white hull, eighty-four feet on the water-line, her shining brasses, her
597  broad spread of snowy canvas, the easy run of her long counter, combined
598  to make a picture which, even personal interest aside, could not fail to
599  stir such enthusiasts as Jack and Tab.
600  
601  On the evening of the arrival of the Merle two gentlemen and three
602  ladies had gone on board, evidently to dine, as they did not leave until
603  nearly ten o'clock. Castleport and Taberman, lying concealed among the
604  bushes overgrowing a tiny promontory on Vinal Haven, had watched all
605  this through their night-glasses. Jack, whose eyes were as keen as a
606  hawk's, had even thought that he could distinguish who the visitors
607  were. With guests on board there was evidently nothing that the
608  conspirators could do but to watch, and when this was over they smoked a
609  good-night pipe together over their campfire, and for the hundredth time
610  fell to considering their chances of success. Behind them in the shadow
611  lay the two sailors, wrapped in their blankets and sleeping the sleep
612  which only the genuine mariner knows; Jack glanced at them as if he felt
613  that somehow he was personally responsible for carrying through the
614  enterprise for which they had been enlisted.
615  
616  "What the deuce shall we do if the President takes it into his head to
617  get under weigh for the island to-morrow?" Jerry demanded in a subdued
618  voice.
619  
620  "Oh, that's all right," Jack answered in the same key. "He won't. He's
621  fond of North Haven; it's an old stamping-ground of his, and he'll never
622  go on without having had at least one night's bridge here. That's part
623  of the cruise. Besides, it's going to be thick, or I'm a duffer."
624  
625  Thick it certainly was next day. The brisk southeasterly breeze that
626  blew through the Thoroughfare all day seemed to roll in white billows of
627  fog far more rapidly than it could take them out at the other end. The
628  strait acted as a sort of condenser, in which the mist became almost
629  tangibly more solid, until at nightfall it was, as one of Castleport's
630  men put it, "blacker 'n a tar-bucket." Under cover of the obscurity Jack
631  had had the market-boat reloaded with such necessities as they had
632  brought over for their camp, and rowed silently over to one of the
633  Casino floats. Here he and Taberman got out, and then the men, by his
634  orders, worked the boat into concealment between the spiles of the
635  wharf, there to await further orders, utterly invisible in the fog.
636  
637  The two arch-conspirators mounted the wharf, and for some time kept
638  watch to see if any one came ashore from the Merle; but as the time wore
639  on to half-past seven they concluded that the President must be dining
640  on board. Assured of this, Jack left Jerry to keep watch, and went up to
641  the village bakery for food, dinner for himself and his friend having
642  been forgotten in the midst of more important things. Tab, left alone
643  in the wet darkness, had mounted to the balcony, and there sat in gloomy
644  state, wondering if Jack were never coming back. He had no light by
645  which to see his watch, but since he had heard seven bells from the
646  Merle he felt sure that eight o'clock must be close at hand, when his
647  attention was caught by the sound through the fog of the quick
648  _thud-thud_, _thud-thud_ of oars against thole-pins. In an instant he
649  was thoroughly alert, his senses primitively acute, and his growing
650  sensation of vague depression utterly dispelled. He heard some one pull
651  hastily to the "Fo'c'sle;" the muffled chugging of the oar-blades as the
652  rower held water; the gentle slapping of the boat's wash against the
653  float; and then the clatter of the oars on the thwarts. Then by the dim
654  light of the lantern at the end of the pier he saw a man spring on to
655  the east float and secure his boat; give a quick, nervous tug at the
656  painter to be sure that it was fast, and disappear from the field of
657  vision which was bounded by the edge of the sloping roof. He fancied he
658  heard a murmur as if the newcomer spoke a word of encouragement to the
659  sailors in damp concealment under the wharf, and then had hardly time to
660  wonder where Jack had been in a boat, before Castleport had run lightly
661  up the plank from the float to the pier, and thence up the steps to
662  Tab's place of concealment.
663  
664  "Sit tight!" whispered Castleport breathlessly.
665  
666  "What's--" began Jerry.
667  
668  "Sh! We've the chance of a lifetime! I--I"--He gasped for breath, but
669  caught it with a great gulp, and hurried on. "I've been aboard, Tab!
670  Come in, man! Get back, get back!" He forced his friend into a seat in
671  the farthest corner of the little balcony, caught his breath again, and
672  began to chuckle. The sound of oars was again audible,--this time the
673  steady, measured stroke of a heavy boat well pulled.
674  
675  "Here's Uncle Randolph," cried Jack with a sort of whispered shout.
676  "Here's Uncle Randolph!" And seizing his friend by the shoulders, he
677  shook him and banged his head noiselessly against the wall for sheer
678  glee.
679  
680  "Stop, Jacko, stop it! Hold up, or by Jumbo I'll yell! Look there! Here
681  they are."
682  
683  As the pair hurried cautiously to look out over the edge of the balcony,
684  a large cutter, pulled by six men, came out of the fog into the dim
685  illumination of the pier-light. Three gentlemen in light overcoats were
686  visible in the stern-sheets, the one in the middle steering. A little
687  removed from the President and the two men who were evidently his
688  guests, sat one of the officers of the Merle.
689  
690  "Way enough," called the steersman in a sharp voice.
691  
692  "Oh, my aunt!" whispered Tab, giving Jack a nudge. "The President has
693  very little idea that he's made all the way in the Merle he's likely to
694  for one while."
695  
696  The cutter ran smoothly along beside the float.
697  
698  "In bows! Fend off, there!"
699  
700  At the word the oars were unshipped, and a couple of sailors caught the
701  rope which edged the staging. The cutter came to a stop. A seaman leaped
702  out and held the boat, the officer sprang to the float and presented an
703  arm for the President and his guests as they stepped to land.
704  
705  "We'll be down at eleven," the President said to the officer. "If you
706  want an hour or two ashore, there's some sort of a shindy going on
707  opposite the post office, I believe--dance or something. Mind you're
708  sharp on time for me, though."
709  
710  "All right, sir. Eleven o'clock it is, sir," returned the officer,
711  touching his cap deferentially as the three gentlemen turned away.
712  
713  "Great Scott!" cried Jack into Tab's ear in an excited whisper. "Do you
714  suppose the President's going to get rid of all those men for me
715  himself? Was ever such luck!"
716  
717  The boat still lay at the landing. The men began to discuss going
718  ashore, and every word was easily audible to the two watchers in the
719  balcony.
720  
721  "I vote we go," quoth he with the boat-hook. "It ain't every day the old
722  hunks gives us a chance to stretch a leg ashore."
723  
724  "It'll be dry, Tom," spoke up one in the boat. "Ye won't get so much as
725  a swig o' cider-water this side o' Bar Harbor."
726  
727  "Well, boys, let's try it, anyhow," advised the officer. "If it's dry
728  there, it's wet enough here."
729  
730  "That's right," responded another. "Damn yer slops, Bill, ye dude; the'
731  're's good as mine, an' any togs is good enough for po'r Jack. Let's go
732  ashore an' take a look at these Thoryfare bewties."
733  
734  This seemed to settle it. The boat was made fast, and the men straggled
735  up the pier, talking and laughing as they went.
736  
737  Tab and Jack fairly hugged each other in delight at this development,
738  and then Jerry opened fire.
739  
740  "You said you'd been aboard," he began, "what"--
741  
742  "When I left the bakery," Jack answered, without waiting for the
743  question to be finished, "I said to myself that the fog was so thick it
744  would be perfectly safe to take a boat and row out, on the chances that
745  I might find out something. I meant to get astern of the Merle and give
746  the wind a chance to bring me some of the talk aboard. I borrowed a
747  little pea-pod from the pier behind Staples', and out I went. When I got
748  to the yacht, I found I could lay alongside, for there wasn't a soul on
749  deck. I hauled off my jacket and hung it over the boat's side for a
750  fender, so she wouldn't make any noise, and took the painter in my fist.
751  Then I stood on the thwart and jumped for the rail on the port side."
752  
753  "You'd have made the devil of a mess if you'd missed it," commented
754  Jerry.
755  
756  "But I didn't. I got hold, but, Gad, I came near going overboard!"
757  
758  He stopped to laugh, this time fearlessly aloud, while Jerry chuckled.
759  
760  "I lay flat along the bulwark," Jack went on, "by the main rigging. The
761  skylight-covers were on, of course, but the frames were half up, and I
762  could get scraps of the talk in the cabin. The men Uncle Randolph's got
763  along with him are old Melford and Tom Bardale. I thought I'd die to
764  hear them go on. Old Melford was grumbling away,--he's always an awful
765  croaker, you know. He piped up once, and said it was just his luck to
766  have to suffer both fog and bridge when he came for solid cruising.
767  Uncle Randolph and Bardale both poo-poohed him, and asked him if he'd
768  rather play slap-Jack. The old boys are going to play bridge
769  somewhere,--I didn't find out where, but it doesn't matter; they're
770  settled, anyway. I didn't hear anything else, for I'd hardly time to
771  drop into the pea-pod and get out of the way of the men from the
772  fo'c'sle that came out to haul in the cutter on the boat-boom. I rushed
773  ashore as tight as I could pelt, and you saw the rest. This dance
774  business, too! Luck's with us!"
775  
776  He stopped, all but breathless. With one accord the pair started for the
777  stairs, and took their way to the pier, where the lantern made a dim and
778  watery illumination in the midst of the fog. Castleport seized Jerry by
779  the arm and led him to the edge of the pier.
780  
781  "With this wind," he said with great earnestness, "we'd best run out to
782  the westward, and beat along the south of Vinal Haven. We'll have more
783  sea-room, and with the weather as thick as this, I don't deny that even
784  that's risky enough."
785  
786  "It is a nasty night," Taberman assented with emphasis. "Are you for
787  going outside Wooden Ball Island?"
788  
789  "Tell that when we've got by Dogfish and the rest of 'em," replied Jack
790  briefly. "I mean to leave that to Dave, anyhow."
791  
792  "You're dead sure you want to do it, old man?" queried Tab with the air
793  of one who would not have asked the question had he not been confident
794  that the answer would be in the affirmative.
795  
796  "I'd do it ten times over just for the lark!" snorted Jack. "Now
797  then--business!"
798  
799  They descended the ladder to the eastern float, and Castleport called
800  out guardedly to the men who had all this time been lying concealed in
801  the market-boat under the wharf. A slight bumping, a muttered oath, the
802  rattle of an oar on the thwart, and then the nose of the boat emerged
803  from beneath the pier. A vigorous thrust with the boat-hook against one
804  of the outer stringers shot her up alongside the float.
805  
806  "All right?" inquired Jack.
807  
808  A stoutly built man of short stature standing in the bow of the boat
809  answered.
810  
811  "Right enough, sir; but a mite holler."
812  
813  "Well, Dave, we'll fix that in a spell," said Jack. "We've got a bit to
814  do first, though. Let's have your watch, Tab."
815  
816  He pulled out his own as he spoke, and took Jerry's with it in one hand.
817  Then with the other hand he struck a match, which he craftily sheltered
818  from the wind.
819  
820  "You're a minute fast of me, Jerry," he commented, throwing away the
821  match and returning the watch. "I say eight seventeen, and you say eight
822  eighteen. You and Jim take the market-boat and go over to the other
823  float. Take the Merle's cutter and tow her out to one of the moorings
824  off the club here. At eight forty-eight sharp,--just half an hour,--you
825  hail the Merle. Sing out like the deuce, and tell 'em to send a boat
826  ashore. I'll see that they send one, and that when they've left there'll
827  be nobody aboard but me. In about fifteen minutes from now a boat'll
828  come ashore, but you needn't mind her. Dave'll look out for that
829  business. Just you pick out some mooring a bit to windward of the direct
830  line between the yacht and the Casino, so they shan't spot you. When you
831  hear a boat coming in answer to your hail, you come out yourselves, and
832  tow the cutter. That you're to make fast astern the Merle. Got it all
833  clear?"
834  
835  "I guess so," Jerry answered. "I don't notice a boat till eight
836  forty-eight; then I hail, and when I hear a boat coming in answer I cut
837  out to the Merle. Give me some matches to see the time with. Well, good
838  luck, old man; be sharp, or you'll dish the whole game."
839  
840  With this parting caution Taberman stepped into the market-boat, while
841  Dave got out. Oars were not needed, but Jerry and the sailor easily
842  pulled the market-boat around by the spiles to the other float, where
843  they lay concealed in the rolling fog.
844  
845  "Now then, Dave," Jack said as they disappeared, "you and I are the ones
846  that are going to open this ball. You take me out, set me aboard just as
847  if you did that sort of thing regularly,--do you see? As if I'd paid you
848  a quarter for setting me aboard, you know. Then you row back. Here's a
849  boat that'll do," he broke off, pointing to a small whitehall boat made
850  fast to the staging. "Get in, and pull me out."
851  
852  The pair stepped into the little craft, and when Dave began rowing Jack
853  continued his instructions.
854  
855  "When you get back to the float," he said, "you just make this boat
856  fast, and hide under the shadow of those stairs on the outside of the
857  Casino--you know?"
858  
859  "Yes, sir."
860  
861  "Wait for a boat from the yacht with three or four men in it.--Pull on
862  your port oar a bit; that's good.--When they get ashore and go up the
863  wharf, you take their tender and rush her out to a mooring same as Mr.
864  Taberman's done. Do you see?"
865  
866  "Guess so, sir," was Dave's response. "Do you want me to catch the same
867  one?"
868  
869  "Any one'll do, provided it won't be seen by a boat pulling ashore from
870  the Merle. You won't have to go far to hide in this fog.--Little
871  stronger on your port oar again; tide's cutting you down.--When you hear
872  Mr. Taberman hailing, you stand by, and as soon as a boat goes by in
873  answer, you pull out to the yacht and make fast astern. Give her plenty
874  of painter; all she's got. Do you see now?"
875  
876  "I guess I do, sir. You're going to have a boat on every davit that way,
877  ain't you, sir?"
878  
879  "If it works," Jack answered in a low voice, for they were now under the
880  yacht's port quarter.
881  
882  Dave pulled around in silence to the steps on the starboard side.
883  
884  "Here we are, sir," he said in an even tone as he caught at the ladder
885  grating.
886  
887  The Merle, dimly visible by the foggy glow of her riding-light, was
888  pitching slightly in the chop, and the small dinghy bobbed up and down
889  beside her like a cork beside a floating spar. The waves slapped against
890  the yacht's sheer, wetting her top-sides with spray and poppling away
891  merrily under her counter. In the thick dimness her masts loomed up
892  almost supernaturally tall.
893  
894  "Hello aboard the Merle," shouted Castleport.
895  
896  "Hello?" answered a voice from forward, and in a moment a tall, burly
897  figure appeared on deck by the ladder.
898  
899  "What is it?" asked the tall man. "What d' you want?"
900  
901  "Hello, Camper," cried Jack, recognizing the voice as that of his
902  uncle's sailing-master. "Hello, Camper, don't you know me?"
903  
904  He sprang up the steps and gained the deck.
905  
906  "Why, Mr. Castleport," the skipper cried in a hearty tone, "whatever are
907  you doin' here? Thought you was over to the Island. How are you, sir?"
908  
909  "Cold," Jack answered with a laugh. "How's yourself? Fit as usual, I
910  suppose. President aboard?"
911  
912  "No, sir. He's gone ashore to some sort of a gatherin'. I never thought
913  to see you here, sir."
914  
915  "Oh, I came over to join the yacht here. I got tired of waiting. I
916  shan't want you any longer," he called down to the figure in the dinghy
917  below. "Much obliged."
918  
919  The dinghy and Dave melted into the blackness of the night.
920  
921  "Come below, Mr. Castleport, sir. You'll have a bracer?" the genial
922  sailing-master asked. "Nasty night, ain't it?"
923  
924  "It is that," Jack agreed, "but I'm in hopes there'll be a change soon."
925  
926  And smiling at the thought how truly the words expressed his secret
927  intent, he followed the worthy Camper below.
928  
929  [Illustration: Decoration]
930  
931  
932  
933  
934  Chapter Three
935  
936  IT BLOWS SOUTHEAST
937  
938  
939  The saloon of the Merle was a spacious cabin, paneled in Cuban cedar.
940  Along both sides ran transoms cushioned in dark green corduroy, which
941  contrasted pleasantly with the red of the woodwork. On either side of
942  the companion-way were big closets, the doors of which, framing large
943  mirrors, opened forward against the after ends of the transoms. Both to
944  port and to starboard the cabin was lined with lockers for flags,
945  charts, and bottles, except where the recessed bookcases came in the
946  middle. Large nickeled Argand lamps to port and starboard on the for'ard
947  bulkhead illuminated the interior. Sheathed in cedar, the butt of the
948  schooner's mainmast stood in the fore part of the saloon; and aft from
949  it ran a mahogany table around which were placed some
950  comfortable-looking chairs. All in all, the impression of power and
951  grace which one received from regarding the outside of the Merle was
952  equaled by the feeling of comfort, and, indeed, almost of luxury, one
953  had upon viewing her below decks.
954  
955  It was in this pleasant retreat that Jack had settled himself in less
956  than a minute after his arrival on the yacht. The good skipper, who had
957  kept an almost fatherly eye on the youth ever since he was old enough to
958  "fist a rope," sat uneasily on the edge of the divan on the port side.
959  Jack, sprawled out on the opposite transom, lit a cigarette, and looked
960  up at the skylight.
961  
962  "My aunt! But I'm glad to be aboard again," he declared. "How is
963  everything? What sort of a run down did you have?"
964  
965  "Pretty fair, sir," returned the master. "We went to Marblehead, and
966  then to Portsmouth. Mr. Drake, he spent the time in seeing his friends.
967  Then we run to Portland, and then to Boothbay. We run in here yesterday.
968  Nothin' much to tell of on the cruise."
969  
970  "You've made schedule time," Jack commented. "You are here just when you
971  were due."
972  
973  "Yes, we got here," Camper assented, "though 't one time, when I see the
974  stores that had to come aboard, I doubted if we should get started for a
975  week."
976  
977  "More stores than usual?" queried Jack, with a little spark of interest
978  in his eye.
979  
980  "Well, Mr. Drake, he 'lowed that last year when we got becalmed down
981  the coast some of the provisions fell short, and he vowed he'd never get
982  caught in that shape again; so this time he's stocked up fit to do the
983  Nor'west Passage. He's got every kind of a thing to eat that man ever
984  put into tins, you may bet your life."
985  
986  "Trust him to have an eye to the galley," laughed Jack, reflecting how
987  satisfactory a complement to the plain provisions waiting at the Island
988  would be this extensive assortment of choice eatables. "Well, I'm for
989  sleeping aboard. Can you give me a lift with my luggage?"
990  
991  Everything he had said since he came on board had been preliminary to
992  this. His one chance of getting the sailing-master to a safe distance
993  lay in inducing Camper to go ashore on an errand. To this question the
994  skipper replied, Yankee fashion, with another.
995  
996  "Where is it, sir?"
997  
998  "Go to Mullin's and tell 'em you're from me;--you'd better do it
999  yourself, Camper;--and get them to give you a steamer-trunk and two
1000  bags. Do you know the place? It's the only boarding-house there is in
1001  the village. Anybody can tell you."
1002  
1003  "I know it, sir. 'Bout a cable's length up the road."
1004  
1005  "Yes; that's it. I don't think you'll find the trunk heavy," Jack went
1006  on, with a secret inclination to speak very fast and a consciousness
1007  that he must appear cool and deliberate. "Of course you'll take a couple
1008  of men to tote it, but I don't like to send an ordinary seaman up
1009  there."
1010  
1011  He wondered what he should reply if asked why not; but Camper, who had
1012  long been trained under President Drake to habits of unquestioning
1013  obedience, replied with perfect simplicity:--
1014  
1015  "All right, sir, I'll have it aboard in half an hour. Your old
1016  stateroom's all ready, I believe. You just ring for the steward if you
1017  want anything, sir."
1018  
1019  "Thanks," responded Jack, taking a book from its place as he spoke, as
1020  if with the intention of settling himself to read.
1021  
1022  Camper withdrew, and Jack listened eagerly till he heard footsteps on
1023  the deck, the rattle of the davit-tackle, the splash of the boat
1024  alongside, and then the rhythm of receding oars. The moment he was sure
1025  of not being seen by the skipper he closed his book with a bang, flung
1026  it on the table, looked at his watch, and went hurriedly on deck. In the
1027  lee of the mainmast he paused to light a fresh cigarette, and then began
1028  untying the cover of the mainsail, loosening the points and pulling
1029  them through the grommets. As he worked his way aft, he suddenly
1030  thought he heard the sound of oars. He stopped to make sure: there could
1031  be no doubt of it; some one was pulling toward the Merle. In a flash
1032  Jack saw his scheme ruined in any one of a thousand ways. He set his
1033  teeth and ran over rapidly in his head the possibilities, but without
1034  reaching any satisfactory conclusion. Then he walked aft, and putting
1035  his hands on the rail, bent over the yacht's port quarter and peered
1036  into the fog. With a feeling of relief he realized from the sound and
1037  time of the strokes that the approaching boat was a small one, and was
1038  pulled by one pair of oars only. He had hardly decided this when he
1039  discerned the cause of his alarm, and almost laughed to see nothing more
1040  formidable than a small pea-pod, pulled by a boy. The rower came
1041  alongside and rested on his oars, while Jack watched him curiously.
1042  
1043  "Is that Mr. Drake's vessel?" inquired the boy.
1044  
1045  "Yes," Jack returned. "What's wanted?"
1046  
1047  "The postmaster said 'f I'd bring ye these letters ye'd give me a
1048  quarter," replied the youthful oarsman.
1049  
1050  "Mr. Drake isn't aboard now," said Jack.
1051  
1052  "Well, ye c'n give me my quarter jes' the same," the boy rejoined. "I'll
1053  let ye hev the letters, 'n' he'll make it right with ye later. He lef'
1054  word this evenin' for his mail to be brung him every time it come, an'
1055  't was that foggy the Sylvy got in late from Rocklan', 'n' I couldn't
1056  get roun' to bring it out before. 'Twan't sorted till after Mr. Staples
1057  hed his supper."
1058  
1059  "All right," Jack said hastily. "Come alongside."
1060  
1061  He feared to create suspicion, and felt that the only thing to do at the
1062  moment was to get rid of the boy. He gave the youth a quarter, and took
1063  the letters in exchange, mentally saying to himself that he hoped they
1064  were not of importance. The boy went pulling away as if in most unusual
1065  elation, and Castleport, thrusting the letters into the breast pocket of
1066  his coat, returned to his work. He had not quite finished untying the
1067  points when he heard Jerry's hail from the mooring.
1068  
1069  "Merle, ahoy! Ho-ro aboard the Merle!" came booming through the fog in
1070  Taberman's most stentorian tones.
1071  
1072  Jack placed himself in the companion-way as if just emerging from the
1073  cabin, and waited for another hail.
1074  
1075  "Merle ahoy! Aho-o-o-y aboard the Merle!" again rang through the thick
1076  night above the sound of the wind, the water, and the cordage.
1077  
1078  "Hallo-o-o!" bawled back Castleport.
1079  
1080  "Send ... boat ... ashore!" came the voice.
1081  
1082  Jerry was apparently able to outroar all the bulls of Bashan, and was
1083  doing his worst.
1084  
1085  "Aye--oh!" Jack yelled in reply, and walked quickly forward.
1086  
1087  The steward had heard the rumpus, and was standing in the forecastle
1088  companion. Capless, and wearing his white jacket, he gaped about like a
1089  quizzical seal.
1090  
1091  "Some one hailing from the shore," said Jack shortly; "want a boat.
1092  Don't know what you'll take unless you go in the longboat. Tell the
1093  men."
1094  
1095  "Beg pardon, sir; there's only me and the cook and two hands aboard.
1096  It'll take us all to pull the longboat."
1097  
1098  The steward had a slow, exasperating whine which always irritated Jack.
1099  
1100  "Then you'll have to take an oar," Jack responded roughly. "There's some
1101  one ashore waiting, and I said I'd send a boat. Get a move on. I'll
1102  watch ship."
1103  
1104  The steward went below grumbling, but soon reappeared with the cook and
1105  the two hands. With some delay they got off in the longboat, pulling
1106  wretchedly toward the shore and nagging at each other. As he stepped to
1107  the foot of the mainmast to take the halyards off the pins, Jack
1108  fervently thanked his stars for the heaviness of the boat and the
1109  evident fact that both cook and steward were hopeless duffers with an
1110  oar. He cleared the halyards with nervous fingers, stripped off the
1111  cover of the mainsail, and undid the canvas stops with which it was
1112  furled. Then he turned to the headsails, and had all clear before his
1113  ear again caught the sound of oars. He ran aft, and called out
1114  guardedly. Dave's voice answered him, and then he heard Taberman urging
1115  his companion to quicken his stroke. In the mist Castleport could dimly
1116  distinguish the heavy boats slowly nearing the yacht. It was all the men
1117  could do to get them alongside and make them fast astern. Once this was
1118  accomplished, all hands turned eagerly to the still harder labor of
1119  getting the Merle under weigh.
1120  
1121  "Jim," ordered Castleport, "skip along for'ard and take down that
1122  riding-light. Set it on deck so it won't show out-board. Dave, you get
1123  up the boat-boom. Haul it right up, 'thout minding the guys! Lively,
1124  now!"
1125  
1126  As Dave and Jim hurried forward to execute these orders, Jack himself
1127  stepped aft, took off the binnacle-cover, and got the lamps lit and in
1128  their places.
1129  
1130  "All hands for'ard on the anchor!" he sang out, rapping his shins on
1131  the cockpit combings as he scrambled out and ran along the deck. "We'll
1132  make sail when we get out the mudhook. 'F we try to get her mains'l up,
1133  they'll hear us all over the place. We'll drop down under heads'ls.
1134  Catch ahold there!"
1135  
1136  The Merle was riding at her port bower in some six fathoms of water. She
1137  had out a good bit of scope, however, and between the eight hands which
1138  gripped the quarter-inch chain and the anchor to which it was bent were
1139  some ten fathoms to be "handed over." In the light of the big Fresnel
1140  anchor-lantern upon the deck, the men, silent, rigid, braced back,
1141  strained steadily. For a full half-minute there was no gain whatever,
1142  but then one link of the chain came to the brazen lip of the hawse-hole
1143  with a sharp rap. The men grunted and hissed, bringing every muscle into
1144  play. Taberman was foremost on the chain. He faced the hawse-hole
1145  squarely, his legs wide apart, and his head thrown back. His face, even
1146  as seen by the white light of the Fresnel, was a dark brick-red, and out
1147  of the left corner of his mouth his tongue protruded. Dave was behind
1148  him, his left knee bent, and his right leg straight from toe to hip. He
1149  hung on savagely, his face unnaturally blank; his hair, damp with fog
1150  and sweat, clung to his brown forehead and temples. The third man was
1151  Jim, lying back in a strange posture, as though the small of his back
1152  were invisibly supported. His cheeks were white; his breathing was
1153  inaudible.
1154  
1155  With a little salvo of metallic snaps a scant dozen links more came in.
1156  Jack was last on the chain, and was separated from the man next him by a
1157  space greater than that between any other pair, so that he could when
1158  necessary take a turn of the slack about one of the brass-capped
1159  bollards at his side. His body was tense and rigid, his face and
1160  forehead full of odd puckers and lines. He was white at the lips, and
1161  the corners of his mouth were drawn down. His nose moved nervously with
1162  almost the suggestion of a rabbit's. One more link came in.
1163  
1164  "Better take it on the winch," gasped Jerry.
1165  
1166  "Damn it,--pull!" cried Jack.
1167  
1168  Jim grunted and Dave drew a breath through his closed teeth with a sharp
1169  whistling sound. Suddenly the chain rattled in so quickly that they
1170  could almost over-hand it. The Merle was moving at last.
1171  
1172  "Smartly!" Jack cried. "Smartly, and we'll make her trip it out
1173  herself."
1174  
1175  The four hauled lustily.
1176  
1177  "Nigh up and down," called Jerry.
1178  
1179  Jack threw a couple of bights of the chain over the bollard, and held
1180  it. The big yacht forged ahead slowly into the eye of the wind, carried
1181  along by the impetus given her by the handing of the chain. The bits
1182  creaked a little, the chain grew very taut and vibrant. The Merle
1183  checked up and began to drift back.
1184  
1185  "Now then!" cried Jack. "Lay along!"
1186  
1187  Each one of them grasped the chain with a fierce vigor, as a man might
1188  seize the throat of his enemy, while Jerry burst into an explosive
1189  whaling chantey, and the men fell into time with its rhythm.
1190  
1191  
1192       "Haul the bowline, the bowline, the bowline;
1193       Haul the bowline, the bowline,--_Haul!_"
1194  
1195  
1196  "Here she comes!" he shouted in the midst of a stave, as, all at once,
1197  the anchor was broken out.
1198  
1199  Jack dropped his end of the chain and ran aft to mind the wheel, leaving
1200  the men to take in the rest of the slack. The headsails were up in
1201  stops, but before breaking them out it was necessary to lay the yacht
1202  round on the port tack. As she was under sternway, Jack whirled the
1203  spokes over to port, and so--for her steering-gear was
1204  "balanced"--brought her head around to the southward. When he felt the
1205  wind on his left cheek, he put his hand to his mouth and shouted.
1206  
1207  "Break out fore-staysail!" he bellowed. "Trim it a-weather!--Hang on to
1208  the weather-sheet till she falls well off!"
1209  
1210  With a great slatting and booming of canvas the schooner payed off
1211  rapidly.
1212  
1213  "Catch on to that port sheet there!" shouted Jack. "Port, I say, port!
1214  Make fast! Not too flat! Give her all she'll use!"
1215  
1216  The Merle was now moving slowly before the wind.
1217  
1218  "Break out the jibs," ordered Jack, "both jibs! That's good. Make fast!"
1219  
1220  The wind had so freshened that the yacht began to move in earnest. At
1221  this juncture voices, faint but frantic, were heard hailing from astern.
1222  
1223  "Merle ahoy! Ahoy-oy-oy! Show--light! A-hoy-oy-oy--'board the Merle!"
1224  
1225  "Hear the steward?" called Jack to Jerry, who was at work with the
1226  head-sheet cleats.
1227  
1228  "Hear him!" laughed Jerry. "His music's a merry send-off."
1229  
1230  "Ahoy-oy-oy!" came the voice again, fainter and full of a dismayed
1231  distress that made them both break out afresh into derisive laughter.
1232  "Ahoy! Anchor! An-chor--Anch"--
1233  
1234  The despairing wail died away on the freshening wind.
1235  
1236  "Hope they won't poke round in the fog all night looking for the Merle,"
1237  Jack said gayly. "I never did like that steward, though."
1238  
1239  A moment or two later, as the yacht was nearing the entrance of the
1240  Thoroughfare, Jack called for Dave. The man came aft.
1241  
1242  "See here, Dave," Castleport asked, suddenly grown grave; "we've got
1243  more weather than we counted on. Can you pilot this yacht round Vinal
1244  Haven in this fog?"
1245  
1246  "Reck'n I kin, sir," Dave replied with pleasing assurance. "Man and boy
1247  I've worked round these shores twelve years."
1248  
1249  "Very well, then,--come down here and take her. Her gear's balanced: put
1250  the wheel over same way you want to swing her head. She's quick as a
1251  flash. If you want the chart"--
1252  
1253  But Dave shook his head with a grin.
1254  
1255  "Well, anyhow," said Jack, turning to leave him, "there's your compass."
1256  
1257  "That don't bother me none," replied the intrepid Dave, with a glance at
1258  once scornful and defiant at the smart binnacle. "I go mos' gin'rally by
1259  the smell," he added by way of explanation.
1260  
1261  "All right," laughed Jack. "Handle her carefully."
1262  
1263  "One thing, sir,--how much does she draw?"
1264  
1265  "Twelve feet," returned Jack.
1266  
1267  Then he stepped up on to the deck, and the Merle sped on into the black
1268  night.
1269  
1270  [Illustration: Decoration]
1271  
1272  
1273  
1274  
1275  Chapter Four
1276  
1277  IT BLOWS NORTHWEST
1278  
1279  
1280  With Dave as her Palinurus the Merle ran down the wind until she was
1281  well outside the western entrance to the Thoroughfare. The headsails
1282  were then dropped, the yacht was put into the wind, and the mainsail was
1283  hoisted. The foresail was left furled, as the wind had freshened
1284  considerably, and the schooner started on a southerly course on the port
1285  tack.
1286  
1287  How Dave knew where he was or by what subtle instinct he was moved to
1288  give the Merle now a spoke or two to starboard or again to port, were
1289  mysteries as insoluble as complex. Taberman was lost in wonder at Dave's
1290  cool assurance; but to Jack, who knew of old the marvelous way in which
1291  the local fishermen handle their craft in the fog, the helmsman's skill,
1292  if wonderful, was yet no new thing.
1293  
1294  The beat to the Island was not, however, without incident. Twice, as
1295  they were tacking about in the thick fog, they ran close to wicked
1296  ledges over which the slow seas just rolled without breaking. At another
1297  point they came about just in time to avoid going ashore against a
1298  precipitous cliff which loomed high in the mist. Near the end of the run
1299  they worked into some shoal water where the uneasy heave and thrust of
1300  the sea made the schooner reel and stagger madly, while all about them
1301  was the thunder of unseen breakers. But in each and every peril Dave
1302  kept his head completely and brought the Merle through in safety.
1303  
1304  The passage was a busy one. Three times they luffed up in open water,
1305  and each time took a boat aboard. It was a difficult--almost a
1306  perilous--operation, but the night was flying and the boats dragged
1307  heavily. The foresail was made ready for hoisting, a reef being tucked
1308  into it without its being raised. The port bower was taken aboard;
1309  lanterns were got ready against the work which was to be done at the
1310  Island; a careful survey was made of the places available for stowage.
1311  Jack and Taberman made a list of the men, assigned watches and berths.
1312  They agreed that Gonzague, as cook, steward, and general major-domo,
1313  should have to himself the little cabin formerly occupied by the
1314  steward. To the men they gave the berths of the old crew; and in
1315  general arranged everything for the ocean voyage which had been left
1316  for adjustment until they should be actually on board. The personal
1317  effects of the President, his guests, the officers and the crew, they
1318  made ready to leave at the Island.
1319  
1320  "How about clothes for the men?" Taberman asked. "I never thought of
1321  that; and we should look like the deuce with a crew in fishermen's rigs.
1322  The police of any harbor in the world would be after us."
1323  
1324  "The uniforms belong to the yacht," Jack answered. "They are cut for the
1325  crew, but the men never own them."
1326  
1327  "Do you suppose those poor devils' traps will be safe at the Island?"
1328  
1329  "Safe as in a church."
1330  
1331  "But how'll they get 'em?"
1332  
1333  "Oh, by nine o'clock to-morrow morning the President will be on his way
1334  to the Island if he has to buy the Sylvia to go on. Camper'll tell him I
1335  ran away with the Merle, and he'll start to the Island to find me or get
1336  track."
1337  
1338  So they talked until, about two in the morning, the yacht ran past
1339  Hardwood Island, hauled her wind, and worked along to the southeast.
1340  Suddenly through the fog a dull red gleam showed on the weather bow.
1341  
1342  "There's Gonzague's bonfire," Jack cried. "You've brought us through,
1343  Dave, about as slick as anything ever was done in this world. 'Twas a
1344  tough job, too."
1345  
1346  The main-peak was dropped to lessen the yacht's way, and as the red
1347  flare became more distinct, the outer jibs were doused. Keeping the
1348  shore close aboard on the port side, the Merle ran along toward the
1349  ruddy blur of the fire, which was now seen to be burning at the end of a
1350  point. As the boat neared this point, Jack seized the megaphone, and
1351  putting the big cone to his lips, faced the fire, which was now abeam.
1352  
1353  "Hallo!" he roared. "Hallo, there! Gonzague!"
1354  
1355  A sudden and confused shouting out of the fog answered him. Then black
1356  figures, silhouetted against the red brightness of the fire and waving
1357  burning brands, ran to and fro with odd antics and caperings.
1358  
1359  "'Bout ship!" cried Dave. "'Ware boom! Douse the heads'ls!"
1360  
1361  The Merle came over on the other tack, and the staysail and jibs were
1362  run down. The main-sheet was then so started as to spill the wind out of
1363  the sail, and the yacht's way was quickly lessened. Having rounded the
1364  point, the schooner moved ahead sluggishly, again passing the bonfire
1365  on the port hand.
1366  
1367  "Stand by the anchor!" sang out Dave, as they ran by the end of the
1368  jetty.
1369  
1370  "Hooray!" yelled a chorus of voices from the pier. "Hooray, Dave!"
1371  
1372  Dave twirled the wheel to starboard, and the Merle came slowly into the
1373  eye of the wind, where he kept her until she seemed to be making
1374  sternway.
1375  
1376  "Well enough!" he shouted. "Let her go!"
1377  
1378  And the anchor-chain rattled down in three and a half fathoms.
1379  
1380  It was after two o'clock, and still thick. The wind, however, was
1381  hauling around to the southward, and the fog was beginning to thin a
1382  little. The main-sheet had hardly been hauled aft when some of the men
1383  were alongside in a boat. Jack stood by the steps, which had not been
1384  taken aboard during the run, while Tab, standing by his side, held a
1385  lantern. The first man aboard was Gonzague. Agile as an ape, for all his
1386  years, the old Provençal ran up the steps and touched his cap smartly,
1387  man-o'-war fashion.
1388  
1389  "I see you leaf in a great hoory, cap'n," he chuckled to Jack. "You 'av'
1390  loosed de matting of de step-grating, eh?"
1391  
1392  "Yes, rather," laughed Jack. "Pile aboard there," he added, addressing
1393  the men in the two boats now alongside.
1394  
1395  The new crew made their boats fast to the grating and came on board.
1396  
1397  "Now, then, all hands aft here for a minute," Jack ordered, when every
1398  one was assembled on deck.
1399  
1400  He knew that with such men as he had been able to collect for this
1401  expedition it was essential to bind them in some way. He had therefore
1402  prepared a paper in which were five articles for them to sign, and he
1403  was firmly resolved that unless they agreed to bind themselves, he would
1404  not trust the President's schooner to their care. The men were resolute
1405  in the face of danger, yet were unused to discipline; they were imbued
1406  with a crude sense of loyalty, but were unruly and quick to take
1407  offense; and unless they should consent at the outset to submit to his
1408  authority, Jack knew that little dependence could be put upon them.
1409  
1410  He instinctively assumed an arbitrary air,--almost dropping half
1411  consciously into the latent bully which lies hid in all strong
1412  characters. Had he reasoned it out, he would have adopted much the same
1413  tone as that which he took by instinct. These men, wild followers of
1414  the sea, would scorn to be led, and were to be mastered only by one who
1415  could browbeat and domineer,--who could, in their own word, "man-handle"
1416  them. They responded to the primitive necessity of seeing force in the
1417  man who is to command; and in showing his determination at the outset
1418  Jack was displaying at least one characteristic of a proper leader of
1419  men.
1420  
1421  He took from his pocket the list of names, and telling the men to answer
1422  to the roll he read it off by the light of Tab's lantern.
1423  
1424  "Elihu Coombs?" he read.
1425  
1426  "Here," answered a thickset lad with a rugged and weather-beaten face.
1427  
1428  "Here, SIR!" said Jack sharply, as he check'd off the name.
1429  
1430  "Edward Turner?"
1431  
1432  "Here, sir," answered a quiet voice on the outer ring of the men.
1433  
1434  "Haskell Dwight?"
1435  
1436  "Here, sir."
1437  
1438  They were all aboard: ten men, exclusive of Jack, Jerry, and Gonzague.
1439  When he had finished the list, Jack handed it to Jerry, and taking from
1440  his pocket a second paper,--the simple articles he had written,--he
1441  knocked the creases out of it with a back-handed rap, and then made a
1442  short speech.
1443  
1444  "My men," he began, "I don't want to haul you into any game with your
1445  eyes shut, so I've drafted articles for you to sign. Of course this
1446  whole business is only a joke, but it's got a serious side to it too.
1447  You can all see that plain enough; and it's my interest--and yours--to
1448  see to it that we don't have to laugh out of the wrong side of our
1449  mouths.
1450  
1451  "If you come on this cruise you'll sweat for your wages, now let me tell
1452  you! I'm not for grinding any man,--most of you know what I am, for
1453  you've seen me growing up from a kid,--but the yacht's got to be kept
1454  up, and that means that every man-jack aboard has got to keep as neat as
1455  a pin and not slight his job.
1456  
1457  "On the other hand, you men'll get a lot of experience in handling a
1458  larger vessel than you've been used to; you'll have good grub; and
1459  you'll see foreign ports. Top o' that, you draw good pay, and keep what
1460  clothes you can save.
1461  
1462  "Now then, these are the articles that every man who sails with me has
1463  got to put his name to."
1464  
1465  He read the whole paper, as distinctly and as impressively as he could.
1466  
1467  "Now," he concluded, "if any man here lacks the heart for this
1468  business, let him clear out. The rest of you, step up and sign."
1469  
1470  Jack laid the paper on the companion-hatch, and produced a fountain-pen,
1471  which he put beside it. Jerry was the first, in virtue of his position
1472  as mate, to put down his name. He set down his lantern and scrawled his
1473  signature at the foot of the articles in a hand that would have dwarfed
1474  that of John Hancock. He passed the pen to Gonzague, who, laboriously
1475  fisting it, wrote his name in a small, cramped hand, absurdly unlike the
1476  characters above it.
1477  
1478  For an instant--an appreciable instant--the rest hung back. Jack's brown
1479  eyes challenged theirs, and every one was very silent. That Castleport
1480  was seconded by those who were obviously attached to him gave the men,
1481  rather than confidence, an uneasy feeling of being another party, and
1482  this prompted an instinctive caution almost like antagonism. Had things
1483  been allowed to rest for a moment, the day might easily have been lost.
1484  Discussion might have arisen to beget argument and discord, explanations
1485  have been demanded, and the men have asked to be satisfied as to the
1486  real grounds on which Castleport was to be justified in appropriating
1487  his uncle's yacht and making off with it, a question which could hardly
1488  have been answered so as to satisfy everybody. At this unrealized
1489  crisis, old Gonzague quietly stepped among the men, passed a jest with
1490  one of them in an undertone, and so equilibrium was restored. He at once
1491  became one of them, and the vague idea of parties and opposition
1492  vanished into thin air before the men had had time even to recognize it.
1493  Dave stepped forward and signed, Jim followed him, and the rest of the
1494  men came after. Jack had sounded all of them separately before unfolding
1495  his plans, and the result was that not one of them drew back now. As the
1496  last one laid down the pen, Castleport spoke.
1497  
1498  "Before we fall to work I don't think anybody'd mind a good glass of
1499  grog; and while Gonzague's getting it, I just want to add one word to my
1500  say. I know this gentleman, Mr. Jerrold Taberman, to be a good
1501  navigator, and I've chosen him as my mate. Gonzague'll be cook and
1502  steward, and A1 you'll find him. I'm bound to make things go as easy as
1503  may be, and I will. I'm sure you'll do your duties, and you may bank on
1504  my doing mine."
1505  
1506  The grog being brought, Tab proposed the captain's health, and the crew
1507  drank it with enthusiasm. Jack emptied his glass to the "crew and a good
1508  cruise;" and then the entire company went to work, loading and stowing.
1509  
1510  Under Jerry's orders part of the crew began to carry provisions from
1511  the boathouse to the yacht, while under Jack's surveillance Gonzague and
1512  two of the crew stored what the others brought out. Gun-tackle purchases
1513  were rigged by the foremast to take the heavier cases aboard. The men
1514  worked feverishly, and almost without sound, as if subdued by the fear
1515  of being heard. At the end of a couple of hours the Merle had only to
1516  fill her water-tanks and she would be ready for sea. The fog was by this
1517  time so thin that in the dim light of the yet unrisen sun Jack, as he
1518  stood in the rigging, could discern vaguely the form of the house on the
1519  Island. As he was considering the weather, Gonzague, his face red with
1520  exertion and his usually immaculate clothes stained and torn, came up
1521  hastily.
1522  
1523  "Mistair Castleport, sair," he said, "I don' fin' any beeg funnel for de
1524  watter-tank. Dey mus' always feel dem from de watter-boat 'ose,--stick
1525  de en' into de deck-plate, I t'ink."
1526  
1527  "How's that?" exclaimed Jack. "No funnel?"
1528  
1529  The tender containing the first installments of the water-supply had
1530  already left the jetty, and Jack fell hastily to considering how the
1531  water was to be got out of the big unheaded casks into the tanks
1532  without its being dribbled in by the dipperful.
1533  
1534  "Did you look everywhere?" he demanded.
1535  
1536  "I look in de peak and go all de way aft to de run," replied the
1537  steward, "and all I find was de funnel in de kerosene-barrel. It ees too
1538  small, and it do fair reek wid de pairfume of de oil, sair."
1539  
1540  "Is there any piping aboard? any hose?" Jack asked. "We might siphon
1541  it."
1542  
1543  Gonzague shook his head, and at that moment the boat laden with water
1544  came alongside. Jack leaned over the rail.
1545  
1546  "I say, Jerry," he called out, "there's no funnel to fill the tanks
1547  with. How the deuce can we make water-stowage?"
1548  
1549  "Search me," returned Jerry with cheerful inelegance. "How should I
1550  know? Might use the megaphone."
1551  
1552  "You're a genius!" roared Jack. "It'll do to a T!"
1553  
1554  The keys were found, the caps unscrewed from the deck-plates, and the
1555  large papier-maché cone of the megaphone was set big-end-up over the
1556  orifice. Two men held it by the rim, while others kept it brimming with
1557  buckets of water bailed out of the casks. At the end of another hour
1558  both tanks were filled and the caps screwed down.
1559  
1560  The Merle was ready for her long cruise. Jack was well satisfied with
1561  the sufficiency of her stores, as in addition to the plain provisions
1562  which he and Taberman had provided, the yacht had been most abundantly
1563  victualed by the President for her summer's cruising.
1564  
1565  "Think of anything we've left, Jerry?" Jack asked.
1566  
1567  "The President?" Tab suggested.
1568  
1569  Jack's official seriousness went entirely to pieces at this suggestion,
1570  but he turned to the steward with an air of business.
1571  
1572  "Have you got everything, Gonzague?"
1573  
1574  "Yes, sair. I t'ink de leest is feel," the old man responded, closely
1575  regarding the dirty paper on which he had made his inventory and checked
1576  off each article as it came on board. Each item in the list had a black
1577  scratch beside it.
1578  
1579  "Well, then," the captain said, with a spark in his eye, "we're off!"
1580  
1581  He gave the word to clear the decks and to get under weigh.
1582  
1583  The wind had come around to the west, and was blowing fresh. They made
1584  all sail, however, chancing the gusty squalls which they were likely to
1585  meet off the high land of Isle au Haut, which they meant to leave on the
1586  starboard. The fog had gone entirely, except for long ghostly wreaths
1587  clinging to the dark green gullies of the Haut or encircling the distant
1588  mountain-tops of Mt. Desert; and when the sun rose clear and fair, all
1589  auspices seemed most cheeringly propitious.
1590  
1591  Jack took his departure from the Eastern Ear of the Haut, when it bore
1592  west-northwest three miles. At four that afternoon, when he and Jerry
1593  came on deck for time-sights, no land was to be seen.
1594  
1595  [Illustration: Decoration]
1596  
1597  
1598  
1599  
1600  Chapter Five
1601  
1602  LAND HO!
1603  
1604  
1605  Some three weeks after the morning when the Merle left the Island, Jack
1606  and Tab were sitting in the saloon, working out the sights they had just
1607  taken for longitude. It was shortly after eight o'clock in the morning;
1608  the air was warm, and had in it a suggestion of the south. Through the
1609  open skylight came a shaft of light which cast a brilliant patch on the
1610  green cushions on the port side of the cabin. As the yacht rolled or
1611  pitched easily over the long seas, the patch of light moved about,--up,
1612  down, fore, aft; now it glanced on the rich red sheathing, now on the
1613  transom, and again on the big table.
1614  
1615  On the leeward side of this table the two men, dressed in canvas
1616  trousers and blue flannel shirts, were seated with their work lying
1617  before them. Between them lay several sheets of paper, parallel-rulers,
1618  the log-book in its brown duck cover, a copy of Norie open at the
1619  tables, and the American "Ephemeris." A large sheet-chart of the North
1620  Atlantic, weighted with a pair of binoculars, was spread in front of
1621  Jack. A heavy line, full of zigzags and acute angles, and running nearly
1622  across this chart, represented the Merle's track. Presently Jack laid
1623  down the pencil with which he had been figuring, and reaching out for
1624  the "Epitome," turned to the table of functions.
1625  
1626  "Through?" asked Tab, without looking up.
1627  
1628  "'Most," returned Jack, running one finger down a column of figures as
1629  he glanced first at his paper and then at the book. "I have it now," he
1630  added, and after jotting down a number he pushed the volume over to Tab,
