1631  went to a cupboard on the port side, and brought back a case of
1632  instruments. He took out a pair of long-legged dividers, and with these
1633  and the parallel rulers he bent over the chart a minute or two, until
1634  the silence was again broken by Jerry.
1635  
1636  "What d' you get?" he asked.
1637  
1638  "Nine-eighteen-fifteen," replied Jack. "What's yours?"
1639  
1640  "Nine-sixteen-nought," answered Tab. "Wait a shake, I'll average them;"
1641  and he fell to figuring rapidly. "Mean is nine-seventeen-seven plus.
1642  Prick it off, and let's see where we're at--the D. R. latitude's
1643  thirty-six forty-eight."
1644  
1645  They bent together over the chart. Jack carefully manipulated rulers
1646  and dividers, found the point, and marked it in red ink.
1647  
1648  "She's making just over six knots now," he said. "We ought to make old
1649  Cape St. Vincent shortly. Let's put up these traps and go on deck."
1650  
1651  They stowed the things in their several lockers, and went out together.
1652  The Merle was running along with a quartering breeze, under all lower
1653  sails, sliding easily over the long swell on the port tack.
1654  
1655  "How about putting a lookout up aloft, Jack?" asked Tab. "We'll be
1656  raising the land pretty soon--if we're anywhere right in our reckoning,
1657  that is."
1658  
1659  "All right," agreed Jack. "Step down and get a pair of glasses; I fancy
1660  Hunter has the best eyes of any of the men. I'll get hold of him."
1661  
1662  Jerry disappeared below, and Jack walked along the windward side. The
1663  sea, rolling eastward in long, measured swells, reflected the sun from a
1664  myriad of glancing ripples that gleamed and glittered in the morning
1665  light. The sky, light blue and cloudless, looked like pale fire. On
1666  board the schooner the brass-work, as she rose and dipped in the troughs
1667  of the long seas, flashed and shone like burnished gold. The white
1668  canvas caught the sunshine, while on the decks, still undried from their
1669  recent scrubbing, the putty in the curving seams showed sharply white.
1670  The four boats were inboard, turned bottom up and cross-lashed to the
1671  rail.
1672  
1673  Castleport found the four men of the watch gathered in the peak, looking
1674  over the bows. He came up and saw that they were watching a school of
1675  dolphins that were keeping ahead of the yacht. The big fish seemed to
1676  vibrate. They sounded and leaped clear of the water, flashing and
1677  dripping with sparkling drops. A thousand colors rippled along their
1678  backs, as they turned and swayed, and they swung ahead like the very
1679  incarnation of frolic.
1680  
1681  The captain saw the man he wanted standing on the port side, and called
1682  him to him.
1683  
1684  "Hunter," he said, "go aft to Mr. Taberman; he'll give you a pair of
1685  glasses. Go aloft and keep a sharp lookout for land. We ought to raise
1686  it on the port bow."
1687  
1688  The effect produced by this order was electrical. The four men whipped
1689  around and stared at Jack and at each other.
1690  
1691  "Land!" exclaimed one with a foolish grin. "Land!"
1692  
1693  Hunter touched his duck hat and flew aft; Jack followed more leisurely.
1694  In a couple of minutes Hunter was ensconced in the foretop, eagerly
1695  scanning the eastern horizon. Castleport settled himself in the sun on
1696  the leeward side of the cockpit, and filled his pipe. He had hardly
1697  lighted it and taken half a dozen whiffs, when from aloft rang out the
1698  magical cry, "Land!"
1699  
1700  "Where away?" shouted the captain, leaping to his feet just as Tab
1701  appeared in the companion-way.
1702  
1703  "Have we raised it, Jack? Have we raised it?" Tab demanded excitedly.
1704  
1705  "Not yet, Tab. Just been sighted," returned Jack, peering up at the
1706  fore-crosstrees, and awaiting the lookout's answer to his hail.
1707  
1708  "'Bout two points off the weather-bow," sang out Hunter from aloft.
1709  "Just a low bank. Looks like cliffs through glasses!"
1710  
1711  "Come along, Tab!" cried Jack. "Let's go aloft and have a look at it."
1712  
1713  They made their way quickly along the deck, gained the weather-shrouds,
1714  and ran up. The watch below had turned out, just as they were,
1715  half-dressed and bareheaded. Two of the men had run out to the
1716  bowsprit's end, and holding on to the topmast stay were looking over the
1717  luff of the flying-jib. Old Gonzague, venerable as Vanderdecken, his
1718  white hair stirred by the wind,--for he was as usual without a cap,--had
1719  already gained the main-trees, where he stood shading his eyes with one
1720  hand while he gripped the shrouds with the other.
1721  
1722  "Where is it?" demanded Jerry, when he and Jack had reached the trees.
1723  
1724  "There away, sir," Hunter answered, pointing as he passed the glasses to
1725  the captain.
1726  
1727  With the unaided eye Jack and Jerry could discern, lying low on the
1728  eastern rim of the horizon, a faint brownish streak. With one arm about
1729  the topmast for support, Jack looked at the land through the glasses. At
1730  first, owing to the oscillation of the mast, he could not keep the brown
1731  streak in the field of vision, but in a moment he overcame this
1732  difficulty, and was able to make out a length of cliff of nearly uniform
1733  height, although split by numerous fjord-like bays. By its varied
1734  color--for he could see that the ribbon of shore was splashed with reds
1735  and blues--he decided that the land-fall was in the neighborhood of Cape
1736  St. Vincent.
1737  
1738  "Have a look?" he asked, passing the glasses to Tab. "It's the Painted
1739  Cape, fast enough,--or close to it."
1740  
1741  "What country is that, please, sir?" asked Hunter, in a tone almost of
1742  awe.
1743  
1744  "Portugal," the captain answered. "Sou'-western point of the land. We'll
1745  have Spain aboard before eight bells this afternoon."
1746  
1747  "By Grab, sir! Beg pardon, sir, but do them Portigee fishermen ye see to
1748  Boothbay an' Boston, do they come from hereaway?"
1749  
1750  "Here or from the islands,--Cape Verde, the Canaries, or the Azores;
1751  here for the most part. You may go below, if you want, Hunter."
1752  
1753  The man went, frequently pausing to look over his shoulder at the coast,
1754  glimpses of which could now be caught from the deck between the rolls.
1755  
1756  After a brief consultation, the captain and the mate followed Hunter,
1757  and went aft to consult the chart. As they passed along the deck, they
1758  noted that all hands were much excited. These men, used as they were to
1759  the sea, had been fishermen of the purely local sort, and it was
1760  doubtful if any one of them save Gonzague had ever before been out of
1761  sight of the high land of his native place; and here they were, in view
1762  of a strange country where the people spoke outlandish jabber, and, for
1763  all they knew to the contrary, went about in toggery as ridiculous as
1764  that of the Chinese laundrymen at Green's Landing. Discussion became
1765  all the more heated when Hunter came down and told them that the land
1766  was one of the countless possessions belonging to the "Portigee king."
1767  Frequent appeals were made to Gonzague, who had descended, and was the
1768  centre of an excited group. As Tab remarked, it was a sight worth
1769  remembering to see these self-contained New Englanders in such a state.
1770  
1771  Down below, Jack and Tab held a brief colloquy over the chart. They
1772  calculated, if the wind held, to make the Straits at nightfall, and run
1773  through by the aid of the lights on Cape Spartel and Tariffa. Having
1774  settled this point, they went on deck and had the course changed
1775  slightly.
1776  
1777  "By Jumbo!" cried Jerry, banging his fist on the deck as he stood in the
1778  cockpit, "by Jumbo, I can't sleep a wink with this land in sight.
1779  Portugal, too! By Jove, it's all very fine," he ran on, "for a _blasé_
1780  old globe-trotter like you to keep cool, but I'm fair dry with it all."
1781  
1782  Jack laughed, and reminded his friend of having lived in England and
1783  France, and of having traveled not a little in northern Europe.
1784  
1785  "Pooh!" sniffed Tab. "That's not really doing anything; everybody does
1786  that. And to think," he burst out, "that we brought ourselves! God bless
1787  me, Jacko, I little thought when you crammed me with navigation in
1788  vacation days aboard the old Luna that I'd ever use it all; really, that
1789  is, as we have used it these three weeks past."
1790  
1791  "Well, I hope you're duly grateful," laughed Jack. "It may prove a
1792  source of bread and butter if you're ever stranded."
1793  
1794  
1795  All that day the Merle ran along gallantly over the bright seas,
1796  occasionally passing ships of different nationalities bound in or out of
1797  the Straits. At sundown, although the bold coast of Morocco was not yet
1798  in sight, a lookout was sent aloft to watch for the light on Cape
1799  Spartel.
1800  
1801  At a little before nine o'clock in the evening, the breeze had so died
1802  down that the yacht hardly had steerage-way. Jack was asleep below; Tab
1803  had charge of the deck. What air there was was soft and warm. It had
1804  hauled around a couple of points against the sun, and was now fragrant
1805  with a faint tellurian odor, which would have been imperceptible to a
1806  landsman, but which was full of meaning to those who follow the sea.
1807  Overhead the great stars blazed in lustrous serenity. Their images kept
1808  appearing and vanishing on the now smooth and oily surface of the
1809  restless sea. The only sounds were those of the water and the
1810  cordage,--the sudden spanking of a big wave under the counter as the
1811  yacht flung her nose starward; the occasional crashing of the great
1812  booms and traveler-blocks as she righted suddenly after a heavy roll to
1813  port or a lurch to starboard; the pattering of the reef-points against
1814  the canvas; and the sharp reports made by the slatting of the lazy-jacks
1815  against the sails.
1816  
1817  In the west, growing smaller and smaller in the distance, the receding
1818  stern-light of an Italian steamship glimmered faintly. Taberman watched
1819  it long after it kept sinking out of sight and again rising in the
1820  weltering seas, and until it at last vanished as if quenched. He was
1821  following out certain grim speculations as to the feelings of a forsaken
1822  swimmer who should watch this star of his hope moving relentlessly away
1823  into the west, grower fainter each time it emerged from the waves,
1824  when--
1825  
1826  "Light ho!" shouted the lookout from the darkness aloft.
1827  "There's--light; 'bout--point--off--starb'd--bow!"
1828  
1829  "What kind?" hailed Jerry from the deck, straining his eyes to where, a
1830  dim blot against the stars, the figure of the lookout could be discerned
1831  standing by the rigging on the cross-trees.
1832  
1833  "Fixed white, red flash," called the man.
1834  
1835  "All right," shouted Jerry; and added in his ordinary tone of command
1836  to the hands on deck: "Lay along, now! Trim in main-sheet a bit--well
1837  enough. Now then, fore and head sheets. Good. That'll do.--We want to
1838  get what air there is," he added to himself.
1839  
1840  Although the wind was slight, yet about the Straits is always a
1841  strongish set of current. The surface current flows into the
1842  Mediterranean continuously, and it kept setting the Merle steadily
1843  ahead. When Taberman judged the light to be no more than five or six
1844  knots away, he sent below to rouse the captain, who was asleep. When
1845  Castleport came on deck, the bearing of the light was taken, the chart
1846  consulted, and a slight change made in the course. It was now calm, and
1847  the yacht, no longer steadied by the wind, rolled heavily.
1848  
1849  "We ought to see it air up before long," remarked Jack, after a short
1850  silence. "It's so beastly calm now. When it's calm on one side of the
1851  Straits, it's always blowing on the other. An Italian sea captain told
1852  me there is always just so much air about here, and however much or
1853  little is on one side, the balance is always kicking about on the
1854  other."
1855  
1856  "Then we'll take the sticks out of her, once we're through the
1857  Straits," Jerry responded with conviction.
1858  
1859  As the schooner entered the Straits, the blue-black sky to the eastward
1860  became dimly albescent, and shortly a blood-red moon rose slowly behind
1861  the inky mass of Monkey Mountain. The huge pile of rock, the more
1862  impressive though the less famous of the Pillars of Hercules, loomed
1863  vast, mysterious, and perdurable in the soft darkness. The waves, as the
1864  face of the moon cleared, were lit with a gray light.
1865  
1866  Suddenly, as a long, smooth swell shouldered the yacht past the edge of
1867  a small promontory, they opened out the lights of Tangiers on the
1868  starboard beam. The moon as yet illuminated only the western half of the
1869  scarped bowl in which lie the little villas which surround the town. The
1870  scattered lights on the east side of the valley were accentuated by the
1871  surrounding gloom.
1872  
1873  "There's Tangiers," cried Jack. "There's old Tangiers."
1874  
1875  "Those lights?" asked Jerry. "What sort of a place is it?"
1876  
1877  "Jolly little hole. All white and pink in the daytime, with red tile
1878  roofs. Hot as Tophet, though. There's Tariffa, boy! That's Tariffa over
1879  there."
1880  
1881  They excitedly discussed the points along their way. To Jerry it was
1882  all new, but Jack had traveled a good deal about the Mediterranean, and
1883  was well able to play the mentor. For an hour they talked, and the Merle
1884  drifted with the current; but they had not passed out of the shadow of
1885  Monkey Mountain before a faint breath of air stirred the headsails. It
1886  came stealing down out of the upper canvas, hot and dry.
1887  
1888  "By Jove!" cried Jack, "we'll have all the wind we want in a bit. You
1889  can tell how hard it is blowing outside the Straits by the distances it
1890  reaches in."
1891  
1892  Then he raised his voice, and called to the watch,--
1893  
1894  "Hello there! Clew up the topsails! Pass gaskets on them!"
1895  
1896  The men, who had a dog-like trust in the captain, obeyed quickly, though
1897  from the remarks they interchanged _sotto voce_ it was easy to see that
1898  the order puzzled them. When everything was made snug aloft, Jack had a
1899  reef tucked in the main and foresails, and the outer headsails stowed.
1900  
1901  Still no wind. The schooner slowly moved along the edge of the great
1902  shadow of the mountain, only her topmast trucks and the peak of her
1903  mainsail silvered by the moonlight.
1904  
1905  A dull, hoarse whisper, faint and continuous, was now audible ahead. It
1906  grew louder by very slow degrees, and Jerry, unused as he was to
1907  Mediterranean weather, knew it for the roar of a mighty wind. In the
1908  moonlight ahead the waters appeared troubled, the hard-heaving seas
1909  being strangely and almost weirdly demarked from the calm in which the
1910  Merle rolled forward languidly. All at once, as the yacht emerged from
1911  the obscurity of the mountain's shadow, a sudden gust of warm air struck
1912  her without warning, and heeled her lee-rail under.
1913  
1914  "Hard down!" roared Jack.
1915  
1916  Jerry leaped to the wheel, and it took all the force of himself and the
1917  helmsman to put the helm hard-a-lee. The Merle righted, and being
1918  unusually quick, flew into the eye of the wind. From the threshing sails
1919  came a thunderous volley of heavy boomings. The sheet-blocks were
1920  whipped to and fro with such violence that twice Jack saw red sparks
1921  struck from the fore-traveler guard. Then, as suddenly as it had come,
1922  the wind left, and it was only by the way she had gathered that the
1923  helmsman could pay the yacht off.
1924  
1925  "We are going to catch it for fair," Jack said. "Best dowse the foresail
1926  entirely, I fancy. Pass the word along to Gonzague to make all snug
1927  below. Jerry, step into the cabin and make sure of the course from off
1928  Ceuta to Port Mahon."
1929  
1930  "Right-o," answered Jerry briskly, diving down.
1931  
1932  "Get down the fores'l!" shouted the captain to the men.
1933  
1934  "Helm up a bit there--steady! That's the talk! Get all the stops
1935  on.--Now then--make fast that sheet there."
1936  
1937  The Merle was hardly on her course again when a second squall struck
1938  her. Her canvas having been reduced, however, the helmsman kept her
1939  broadside to it. The yacht's strongest point was the quickness with
1940  which she gathered way, and on this occasion, when nine tenths of her
1941  class would simply have lain over and quivered, she rushed ahead with
1942  the fury of an avenging goddess. When the hot flaw left her, she was at
1943  the very last verge of the calm water.
1944  
1945  "Stand by the main-sheet to square off when she meets it!" shouted Jack.
1946  
1947  The men had hardly time to get to their stations before a third squall
1948  caught the Merle and sent her tearing over the line into the full
1949  strength of the wind. The air, hot from the desert, and laden with fine,
1950  parching dust, sang in the shrouds and the running-rigging. It slashed
1951  the salt spindrift in the smarting faces of the men. The seas grew
1952  suddenly confounding in size; huge weltering masses--tons--of greenly
1953  black water wallowed without rhythm all about the yacht, up as high as
1954  the light-boards. To a landsman it would have seemed impossible that
1955  thus scourged by the sirocco across these maddened seas the schooner
1956  should escape destruction.
1957  
1958  The sheets were started, the yacht was paid off before the wind, and
1959  began the last stretch of her run. Tab came on deck with the course,
1960  staggering and holding on, and shouted it into Jack's ear. Jack nodded,
1961  and gave orders for setting it, a fresh departure being taken from the
1962  light on the mole at Ceuta.
1963  
1964  The Merle ran close in on the eastern side of Gibraltar. The great rock,
1965  sheer and silver-gray in the moonlight, rose out of the raging seas
1966  which ringed it about with a zone of roaring breakers. Grimly
1967  self-reliant, it stood grand, silent, stupendous, unassailable in the
1968  midst of the turmoil and uproar. As the yacht raced by, staggering under
1969  her reefed canvas, Taberman regarded the rock, in face of which their
1970  craft seemed a mere mote on the blast, with a feeling as near awe as it
1971  is possible for buoyant youth to feel. He did not speak until the Merle
1972  had swept past the rock-hewn fortress. Then he drew a deep breath and
1973  bent over so that Jack could hear him amid the hissing of the sirocco.
1974  
1975  "That's immense, Jack, isn't it?" he said.
1976  
1977  Without taking his eyes from the throat of the mainsail he was watching
1978  as a physician at a crisis watches the pulse of a patient, Jack nodded a
1979  deep assent.
1980  
1981  At times the Merle seemed fairly to leap like a flying fish from one
1982  wave-crest to the next in her northeasterly flight.
1983  
1984  [Illustration: Decoration]
1985  
1986  
1987  
1988  
1989  Chapter Six
1990  
1991  DINNER ASHORE
1992  
1993  
1994  On a Thursday afternoon in the middle of July, the Merle dropped anchor
1995  behind the inner mole of Nice. In her course northward from the Straits,
1996  she had passed to the eastward of the Baleares, crossed the Gulf of
1997  Lyons, and run smoothly into harbor before the same powerful wind that
1998  had greeted her so boisterously on her entrance into the Middle Sea.
1999  
2000  The moment when the port officer came aboard had been a nervous one, but
2001  the dapper little official had merely glanced at the yacht's papers,
2002  complimented the captain on his seamanship, and then gone ashore without
2003  a sign of suspicion.
2004  
2005  The yacht had no sooner been made trig and ship-shape, her sails stopped
2006  with "harbor furl," the canvas covers on, the boats unlashed and swung
2007  on the davits, the running-rigging coiled down, and the details proper
2008  to coming into port attended to, than Jack, unable to put off going
2009  ashore until the morrow, gave orders for the crew to turn out in their
2010  best attire. Then with Taberman he went below to array himself for the
2011  land. In Castleport's mind the idea of calling on Mrs. Fairhew and Miss
2012  Marchfield, who he knew should now be in Nice, was paramount to all
2013  else. He would see Mrs. Fairhew, he would see Katrine, and then--well,
2014  then it would be time to consider.
2015  
2016  Once below, Jack and Jerry began the overhauling of their wardrobes,
2017  doing their dressing half in their staterooms and half in the cabin,
2018  that they might go on with afternoon tea at the same time. During the
2019  voyage they had gone about most of the time in flannel shirts and duck
2020  trousers, the only two rules in regard to toilet having been that they
2021  should shave regularly, and that they should not come to dinner in
2022  oilers, no matter what the weather. The first rule had been framed by
2023  Jack; and Tab, as author of the second, had declared that he would
2024  rather eat hardtack in his pajamas, than a six-course dinner in his
2025  oilers. Now, as they stood in the doors of their staterooms examining
2026  their shore clothing,--each holding, like the Hatter at the trial of the
2027  Knave of Hearts, a teacup in his hand,--they had the air of being almost
2028  surprised at finding themselves in possession of so many garments, or
2029  of not knowing exactly what to do with them.
2030  
2031  "Got any extra duck trow-trows, Jack?" asked Jerry. "We made a great
2032  mistake not shipping a laundress along with the other stores."
2033  
2034  "Hanging them up on the rigging to dry doesn't give them an extra fine
2035  polish," Jack returned. "I have two pairs I've been saving for shore,
2036  and I suppose I can sacrifice one of them on the altar of friendship."
2037  
2038  "That's truly noble of you," Tab said, coming over to Jack's cabin after
2039  the clean ducks; "but it's all right. When we go ashore we'll take
2040  Gonzague and a bag of things, and have some real washing done on land.
2041  What's that official-looking envelope?"
2042  
2043  From the pocket of a coat which Castleport had thrown aside in his
2044  search for the desired garment, a long blue envelope, still sealed, had
2045  fallen to the floor. Jack pounced upon it, with an exclamation of
2046  dismay.
2047  
2048  "Great guns!" he exclaimed. "It's Uncle Randolph's mail!"
2049  
2050  "It's what?"
2051  
2052  "Why," the captain explained, rummaging in the pocket from which the
2053  letter had fallen and producing a couple of others, "I told you about
2054  the boy's bringing out the letters to the Merle while she was changing
2055  crews at North Haven."
2056  
2057  "You mean the letters the boy brought out for the President?"
2058  
2059  "Yes, damn it!" responded the other, regarding the letters with a
2060  troubled brow. "This is a pretty kettle of fish. Uncle Randolph's
2061  letters are apt to be important, and this one has a beastly official
2062  look. It's sure to be something that couldn't wait. It's probably the
2063  thing he was looking for when he gave orders to have his mail brought
2064  out to him."
2065  
2066  "'If not delivered in five days return to R. B. Tillington, 57 State
2067  Street, Boston,'" read Jerry over his shoulder. "Tillington's the
2068  zinc-mine man, isn't he?"
2069  
2070  "Zinc, copper, gold,--any old thing that you can make a mining
2071  speculation out of. I think he's a slippery old fraud, but he's hand in
2072  glove with Uncle Randolph; or rather they have a lot of business
2073  together. Uncle Randolph thinks Tillington wouldn't dare to play him
2074  false, but he's an eely old beggar. Anyhow, this letter may mean the
2075  making or the losing of a fortune for all I know. Gad! Running away with
2076  his yacht is nothing to going off with his letters!"
2077  
2078  "I don't suppose it would do to mail them here?" suggested Jerry.
2079  
2080  "That would dish us all right," Jack answered. "It would give us away by
2081  the postmark. Uncle Randolph isn't likely to think of our coming across.
2082  He can't know we were provisioned, and he very likely thinks we are
2083  still knocking about on the other side of the Atlantic."
2084  
2085  "He might find out about the stores by asking at the express offices and
2086  that sort of thing."
2087  
2088  "Why should he, unless something puts the idea into his head?"
2089  
2090  "I suppose he wouldn't," Jerry assented thoughtfully. "How would it do
2091  to return this letter to Tillington?"
2092  
2093  "Just as bad as to send it direct to Uncle Randolph. Once let them know
2094  at home where we are, and we are done for fast enough."
2095  
2096  "Well," Taberman said, after a brief pause in which he had apparently
2097  been summing up the situation in his mind, "the harm's done by this
2098  time, anyway; and I don't see that there's anything for us but to stick
2099  to our guns, blow high, blow low. We'll mail 'em when we get ready to go
2100  back."
2101  
2102  Castleport regarded the letters in his hand gravely.
2103  
2104  "I suppose there's nothing else to do," he said slowly. "The Merle is
2105  of course registered at Lloyd's, and he'd only have to cable over to
2106  have us nabbed anywhere along the whole coast."
2107  
2108  "He may see the arrival in the shipping-lists as it is, I should think,"
2109  Jerry observed rather gloomily.
2110  
2111  "Of course; but we've got to run our chances on that. He's not very much
2112  in the habit of studying the sailing-lists as far as I know, but he may
2113  do it now. Anyway we've got to run for luck."
2114  
2115  "The luck has been pretty good so far," was Jerry's consoling
2116  observation; "and I won't begin to distrust it now."
2117  
2118  The result of the conversation was that the letters were put carefully
2119  away, and the two adventurers resolved not to worry about them.
2120  Castleport admitted that the matter troubled him not a little, but he
2121  was under the circumstances disposed to accept his comrade's very
2122  sensible observation that after all the letters might be of no especial
2123  importance.
2124  
2125  "You see," Jerry said, with a laugh, as he gulped down the last of his
2126  tea, which had had time to become thoroughly cold, "we are really
2127  pirates, and here you go bringing the conscience of a gentleman into the
2128  business. None of that."
2129  
2130  Castleport laughed, and once more their attention was given to dressing
2131  for the shore.
2132  
2133  No one aboard understood the care and manipulation of the small
2134  steam-launch which the President used on state occasions, so they went
2135  ashore in the big cutter, with six men to pull and old Gonzague in
2136  charge.
2137  
2138  They landed at the quays, and left Gonzague to act as interpreter and
2139  mentor to the men, while they took their way across the Quay Rosaglio
2140  and along the narrow Rue Paglione. They came out soon upon the Promenade
2141  des Anglais, thronged, in spite of the time of year, with foreigners of
2142  many nationalities. Delicate French ladies in the latest fashions from
2143  Paris, were here escorted by anæmic gentlemen looking absurdly out of
2144  place in evening dress; vulgar Teutons in baggy trousers with impossibly
2145  dowdy wives, legitimate evolutions from generations of sauerkraut and
2146  beer; now and then an unmistakable "remittance man" from England, with
2147  puffy eye-sockets and brutal face, accompanied by the companion paid by
2148  some noble family to take charge of the prodigal till he drank himself
2149  into a dishonored grave; the British cleric, too, with the inevitable
2150  string of hopelessly dull daughters tagging after him like bobs on a
2151  kite; swarthy Roumanians or Swabians; Russians deep-eyed and surrounded
2152  by an almost palpable atmosphere of haughtiness; in a word, the
2153  cosmopolitan crowd of a fashionable promenade of Southern Europe.
2154  Through such a throng Jack and Jerry made their way toward the centre of
2155  the foreign element of the better sort, the Hôtel des Anglais.
2156  
2157  As they reached their destination, Jack became visibly excited, and made
2158  his way to the office with an air of determination vastly amusing to his
2159  companion. He was on the point of asking for Mrs. Fairhew when he was
2160  startled by a voice behind him.
2161  
2162  "Why, Mr. Castleport!"
2163  
2164  Her voice! Jack spun around like a teetotum.
2165  
2166  "Katrine--Miss Marchfield!" he cried. "How do you do? I--I-- You know, I
2167  came here--this minute--I was just going to ask if you were here."
2168  
2169  "Well," laughed the lady, whose heightened color and shining eyes were
2170  evidences of a pleasant excitement, "you see I am.--Oh, Mr. Taberman,
2171  how do you do? I'm delighted to see you."
2172  
2173  "How are you?" responded Jerry, taking her slim hand in his own hard
2174  paw. "It's awfully jolly to see you here. How's Mrs. Fairhew? Well, I
2175  hope."
2176  
2177  "Yes, thank you," answered Katrine. "She's never better than when she's
2178  traveling, you know."
2179  
2180  Miss Katrine Marchfield was one of those girls who, though not
2181  beautiful, are more than pretty. She was too attractive to be fairly
2182  disposed of by being credited with mere prettiness; yet she had not
2183  fully that quality, august and indefinable, which confers upon the
2184  fortunate possessor real beauty. She was slightly above medium height,
2185  and could now, having been out for a couple of winters, carry herself
2186  exquisitely. A beautiful figure could not have been denied her by the
2187  most envious rival; and her fairly broad shoulders, always drawn well
2188  back, gave her a charming air of delicately athletic power. Her face, at
2189  first merely piquant,--perhaps from the slight arching of her eyebrows
2190  and the wholly delightful way in which she carried her head,--showed at
2191  a second glance, by the height of the forehead, the clear chiseling of
2192  the features, and the intelligent sympathy of the gray eyes, a true and
2193  sensitive nobility of nature which gave to her countenance a charm at
2194  once fine and abiding. Her eyes Jack--and for that matter a score of
2195  adoring youths--considered her greatest beauty. They were at times
2196  thoughtful, at others sparkling with vivacity. Now and then they might
2197  be surprised in a quickly vanishing expression wistful or even almost
2198  sad, as if some deeper self looked out but did not will to be seen. A
2199  mouth small, the upper lip a trifle fuller than the under; a nose almost
2200  Greek; and above the high forehead a cloud of dusky brown hair,--these
2201  physical attributes, with a sympathetic temperament and a mind sensible
2202  yet deliciously feminine, a pleasant voice and a delightful laugh, had
2203  won for Katrine Marchfield more conquests than could be boasted by many
2204  an older woman of really marked beauty.
2205  
2206  Her relations with Jack Castleport, whether she had admitted it to
2207  herself or not, had for some time been greatly different from those she
2208  held with any one else. They had met at a dinner shortly after Katrine,
2209  for two years doubly orphaned, had come from Philadelphia to live with
2210  her widowed aunt, Mrs. Fairhew, in Boston. After meeting Katrine,
2211  Castleport had taken to calling at Mrs. Fairhew's, at first nominally to
2212  see the aunt and later frankly to see the niece. He was at this time a
2213  Junior at Harvard, and a popular man on both sides of the river; the
2214  acquaintance during his Senior year had ripened into friendship, and the
2215  most important feature of Class Day for Jack was the presence of Miss
2216  Marchfield; he had thought more of her in the audience than of the
2217  dignitaries on the platform when on Commencement Day he had taken his
2218  degree; and what with dancing with Katrine, driving with Katrine, and
2219  dreaming of Katrine for the winter which lay between Harvard and this
2220  summer, he had come to measure the uses of life chiefly as they might
2221  help to make her care for him or to reveal to him what were her feelings
2222  toward him.
2223  
2224  For a moment or two the three Americans stood talking near the desk of
2225  the hotel. Then Miss Marchfield stepped forward and dropped into the
2226  mail-box some letters she was carrying.
2227  
2228  "If you'll excuse me one minute," she said, "I'll send for Aunt Anne,
2229  and see about dinner. Of course you'll stay to dine?"
2230  
2231  "Delighted," Jack said. "That is," he added, "if it's all right for us
2232  in these clothes. You see, we stupidly came off without evening togs."
2233  
2234  "That's all right," Katrine returned; and went away smiling.
2235  
2236  Jack looked after her with an expression which made Jerry smile.
2237  
2238  "Gad! She's looking ten times better than when she left home," Tab said
2239  in an undertone.
2240  
2241  "She always does," the captain responded with fervent fatuousness. "She
2242  can't help it, you know. God bless me," he added with equal fervor and
2243  absurdity, "it's worth coming over steerage just to hear her voice!"
2244  
2245  "Well, you _are_ hit!" commented his friend; and then, seeing a shade
2246  come over Jack's face, he laid his hand on his friend's shoulder, and
2247  added: "Don't mind my chaff, old man. I really wish you all kinds of
2248  luck."
2249  
2250  Jack gave him a flash of sympathy and understanding, and then turned his
2251  head aside.
2252  
2253  "Pity we haven't got evening slops," Jerry remarked, by way of changing
2254  the conversation; "but I suppose we'll do, seeing the way we came over,
2255  and all that."
2256  
2257  "I'm not worrying about clothes," returned the captain of the Merle.
2258  "Men wear all sorts of things traveling. I'm thinking what Mrs.
2259  Fairhew'll say about our being here in the yacht without Uncle
2260  Randolph."
2261  
2262  "What's your game if we're quizzed about the President?"
2263  
2264  "I'm hanged if I really know," Jack returned; "but I've got to pull it
2265  through somehow, and you'll have to follow my lead."
2266  
2267  He had time to say no more, for Katrine came forward to rejoin them, and
2268  before she had reached the friends, Mrs. Fairhew appeared.
2269  
2270  Mrs. Fairhew was a striking woman of some forty years, of medium
2271  height, with quick and alert bearing, with the unmistakable air of a
2272  well-bred woman of the world. A widow of some six years, she still,
2273  except upon occasions of particular state, wore black,--from devotional
2274  feeling, according to her friends, and, according to the captious,
2275  because it so well became her. Between her and her niece existed a
2276  subtle and baffling likeness, but in what it consisted one would have
2277  found it well-nigh impossible to say. Of good birth, perfect breeding,
2278  and a wide social experience, she possessed also an intellect naturally
2279  good and improved by careful training; while for her rare good taste she
2280  was perhaps equally indebted to nature and to a somewhat old-fashioned
2281  training in whatever is best in the English classics. With these good
2282  gifts and graces and a perfect poise, she combined whatever is most
2283  admirable in the best type of American gentlewoman.
2284  
2285  "Mr. Castleport," she said, giving that gentleman her hand with gracious
2286  cordiality, "this is an unexpected pleasure! How do you do, Mr.
2287  Taberman. I am very glad to see you both."
2288  
2289  Greetings were exchanged, and then, after a moment's chatting, the men
2290  gave over their hats to an attendant, and the party went into the
2291  dining-room. On account of the season, the number of people at the
2292  hotel was comparatively small, and the huge _salle à manger_, with its
2293  slim pilasters and its long French windows, its tubs of palmetto and
2294  oleander, might have impressed Jack and Jerry as rather barn-like and
2295  forsaken had either been in the mood to find anything in their
2296  surroundings unsatisfactory. The four made their way to a small square
2297  table in an alcove, behind which stood a tall, round-shouldered waiter
2298  in an antediluvian dress-suit. Jack put Katrine into her chair and was
2299  placed next her, and with much pleasant talk the party began dinner.
2300  
2301  The fish was served before any mention was made of the President. Then
2302  Jack suddenly found himself in dangerous waters, owing to a random
2303  remark from Mrs. Fairhew.
2304  
2305  "And Mr. Drake?" she asked. "What a pity he didn't come too. I suppose
2306  he couldn't get away."
2307  
2308  "Not on the Merle," responded Jack. "It takes a long time to cross on
2309  such a small boat."
2310  
2311  Jerry watched his friend closely to detect signs of embarrassment, but
2312  was able to perceive nothing more than a faint flush in the brown
2313  cheeks. He recalled the captain's words about following his lead, and at
2314  this point, in his own picturesque phraseology, "shoved in his oar."
2315  
2316  "Besides," he said glibly, with a secret mischievous glee at feeling
2317  Jack's anxious eye upon him, "it's so hard to get the President away
2318  from his everlasting bridge,--_Pons Asinorum_, I call it. When we left
2319  North Haven he was so absorbed in his game that he didn't even see us
2320  off."
2321  
2322  "I didn't know he was so attached to cards," Mrs. Fairhew commented,
2323  with a smile. "As you have the yacht, Mr. Taberman, you should at least
2324  speak well of the bridge that has brought you over."
2325  
2326  "Did Mr. Drake put you two in charge of his sailing-master, Mr.
2327  Taberman?" asked Katrine, with a suspicion of a glance at Jack, as if
2328  she meant to tease him.
2329  
2330  "No," returned Jerrold. "Jack and I did the navigating; he's a past
2331  master, I assure you."
2332  
2333  "Yes," rejoined Katrine, "but I should have fancied he would have had
2334  some one that was--Well, some one with a professional experience, you
2335  know."
2336  
2337  "If the idea struck him he didn't mention it," put in Jack. "If it
2338  occurred to him after we left, I can't tell, as I haven't heard from
2339  him."
2340  
2341  "Haven't heard from him!" exclaimed Mrs. Fairhew in mild surprise.
2342  "Haven't you been to your bankers?"
2343  
2344  "Haven't been anywhere except at this hotel," Jack returned sturdily;
2345  and then added: "It was after bank hours when we came ashore."
2346  
2347  "Of course you cabled him your arrival?"
2348  
2349  "Mercy! I might have done that, mightn't I? Upon my word, it never
2350  occurred to me."
2351  
2352  "Thoughtful of you," Katrine commented demurely.
2353  
2354  "Well, I did get some letters ready to send to him," Jack protested,
2355  while Jerry grinned broadly.
2356  
2357  "Got them ready! How like a man!" laughed Mrs. Fairhew. "A woman would
2358  have had them ready before she saw land, and had them mailed by the time
2359  the anchor was down."
2360  
2361  "So did Jack have them ready," put in Jerry imperturbably.
2362  
2363  "Then it's doubly dreadful that they are not posted," retorted Mrs.
2364  Fairhew.
2365  
2366  Jack leaned forward and settled a pink candle-shade that threatened a
2367  conflagration, and by a comment on the inflammability of these table
2368  ornaments managed to bring the conversation into safer channels.
2369  
2370  In the course of the talk it transpired that the ladies had no very
2371  definite plans, except that Mrs. Fairhew had determined, despite the
2372  heat of the Italian summer, to visit an old school friend, whose
2373  husband was vice-consul at Naples.
2374  
2375  "I fancy," she said, "that we shall go straight to Genoa. I'm going to
2376  make Katrine work, and to see that she does her duty by the galleries
2377  and things,--Florence and all the Tuscan cities, you know. Then Rome and
2378  the Campagna. It will be dreadfully hard on us both, I dare say, but we
2379  shall be upheld by the proud consciousness of doing our best."
2380  
2381  She made a little gesture of comical despair, and her niece laughed.
2382  
2383  "It would doubtless be intolerable to either of you without the other,"
2384  said Jerry in one of his boyishly elaborate attempts to be gallant.
2385  
2386  Mrs. Fairhew regarded him with a glance well-bred though quizzical, but
2387  evidently perceived that he was completely sincere in his desire to say
2388  something agreeable, and smiled, although less broadly than Katrine, who
2389  showed in her amusement a row of beautiful teeth.
2390  
2391  "Won't it be pretty hot in the south?" asked Jack. "I've never been in
2392  Naples in summer, nor south of Rome, in fact; but I've always been told
2393  that it is too torrid for foreigners."
2394  
2395  "Oh, we are used to it," Mrs. Fairhew returned. "Besides, it is after
2396  all the English that have spread the stories about Italy's being so
2397  hot. They've been kept at so low a temperature all their lives by their
2398  horrid fogs that they're the greatest babies imaginable about climate."
2399  
2400  "I fancy you're right," assented Jack. "At all events, as you are used
2401  to all climates, and as Miss Marchfield comes from Philadelphia"--
2402  
2403  "Oh, but I've never been there in summer," Katrine broke in. "And,
2404  besides, I've lived in Boston so long that"--
2405  
2406  "That you can stand anything?" interrupted Jerry in turn.
2407  
2408  "I think I can," laughed Katrine.
2409  
2410  Mrs. Fairhew toyed with her coffee-spoon thoughtfully a moment; then she
2411  looked up at Jack.
2412  
2413  "Where are you bound, Mr. Castleport?" she asked.
2414  
2415  "I don't know," Jack answered quite frankly. "I think we shall probably
2416  coast along--Monaco, Bordighera, and Mentone, you know; and then go to
2417  Genoa. Then perhaps we'll see Elba and Naples and Capri. After that we
2418  must start for home. Nothing is settled with us."
2419  
2420  "I detest Monaco," Mrs. Fairhew said, with some irrelevance.
2421  
2422  "Why?" inquired Jack, with a smile. "Does the gambling offend the
2423  Puritan that is in every Bostonian?"
2424  
2425  "It certainly does," was the reply, "though my aversion isn't entirely a
2426  matter of conscience. I bought it on the spot for a thousand francs."
2427  
2428  "That was awfully dear," remarked Jerry. "It would have been much
2429  cheaper to be born with it."
2430  
2431  "As in your case?" asked the lady, raising her eyebrows a little and
2432  smiling.
2433  
2434  "Oh, one can't inherit all the virtues!" responded Taberman with the
2435  greatest seriousness.
2436  
2437  "Most certainly not," laughed Mrs. Fairhew. "At least I had not that
2438  good fortune."
2439  
2440  "Nature left you one to get for yourself, because she knew you'd do it
2441  so easily," Tab said gallantly.
2442  
2443  "Really," cried the lady, "you are evidently determined to overwhelm me,
2444  Mr. Taberman. Compliments drop from your lips like the traditional
2445  showers of pearls."
2446  
2447  "There are frogs too in that fairy story," suggested Jack.
2448  
2449  "Oh, Mr. Castleport," declared Katrine, coming to the rescue of Jerry,
2450  "that is simply brutal."
2451  
2452  "Of course it's brutal," retorted Jack, willfully twisting her meaning,
2453  "but he keeps it up all the same."
2454  
2455  Jerry tried to defend himself by charging Jack with never being able to
2456  appreciate a compliment unless he were himself the subject, and so they
2457  drifted lightly from one bit of good-natured raillery to another. Now
2458  and then a more serious note was struck, and through it all the spirit
2459  of the party was more kindly and friendly than could be pictured by any
2460  words in which they might have tried to express it.
2461  
2462  When dinner was over, they went for a short stroll on the promenade. It
2463  naturally happened that Mrs. Fairhew walked with Taberman, and that Jack
2464  and Katrine strolled on together some little distance behind.
2465  
2466  "You don't know," said Jack, for the fourth or fifth time that evening,
2467  but with an evident sincerity which might have excused even further
2468  repetition, "how good it is to see you again."
2469  
2470  "Yes," Katrine responded with a carelessness too complete to be entirely
2471  genuine, "I suppose that it must be pleasant for you to see any one
2472  after being cooped up in a boat for five or six weeks."
2473  
2474  "That's not at all what I meant," he returned pointedly, and with a
2475  little vexation.
2476  
2477  "Perhaps not; but it's practically what you said."
2478  
2479  "I said it gave me pleasure to see you," Jack insisted, with a daring
2480  emphasis on the final pronoun.
2481  
2482  "Oh, a compliment!" she exclaimed, as if the thought had just struck
2483  her.
2484  
2485  "You may take it as such," he replied rather grumpily. "It's the
2486  feminine attitude toward everything."
2487  
2488  Katrine was silent a moment, examining with an appearance of the
2489  greatest interest the ground at her feet.
2490  
2491  "How queer you are this evening," she said at length.
2492  
2493  "Am I?" he retorted. "Well, I suppose if I'm only amusing into the
2494  bargain that's all that's necessary."
2495  
2496  Another brief interval of silence intervened, and then he remarked
2497  blunderingly:--
2498  
2499  "I suppose it makes very little difference to you whether you see any
2500  one while you're here."
2501  
2502  "What an atrocious reflection on my efforts to be entertaining," she
2503  laughed.
2504  
2505  "Oh," he said savagely, "that's a nice meaning to twist out of my words!
2506  You know I don't mean that."
2507  
2508  "You seem to have some difficulty in saying what you do mean this
2509  evening," Katrine commented mockingly.
2510  
2511  Jack laughed uneasily, with that absurdly tragic air possible only to a
2512  young man much in love.
2513  
2514  "See here," he asked explosively, "why do you think I came over here?"
2515  
2516  "I'm sure I can't say, Mr. Castleport," she replied, with a touch of
2517  coolness. "I never was good at riddles. Don't you think we had better
2518  catch up with Aunt Anne and Mr. Taberman?"
2519  
2520  And greatly to his own disgust, and perhaps, could he but have known the
2521  truth, to the secret disappointment of Katrine, Jack acted upon her
2522  suggestion without a word more.
2523  
2524  As they were taking leave of the ladies at the hotel a little later,
2525  Jerry broke out with a clumsily worded invitation that they should on
2526  the morrow go for a sail on the Merle.
2527  
2528  "You are really very good, Mr. Taberman," Mrs. Fairhew said, "but I 'm
2529  afraid it's only half an invitation, for Mr. Castleport doesn't second
2530  it."
2531  
2532  "I certainly do," Jack responded. "I was hesitating only because I
2533  didn't think the yacht, just in from an ocean voyage, was exactly in
2534  trim. I wasn't sure it was fair to invite you."
2535  
2536  "I think we can put up with anything that is amiss in that line," Mrs.
2537  Fairhew answered, smiling. "What do you say, Katrine? Would you like to
2538  go?"
2539  
2540  "Very much, Aunt Anne," her niece said, with a quick little glance at
2541  Jack, a sort of bird-twinkle of the eyes, "if we shall not be too
2542  intrusive."
2543  
2544  "Capital!" cried Jack, whose good nature had returned, and who was
2545  anxious to make amends for his fit of pique. "I'll call for you in the
2546  morning at about noon, if that will suit you. We shall want a little
2547  time to get the yacht in trim."
2548  
2549  "Any time after ten will do for us," Mrs. Fairhew answered. "Don't, I
2550  beg, bother too much about making things neat. I know how necessary
2551  disorder is to the real happiness of you men."
2552  
2553  [Illustration: Decoration]
2554  
2555  
2556  
2557  
2558  Chapter Seven
2559  
2560  LUNCHEON ABOARD
2561  
2562  
2563  Noon.
2564  
2565  The famous promenade was deserted, and all the foreigners who were able
2566  were safe in the coolest retirement of their little pink and white
2567  villas. A warm off-shore breeze wandered through the silent streets of
2568  Nice, came to the water-front, and there, as if alarmed by the noise and
2569  bustle of the few sailors and fishermen whom the heat had not driven
2570  from the quays, grew brisker and fled away southward over the sea.
2571  
2572  Down one of the smaller streets between the Hôtel des Anglais and the
2573  Porta Vecchia, Mrs. Fairhew and her niece, escorted by Jack, were making
2574  their way. Miss Marchfield, dressed in a simple gown of white, looked
2575  deliciously rosy under her red sunshade. Mrs. Fairhew walked in the
2576  narrow strip of shadow next the wall; Katrine was between her and Jack,
2577  who, owing to the straitness of the sidewalk, picked his way--to the
2578  evident amusement of Miss Marchfield--along the kennel. As Katrine was
2579  fond of him, she paradoxically took unfailing delight in seeing him
2580  humiliated, always provided, of course, that no one other than herself
2581  was the author of the discomfort. The three were nearing the water-front
2582  when the elder lady broke a silence of some minutes' duration.
2583  
2584  "I hope the yacht is not very much farther, Mr. Castleport," she
2585  ventured.
2586  
2587  "No," Jack answered, "she's at the foot of the next street. 'Twas
2588  awfully stupid of me not to have got hold of a fiacre, but it seems so
2589  short a distance for me to walk that I didn't think."
2590  
2591  "I wonder why a yacht is always _she_ and _her_," observed Katrine. "Why
2592  not _it_?"
2593  
2594  "Oh, the reason's plain enough," was Jack's answer. "Yachts have two
2595  characteristics that are thoroughly feminine,--caprice and beauty."
2596  
2597  "It is good of you to temper the aspersion on my sex with a compliment,"
2598  Katrine returned.
2599  
2600  "It is obliging in me," Jack assented; "but politeness requires that I
2601  should stretch a point, since you are my guest."
2602  
2603  "I am sorry to put you to the inconvenience," she said.
2604  
2605  "Of being polite? Thank you!"
2606  
2607  "Do you know, I'm sorry that your uncle is not here, Mr. Castleport,"
2608  said Mrs. Fairhew, as they turned the corner. "It is all very well to
2609  have an old woman for a chaperon, but it is rather hard on you and Mr.
2610  Taberman not to have some older man to talk to me."
2611  
2612  "Oh, you mustn't depreciate your charm at the expense of your age," Jack
2613  cried.
2614  
2615  "Very pretty," laughed Mrs. Fairhew; "but your uncle"--
2616  
2617  "Ouch!" exclaimed Jack, making a fine show of stubbing the toe of his
2618  rubber-soled shoe against a projecting paving-stone.
2619  
2620  "What did you say?" inquired Katrine, with an air of mild interest.
2621  
2622  "Nothing. I stubbed my toe on that beastly stone," answered Jack, with a
2623  feeling of satisfaction that the President was once more shelved. "Now,"
2624  he added, "the boat is just here."
2625  
2626  A small but motley crowd was scattered along the water-front: bronzed
2627  fishermen, with close-cropped hair and long earrings, carrying osier
2628  baskets of shining sardines from their boats to their little carts; fat,
2629  raucous-voiced women, with red or yellow scarves pinned across their
2630  bosoms; lean-shanked 'longshoremen, too old for the sea this many a day;
2631  brown sailors, picking their way among the piles of iridescent
2632  fish,--liver-colored squid and flabby octopi; half-naked boys,
2633  outrageous and beautiful; with a miscellaneous sprinkling of human
2634  flotsam and jetsam, as if the sea had cast them up battered and damaged.
2635  Over all floated a distracting hubbub, made up of the rattling of
2636  cart-wheels on the flags, the shrill cries of the venders, the calls of
2637  the lads, the songs of the fishermen, and a medley of oaths, jests,
2638  curses, directions, questions, and all sorts of vociferous shoutings.
2639  
2640  Both the ladies drew closer to Jack, who, masterfully making his way
2641  through the press, piloted them across the quay. At the landing-steps
2642  they found Jerry and the Merle's cutter, the object of the staring
2643  curiosity and admiration of the wharf-rats and the loungers of the
2644  docks.
2645  
2646  "Good-morning, Mr. Taberman. Have we kept you waiting long?" asked Mrs.
2647  Fairhew.
2648  
2649  Tab had been broiling for half an hour, but was too courteous to say so.
2650  He responded cheerily, then helped the ladies aboard, and established
2651  them in the sheets. Jack took the tiller-lines, word was given, and the
2652  men fell to pulling. The breeze was fresher and cooler on the water; it
2653  made the ripples dance and glitter in the sunshine, and kept playfully
2654  curling the ensign at the stern of the cutter about Jack's head.
2655  According to previous instructions, the watch on the Merle got up anchor
2656  on seeing the cutter leave the quay, and were now holding the yacht in
2657  the wind's eye. When the boat came alongside, the ladies were handed
2658  aboard, the guest-salute was fired, the cutter was hoisted to the
2659  davits, and the yacht was paid off.
2660  
2661  They ran out past the old battery and the lighthouse on the outer mole,
2662  and coasted along to the westward. In the bright sunlight the numerous
2663  dwellings--villas, hotels, and _pensions_--showing among the green
2664  foliage of the trees looked very gay and attractive. The sea was dimpled
2665  with laughter. The breeze, although it gave promise of freshening, was
2666  now only strong enough to make the schooner, which was carrying all
2667  sail, heel gracefully as she slipped along. The day was perfect for
2668  light sailing.
2669  
2670  At one o'clock old Gonzague, his linen jacket dazzling in its whiteness
2671  and his snowy hair brushed back from his high forehead, served luncheon.
2672  Jack sat by Mrs. Fairhew on the starboard side, with Katrine and Jerry
2673  opposite. Gonzague had outdone himself for the occasion. A Provençal by
2674  birth, he knew the culinary value of all the wares--to foreign eyes so
2675  puzzlingly useless and hopelessly inedible--displayed in Mediterranean
2676  markets. The dishes which appeared on the table made Jack and Tab stare:
2677  fresh sardines broiled and served with some mysterious sauce of which
2678  they tried in vain to guess the ingredients; something which Katrine
2679  pronounced delicious until she discovered it to be cuttlefish, and then
2680  could not be prevailed upon to taste further; a salad which had lettuce
2681  as its obvious foundation, but which was fragrant with a dozen strange
2682  and piquant herbs; ripe citrons and limes; figs and bullaces; and a
2683  wonderful fruity sherbet for dessert.
2684  
2685  "Do you generally fare like this on board the Merle?" Mrs. Fairhew
2686  inquired. "If you do, I should like to come here to board while you are
2687  in harbor."
2688  
2689  "Not much," returned Jerry bluntly. "This is all Gonzague's gallantry to
2690  you ladies. As a rule he gives us only pork and beans."
2691  
2692  "Dear me," she commented. "That's pretty hard fare."
2693  
2694  "Do you really have to live on pork and beans on a cruise?" asked
2695  Katrine.
2696  
2697  "Jerry was only speaking figuratively," explained Jack, with a laugh.
2698  "Of course we do better than that. The only time we really suffered was
2699  in a bit of a shake-up we had on the way over. The second week out we
2700  had a blow, and had to live on hardtack and coffee for three days."
2701  
2702  "And Gonzague must have stood on his head to make the coffee, too," put
2703  in Tab.
2704  
2705  "Was it really so bad as that?" asked Katrine. "I mean," she explained
2706  as the others laughed, "did it really blow so hard he couldn't cook
2707  things?"
2708  
2709  "Well," responded Taberman, "for forty hours we had it so hard we jolly
2710  well thought we'd have to cut."
2711  
2712  "Cut?" queried Mrs. Fairhew.
2713  
2714  "Yes, the sticks, you know," Jack explained.
2715  
2716  From the expression on her face it was abundantly evident that the lady
2717  did not know, but she said nothing. She had but the most casual
2718  acquaintance with nautical affairs, and made no pretense of
2719  understanding the speech of mariners; and she was always willing to let
2720  a matter of this sort go, rather than to submit to a lengthy exposition.
2721  
2722  Katrine, on the other hand, while of course not proficient in the art of
2723  handling yachts, knew enough to appreciate that when cutting away the
2724  masts had been contemplated, things must have been at a pass really
2725  dangerous. Now she made no comment, but she gave a swift glance at
2726  Jack, that had in it much of the admiration which Desdemona felt at the
2727  recital of the perils through which Othello had borne himself bravely.
2728  Jack happened to catch her eye; she flushed and turned to Jerry.
2729  
2730  "Don't you tire of it all?" she asked. "I should think that to have the
2731  monotony broken only by danger in which you can't have any rest or
2732  comfort would be dreadfully wearisome."
2733  
2734  "Oh, it's great sport!" cried Tab heartily. "Besides, you know, there
2735  are no end of things to do."
2736  
2737  "Such as what?" inquired Mrs. Fairhew. "I've always found the ocean
2738  voyage the most boresome thing about traveling, although I'm a perfectly
2739  good sailor."
2740  
2741  "Oh," said Jerry, with a flourish of his cigarette,--for coffee had been
2742  served and the ladies had permitted smoking,--"there are rope-ends to be
2743  attended to, and gear changed, and all that sort of thing, besides
2744  seeing that the men go over the brasswork properly every day; and there
2745  is taking sights, and making reckonings, and all sorts of things."
2746  
2747  "But I thought the men did all the work on the ropes and things."
2748  
2749  "So they do," Jack said, with a smile; "but it is our business to tell
2750  them what to do and to see that they do it. You must remember that we
2751  are the ship's officers."
2752  
2753  "We have to look things over all the time," Jerry added. "Just before we
2754  went ashore to-day I saw a thing that'll have to be attended to as soon
2755  as we get back at anchor. The fore-peak halyards are 'most chafed
2756  through where they reeve through the block on the cap."
2757  
2758  "Dear me!" said Mrs. Fairhew. "Is it dangerous?"
2759  
2760  "Not in the least dangerous," Jack returned reassuringly. "Is it really
2761  bad, Tab?"
2762  
2763  "Oh, well, I fancy it'll hold; leastways if there's no sudden strain on
2764  it. The rope's new enough; but it jammed there the other day, you
2765  remember."
2766  
2767  "Well, let's go on deck," suggested the captain. "It's such a gorgeous
2768  day, it's a shame to miss any of it."
2769  
2770  On coming up they found that the wind had so freshened that the
2771  fore-topsail and staysail had been struck, as well as the outer jib.
2772  
2773  "We can run on till about four o'clock," Castleport said, "and have
2774  plenty of time to run back with this wind."
2775  
2776  They still held to the westward, keeping about a mile off shore, now
2777  and then passing fishing craft, headed for Nice, their big lateen sails
2778  shining in the sunlight. Jack, watching Katrine keenly, read her delight
2779  and enjoyment in her eyes, and could see how she responded to the beauty
2780  of the day, the picturesqueness of the shore, the exhilaration of the
2781  wind, and the sparkling sea. At eight bells they had tea _au Russe_ on
2782  deck, and before they had finished drinking it the Merle was put about
2783  and headed for the harbor.
2784  
2785  They had hardly gone a knot before they fell in with a large black yawl
2786  flying the English colors and the burgee of the Royal Yacht Squadron.
2787  She was sailing easily along under all lower canvas, her black hull
2788  lifting gracefully over the sloping seas at about two cable-lengths
2789  ahead. She was in cruising rig, with no boom to her mainsail, yet was so
2790  large that her spread of canvas was at half a glance much greater than
2791  that of the Merle. She crossed the schooner's bows, and then, luffing
2792  occasionally, waited until the American yacht was on her beam.
2793  
2794  "Looks's though she wanted something of us," remarked Jerry. "Will you
2795  take another look at her, Miss Marchfield?" And he handed her the
2796  glasses.
2797  
2798  "She is a beauty!" exclaimed Katrine, regarding the yawl through the
2799  binoculars. "I can see her name now. I-s-i-s Isis, of--of Plymouth.
2800  Don't you want to look at her, Aunt Anne?"
2801  
2802  Mrs. Fairhew took the glasses with the air of a person doing a favor,
2803  and stared at the yawl in a perfunctory manner.
2804  
2805  "What an absurd bobtail of a sail that is set 'way back," she observed.
2806  "It looks quite like a deformity."
2807  
2808  "That's for balance in heavy weather," said Jerry, with gusto. "Hadn't
2809  we better salute, Jack?"
2810  
2811  "I suppose so," was the answer. "See; he's fallen off. Means to give us
2812  a run for it, I fancy."
2813  
2814  The Merle dipped her ensign, and the Englishman returned the salute in
2815  kind.
2816  
2817  "I say," cried Jerry, "they're setting their topsail. They want a race
2818  in earnest."
2819  
2820  "They've an able boat, to carry all sail when it's breezed up like
2821  this," commented Jack, giving the black yawl a critical look.
2822  
2823  "Come!" urged Tab. "Let's take a brace and give 'em a run for their
2824  money. We can beat 'em all right enough, both sides of the Atlantic."
2825  
2826  Jack looked first at Katrine and then at her aunt.
2827  
2828  "Would you mind?" he asked.
2829  
2830  "Mind?" cried Mrs. Fairhew, "I shouldn't mind it the least in the
2831  world--especially if we beat them."
2832  
2833  "All right," shouted Tab, leaping boyishly out of his wicker chair.
2834  "We'll show 'em! Watch along!" he roared to the crew.
2835  
2836  "Sway up on the main-peak halyards there," sang out Jack, who had also
2837  started up quickly. "That's good! Fore-peak now--that'll do! Set
2838  fore-topsail there--haul away! Good enough! All hands up to windward!"
2839  Then he turned to the helmsman. "I'll take her," he said. "You get up to
2840  windward with the rest."
2841  
2842  The man handed the helm over to him, and the race began.
2843  
2844  The yawl was on the windward beam, and both she and the schooner were
2845  carrying so much sail as now and again to be heeled lee rail under. At
2846  the end of twenty minutes the American boat seemed to be drawing ahead,
2847  although the Englishman, his red flag blowing out from his maintop, was
2848  still to windward.
2849  
2850  Katrine and her aunt had abandoned their chairs for the weather transom
2851  of the cockpit. Katrine was thoroughly alive to the excitement of this
2852  impromptu contest, while Mrs. Fairhew's well-bred face wore a smile
2853  which might be taken to signify either her superiority to such a
2854  youthful means of enjoyment or confidence in the power of the Merle to
2855  outstrip her rival.
2856  
2857  Jack, his strong, shapely hands grasping the spokes of the wheel,
2858  glanced only from the sails aloft to the yawl and back again. Katrine
2859  watched him furtively. His keen, eager pose, wholly free from
2860  self-consciousness and suggestive of power and vigilant activity, his
2861  masterful management of his craft,--she noted them all, and felt a
2862  certain pleasure in them, as if in some way she were responsible for
2863  them.
2864  
2865  "Think we'll come 'round, Jerrold," said the captain.
2866  
2867  He gave a rapid succession of orders as he twirled the spokes to port.
2868  The Merle came about on the other tack, the men got to stations on the
2869  weather side, and the ladies changed their places.
2870  
2871  "Now we'll see how much we've gained on them," said Jerry, half to the
2872  guests and half to himself.
2873  
2874  They drove toward the shore in the roughening sea, the port runway being
2875  now covered with a thin sheet of hissing green water. Up forward an
2876  occasional wave would come slap against the yacht's shoulder with a
2877  sound like a rifle-shot. The Isis crossed their bows at a distance so
2878  little ahead of them that her name and hail could be read easily
2879  without the aid of a glass.
2880  
2881  "We're outfooting them, Jack. We'll have 'em cold in twenty minutes!"
2882  cried Tab enthusiastically.
2883  
2884  "Don't count your chickens before they're hatched," laughed Katrine.
2885  
2886  "Oh, but we can't help doing 'em," he responded. "We'll have 'em so
2887  walloped that they'll go into dry-dock for a month."
2888  
2889  "You'd better rap on wood, Mr. Taberman," cautioned Mrs. Fairhew, with a
2890  smile. "I don't wish to be a croaking raven, but surely they're ahead
2891  now."
2892  
2893  Mrs. Fairhew had, as the race went on, grown more and more alert. Her
2894  eyes had in them the spark of a genuine lover of sport, and all the
2895  womanly love of contest and conquest showed in the eagerness of her pose
2896  and air.
2897  
2898  "Of course they're ahead," Jerry answered; "but we have the wind of them
2899  by a good deal."
2900  
2901  "I hope that means something," the lady commented, with a movement of
2902  the head half eager, half humorous, "but I confess that it is all Greek
2903  to me."
2904  
2905  Jerry began to explain, but before he could make things clear to the
2906  lady's unnautical mind, the yacht came about again to the port tack.
2907  The Merle was then so far to weather of the yawl that Jack ordered the
2908  sheets to be started a trifle.
2909  
2910  "Now then, Jerry, here's where we overhaul them," Jack cried exultingly.
2911  "Just set the balloon-jib outside the headsails. I think she'll stand
2912  it."
2913  
2914  "Want the staysail?" asked the mate.
2915  
2916  "No--'twould spoil her helm," returned the captain. "Jump along, old
2917  man."
2918  
2919  The change was effected as quickly as might be, and the yacht's speed
2920  was visibly increased.
2921  
2922  "That yawl's better on the wind than off," the captain commented. "We're
2923  picking up on 'em now like smoke."
2924  
2925  After an hour's chase and half an hour's jockeying off the mouth of the
2926  port, the Merle was about to run in when the English yacht luffed up and
2927  crossed the schooner's bows. Both boats were close-hauled, but the
2928  American was on the starboard tack and had the right of way. The
2929  helmsman of the Isis gave Jack his choice of running the yawl down or
2930  luffing himself. Jack chose the latter alternative; although naturally
2931  angry at such an unsportsmanlike trick, he could not take risks with his
2932  uncle's yacht, least of all with the ladies on board. The Englishman did
2933  not spare him, but first blanketed him, and then, putting his helm up
2934  and leaving the Merle with a small ledge frothing to leeward, forced the
2935  schooner about. Under his tan Jack grew white with indignant anger. He
2936  was not the man to lose his temper in his pastimes, but he had a strong
2937  sense of justice, a thorough contempt for trickery, and he was quick to
2938  resent a deliberate outrage of this sort. The performance was so
2939  evidently premeditated on the part of the Isis that it amounted to a
2940  most flagrant insult, a cold-blooded piece of sporting caddishness. The
2941  only remedy possible under the circumstances was a desperate one, but in
2942  his state of mind he did not hesitate.
2943  
2944  "Stand by to jibe!" he roared. "Cast off the topsail halyards! Now aft
2945  on the sheets!"
2946  
2947  It was blowing too hard for jibing with safety even under reduced cloth,
2948  and barring staysail and topsails, the Merle was under full canvas.
2949  
2950  "My God!" exclaimed Jerry to the winds, as he tumbled aft to help on the
2951  sheet, "he'll pull the sticks out of her! Something's bound to go!"
2952  
2953  Jack held the wheel hard up, and the schooner swung steadily off. The
2954  booms rushed over the decks, fetched up with a crash, and then swung out
2955  as the men payed off the sheets. The lee rail went clean under, and for
2956  a second or two unpleasant and portentous creakings and groanings
2957  filled the air. The men flew about with wonderful dexterity, while the
2958  two ladies held on to each other to avoid being pitched headlong.
2959  
2960  "Are any of your teeth shaken out, Katrine?" Mrs. Fairhew inquired, when
2961  they were able once more to sit up. "All mine were loosened by that
2962  awful jerk."
2963  
2964  "They are all safe, Aunt Anne," Katrine cried, her voice vibrant with
2965  delighted excitement. "Isn't it splendid?"
2966  
2967  Her hair was blowing about her face, her eyes were shining, her cheeks
2968  were flushed; and Jack, though his swift glance merely caught a view of
2969  her as it flashed up to the sails, carried the alluring picture in his
2970  mind for many a day. The thought of it was for the time being instantly
2971  crowded out of his mind as he caught sight of the rigging. As the Merle
2972  had leaped ahead, the fore-peak halyards, which had not been started
2973  before the yacht was jibed, had parted. The gaff hung nearly at right
2974  angles to the boom, and the sail was being strained out of shape. The
2975  captain was so upset that in his rage he was guilty of swearing before
2976  ladies.
2977  
2978  "What shall we do?" sang out Jerry.
2979  
2980  Jack's cry had called his attention to the mishap, and he had run
2981  forward.
2982  
2983  "Really this grows exciting," remarked Mrs. Fairhew, as if she were at
2984  the theatre.
2985  
2986  "Oh, what a shame! what a shame!" wailed Katrine, looking despairingly
2987  up at the drooping gaff.
2988  
2989  "Get some half-inch on it!" shouted Jack, almost beside himself at
2990  having been bullied into this predicament. "Take it out as far as you
2991  can! Reeve it through the cap-block first. Move along there! Smartly!"
2992  
2993  "All right!" cried Tab; and in the same moment, with a coil of new rope
2994  over his shoulder, and followed by one of the men, he ran up the weather
2995  rigging.
2996  
2997  On reaching the cross-trees, Tab passed the end of his rope through the
2998  block on the masthead cap and fastened it to his belt. Then he swung
2999  himself down to the jaws of the gaff and lay out along the spar. The big
3000  stick threshed about wildly, threatening to snap him into the sea at
3001  every fling. Slowly and painfully he worked his way out. He clung on
3002  desperately, so that it seemed like a conscious fight between himself
3003  and the plunging spar whether he should be shaken off. It was like a
3004  man's trying to tame a bucking horse, only a hundred times more
3005  exciting, and Katrine grew pale as she watched, while even Mrs. Fairhew
3006  set her lips closely. The three minutes it took Jerry to reach the
3007  peak-halyard block seemed to every person on the Merle all but
3008  interminable. Twice he nearly fell,--once at the outset when he slipped,
3009  and again when he had to crawl around the throat halyards between rolls.
3010  The second time he was actually thrown off the spar, but fortunately he
3011  held his grip on the halyards. The next lurch of the yacht playfully
3012  tossed him into the air, and he was lucky enough to regain his position
3013  on the spar.
3014  
3015  Getting to the peak-block, he unknotted the rope from his belt, passed
3016  it about the spar, and took a "timber-hitch." He then slowly worked his
3017  way back, and eventually reached the cross-trees in safety. The nervous
3018  tension had been so strong that when the men saw him coming down the
3019  ratlines they fell to cheering lustily, Gonzague, his white hair ruffled
3020  by the wind, waving his arms and out-shouting the whole of them. They
3021  speedily got hold of the jury halyard, and even before Jerry had reached
3022  the deck, the gaff was again well raised, and the topsail set.
3023  
3024  In the mean time the Isis had in her turn got into difficulties. It is
3025  poor business jockeying among reefs, and the yawl had been forced to
3026  come about, luff up, and drift sternwards until her chances of beating
3027  the Merle were utterly gone. The fact seemed to be that the English
3028  captain had counted upon the Merle's not daring to jibe, and so had been
3029  too clever by half.
3030  
3031  Jerry came aft, very red in the face, and with the customary twinkle in
3032  his eye. The ladies were evidently greatly impressed by his feat, and
3033  Jack, who of course understood more clearly than they how dangerous the
3034  task had been, took one hand off the wheel and wrung Jerry's.
3035  
3036  "Awfully sorry, old man," he said. "But I was so hot at that Englishman
3037  I lost my head for a minute."
3038  
3039  "Oh, go 'long!" returned Jerry, grinning. "Don't you suppose I was hot
3040  myself?"
3041  
3042  He dropped on to a seat beside Mrs. Fairhew, to recover his breath.
3043  
3044  "Mr. Taberman," said that lady, "I'm an old woman,"--it was one of Mrs.
3045  Fairhew's idiosyncrasies to call attention thus whimsically to the fact
3046  that she looked hardly more than thirty,--"I'm an old woman, and
3047  consequently I disapprove of rashness; but I don't mind saying that I
3048  like your pluck."
3049  
3050  She looked at him in a curious way, as if he were an amusing case of
3051  arrested development, but her glance was full of kindliness.
3052  
3053  "Thank you," Tab answered, with a smile which was too confused not to
3054  be almost a grin. "It's more a sound wind than pluck, I assure you."
3055  
3056  "It was perfectly magnificent!" Katrine cried. "You're a perfect hero!"
3057  
3058  They all laughed, more perhaps from the nervous reaction after the
3059  strain than from any especial amusement, and Jerry blushed more than
3060  ever.
3061  
3062  "I'm afraid you're inclined to make a mountain out of a molehill," he
3063  said. "We don't allow heroics aboard here, you know. Jack did the
3064  only"--
3065  
3066  "That'll do, Jerry," called Jack from the wheel.
3067  
3068  "All right, captain," Tab returned, laughing. "Under orders."
3069  
3070  "Oh, but that's not fair," cried Katrine. "If Mr. Castleport played the
3071  hero too, we want to know all about it."
3072  
3073  "I'll masthead that mate if he goes on talking about his superior
3074  officer," Jack threatened. "See, the Isis has given the whole thing up."
3075  
3076  "She'd better," commented Jerry, "though I don't see that she had
3077  anything left to give."
3078  
3079  The yawl was well astern now. Her sailing-master had for a little time,
3080  in a vain endeavor to overtake his rival, pinched his boat unmercifully,
3081  so that with her nose in the wind's eye her sails were every now and
3082  then a-shiver. Now she had evidently accepted the inevitable, and was
3083  making quietly for an anchorage.
3084  
3085  "Tell us about Mr. Castleport," Katrine said to Jerry in an undertone.
3086  
3087  "Oh," returned Tab, "he stuck to the wheel over forty-eight hours when
3088  we had that blow we were talking about. It was a magnificent thing to
3089  do, and I think he saved us from everlasting smash. Of course he
3090  pooh-poohs the idea, but Jack's never willing to have anybody say he's
3091  done anything big. He's as modest as he is stunning," he ended warmly,
3092  throwing at the captain a glance of admiration and affection.
3093  
3094  Katrine made no audible comment, but her glance followed his, and had
3095  Jack intercepted her look at that moment, he might have felt his heart
3096  beat more briskly.
3097  
3098  The superior speed of the Merle, aided by the poor tactics of the
3099  skipper of the Isis, who seemed to lose his head when he found he was
3100  beaten, gave the American so much the lead that the schooner had dropped
3101  her anchor a minute or two before the yawl rounded the inner mole.
3102  
3103  "I never had so splendid a sail in my life," Katrine said.
3104  
3105  "I was sure you would beat that other boat, Mr. Castleport," Mrs.
3106  Fairhew told him, "and I confess I enjoyed seeing you do it."
3107  
3108  "I couldn't be so rude as to let you ladies be beaten in a race," the
3109  captain responded, laughing.
3110  
3111  "Of course not," put in Jerry; "no gentleman would let a lady be
3112  beaten."
3113  
3114  "What an atrocious pun!" cried Katrine; "and Mr. Taberman looks actually
3115  wistful for fear we shouldn't see it."
3116  
3117  "Well," her aunt said, moving toward the ladder, where the cutter was in
3118  waiting, "it has been a delightful day, and we are greatly obliged."
3119  
3120  While the ladies were being pulled ashore, and before Jack and Jerry had
3121  returned, everything on the Merle was put in order. Just as they went
3122  below to dress for going ashore for dinner, a boat from the yawl came
3123  alongside with a note for the "Captain of the Merle; sch. Y't." Gonzague
3124  brought it to Castleport, who looked at it, and then read it aloud to
3125  Jerry.
3126  
3127  
3128       YAWL YACHT ISIS. Y. S.
3129  
3130       Lord Merryfield presents his compliments to the gentleman who
3131       handled the Merle in such a masterly fashion this afternoon, and
3132       requests the honor of his presence at dinner on board the Isis this
3133       evening at six bells, A. T. It will be an additional pleasure to
3134       Lord Merryfield if the gentleman who so pluckily rose to the
3135       occasion in the matter of a parted halyard will accompany the
3136       captain of the Merle.
3137  
3138       R. S. V. P.
3139  
3140       NICE, July 17, 1902.
3141  
3142  
3143  "Rot!" said Jerry inelegantly. "Let me answer it."
3144  
3145  "Get out!" responded Jack. "I think I can settle him."
3146  
3147  He got out the President's most elaborate stationery, and after some
3148  meditation and the destruction of one or two epistles which would not go
3149  quite to suit him, he handed to Jerry the following:--
3150  
3151  
3152       SCH. YT. MERLE, E. Y. C.
3153  
3154       Captain John Castleport and Mr. Jerrold Taberman present their
3155       compliments to Lord Merryfield and regret that, owing to a previous
3156       engagement, it is impossible for them to accept the invitation so
