3157       kindly tendered to them. Captain Castleport further desires
3158       earnestly to express his opinion in regard to having been forced
3159       about by the Y. Yt. Isis this afternoon when he had the right of
3160       way; and to say that he considers such a manoeuvre so
3161       unsportsmanlike and insulting that it should be impossible in a
3162       gentleman's race. As the injured party, he ventures to remind Lord
3163       Merryfield that the only reparation that can be made is the
3164       severest reprimanding of the sailing-master, or whoever was
3165       responsible for this inexcusable expedient.
3166  
3167       NICE, July 17, 1902.
3168  
3169  
3170  "You see," Jack explained, "we let him know what we think of that
3171  caddish trick without being in the least rude ourselves. Of course the
3172  chances are that he was responsible for the thing himself, and there we
3173  have him on the hip."
3174  
3175  "I suppose it's all right," grumbled Jerry. "You know best; but if I 'd
3176  written it, I should have told him straight out that I thought him a
3177  damned cad!"
3178  
3179  [Illustration: Decoration]
3180  
3181  
3182  
3183  
3184  Chapter Eight
3185  
3186  A CHANGE OF TACTICS
3187  
3188  
3189  As they sat that evening in the garden of the hotel drinking their
3190  after-dinner coffee, which the gentlemen accompanied with cigarettes,
3191  they discussed the news from home contained in a batch of letters Mrs.
3192  Fairhew and her niece had found awaiting them on their return from the
3193  yacht. The announcement of an engagement, rumors of flirtations which
3194  might end in others, the latest gossip about people they all knew, were
3195  mingled with chat about an extraordinary yacht race at Northeast Harbor,
3196  a Russian princess at Nahant, an automobile accident at Lenox, and a
3197  fresh divorce at Newport.
3198  
3199  "Everything else," Mrs. Fairhew said at length, "is simply nothing at
3200  all in comparison to a piece of business news I received. Have you heard
3201  of the Tillington failure?"
3202  
3203  "What!" cried Jack. "R. B. Tillington?"
3204  
3205  "Yes. Their own notice was with the other mail this afternoon," she
3206  responded. "Liabilities something like a third of a million and their
3207  assets nothing."
3208  
3209  "How in the world did it happen?" asked Tab. "I knew they had a lot to
3210  do with mines, and of course those are always risky; but Tillington
3211  always had the name of being awfully clever."
3212  
3213  "Perhaps he was too clever," Jack suggested.
3214  
3215  "Clever or not," Mrs. Fairhew said, "he has come to grief, and, I am
3216  ashamed to confess, he has lost some money for me."
3217  
3218  "I am very sorry for that," Jack responded. "I'll wager you'll have
3219  plenty of distinguished company. I'm awfully afraid Uncle Randolph got
3220  his fingers burned. He's had dealings with Tillington for ever so long.
3221  I never took kindly to the man myself, but Uncle Randolph had a great
3222  opinion of his business sagacity."
3223  
3224  "I'll wager Mrs. Fairhew's bound to be in good company even in
3225  misfortune," Jerry declared with his usual somewhat clumsy gallantry.
3226  
3227  Mrs. Fairhew smiled, and made a little sweeping gesture with her fan as
3228  if the subject were a disagreeable one and should be waved aside.
3229  
3230  "Even that," she said, "doesn't soothe my wounded vanity. The money I've
3231  lost is fortunately not very much, but I pride myself on my business
3232  head, and I made this investment in spite of the advice of my banker.
3233  Think how he will chuckle! I'd rather have lost three times as much on
3234  an investment he selected."
3235  
3236  "How thoroughly feminine!" Jack laughed.
3237  
3238  "Of course you can't understand," Katrine struck in. "I agree with Aunt
3239  Anne entirely. Of course one would rather lose money than to give a man
3240  a chance to crow over her."
3241  
3242  The talk was thus drawn into the inexhaustible discussion of feminine
3243  and masculine characteristics, that topic about which revolves two
3244  thirds of all the small talk of the world. Then it drifted back to the
3245  personal news of the letters.
3246  
3247  "I don't think Billy Rafton's to be congratulated," announced Tab
3248  emphatically, in reference to a recent wedding. "Edna Leighton has
3249  plenty of money of course, and is a stunning girl and all that; but
3250  she's so horribly ambitious that she won't give poor Billy a minute's
3251  peace."
3252  
3253  "And Billy is one of the most quiet men alive," put in Jack.
3254  
3255  "Ambitious?" queried Katrine. "How? I've known her pretty well, and to
3256  me she always seemed nice. Certainly she's clever."
3257  
3258  "So she is clever," Jerry assented; "but of course that'll make it
3259  harder for Billy to stand out against her."
3260  
3261  "She naturally would have the instinct to get ahead in the world,"
3262  commented Castleport. "Her mother was a Farquhar."
3263  
3264  "Mr. Castleport," remonstrated Mrs. Fairhew, "that remark is too
3265  feminine to be worthy of you."
3266  
3267  "Do you regret that I didn't leave it for you to say?" he asked saucily.
3268  "I know you entirely agree with me."
3269  
3270  "Her father, Stephen Leighton," Mrs. Fairhew continued, making no answer
3271  but a hardly perceptible smile to his statement, "was a thoroughly
3272  charming man and of very good family. You can't deny that, Mr.
3273  Castleport."
3274  
3275  "I haven't any wish to. I'm not trying to run down Edna
3276  Leighton--Rafton, that is."
3277  
3278  "I always thought," began Katrine. Then she stopped, with an involuntary
3279  movement of the eyes in the direction of Taberman.
3280  
3281  "Oh, I was hit there once," Tab said jovially, "if that's what you mean.
3282  I got over it at a boat race."
3283  
3284  They all laughed, and the topic seemed exhausted, when the elder lady
3285  said:--
3286  
3287  "We shall have sight of them at Florence, I suppose. They are to be at
3288  the Villa Foscagni for the summer. It belongs to the Raftons."
3289  
3290  "When do you expect to get there?" Tab inquired carelessly.
3291  
3292  "Florence? In five or six days."
3293  
3294  "Five or six days!" cried Jack. "Why, when do you leave here?"
3295  
3296  "To-morrow afternoon," answered Katrine in a tone of which the
3297  indifference might have struck Jack as a little overdone had he not been
3298  too perturbed to notice.
3299  
3300  "Why--but--" Jack began; "I had no idea"--
3301  
3302  "Did you fancy we were here for the summer?" queried Katrine with demure
3303  interest.
3304  
3305  The hint of teasing in her tone brought Castleport to himself. Half his
3306  social success lay in the fact that he was not easily disconcerted.
3307  
3308  "As Mrs. Fairhew was good enough to tell me her plans," he returned
3309  coolly, "I naturally understood that you were to leave here before long,
3310  but I admit I hadn't thought you would go so soon."
3311  
3312  "You see," Mrs. Fairhew explained, "we really must get on. Katrine has
3313  to do museums and things, as I told you. When I was a girl it wouldn't
3314  have been thought respectable for a girl to come out before she'd seen
3315  the Pitti and Uffizzi; but it's all different now."
3316  
3317  "What nonsense, Aunt Anne! I don't believe you'd seen the galleries
3318  yourself when you came out."
3319  
3320  "Indeed I had. I'll make you read all the finest print in the
3321  guide-books if you are impertinent. We take," she added, turning to
3322  Castleport, "the 3.08 for Genoa."
3323  
3324  Jack was by nature quick and resolute; and before Mrs. Fairhew had got
3325  to this remark he had conceived a plan, and resolved to follow it out.
3326  Gravely regarding the thicket of oleanders behind Miss Marchfield, yet
3327  with the tail of his eye on the face of Jerry, which was alternately
3328  lighted and obscured as his cigarette glowed or waned, the captain
3329  remarked coolly:--
3330  
3331  "That's a curious coincidence."
3332  
3333  "Coincidence?" repeated Mrs. Fairhew questioningly.
3334  
3335  "It would seem so," Jack almost drawled. "You said the 3.08, didn't you?
3336  How far do you go? All the way to Genoa?"
3337  
3338  "Yes. What is there extraordinary about that?"
3339  
3340  "Why, nothing much," returned Jack in a brisker tone, throwing away the
3341  butt of his cigarette; "only--yes--that's the very train I go on
3342  myself. Same destination, too, unless I decide to stop at Bordighera."
3343  
3344  There naturally was a sensation at this unexpected announcement. Katrine
3345  drew in her breath audibly; in the very nick of time Jerry caught
3346  himself in the act of saying profanely what he would be; Mrs. Fairhew
3347  closed her fan quickly, but she was too much mistress of herself to give
3348  any indication of her feelings beyond a little quick laugh.
3349  
3350  "I had not remembered that you spoke of going," she said.
3351  
3352  "No?" Jack said politely.
3353  
3354  "But," gasped Jerry, "I say--you know, I say"--
3355  
3356  Evidently his feelings were too much for him, and he collapsed. So
3357  sudden a move on the part of Jack was sure to disconcert his
3358  slower-witted comrade, and the captain had fortunately been prepared by
3359  previous experiences for some mental confusion on the part of the mate.
3360  
3361  "Yes, Jerry?" he asked.
3362  
3363  "Nothing--I--I don't remember what I was going to say," murmured the
3364  bewildered Tab.
3365  
3366  "Really," observed Mrs. Fairhew, "it hadn't occurred to me that you
3367  could or would leave the yacht. What becomes of her?"
3368  
3369  "Oh, you don't doubt Jerry, do you? He's going to take her in charge."
3370  
3371  Once determined upon his plan, Jack felt it best to carry matters off
3372  with a high hand. He did not in the least care whether Mrs. Fairhew and
3373  Katrine suspected that his resolution to go on by land had been taken on
3374  the spot or not; but he liked to play the game well, and to put a good
3375  face on things. He spoke as though his mind had been made up long
3376  before, although all the time his brain was working with furious energy,
3377  as he tried to shape the scheme thoroughly and to foresee all possible
3378  contingencies. To give over to Jerry the care of the President's yacht
3379  was a bold stroke, but he said to himself that he was confident his
3380  friend was entirely competent to manage her for the comparatively short
3381  run to Naples; and his thought nimbly disposed of objection after
3382  objection as they rose in his mind.
3383  
3384  Rapid as had been his decision, it was less wild than it might seem; and
3385  by the time he spoke again Jack had all the details pretty well
3386  mastered.
3387  
3388  "Do you leave the Merle here?" inquired Mrs. Fairhew.
3389  
3390  Katrine, Jack noted, had said nothing, but he had heard that quick,
3391  indrawn breath, and he did not believe that her silence arose from
3392  indifference.
3393  
3394  "Oh, no; Jerry's going to take her to Naples," was Castleport's cool
3395  reply.
3396  
3397  It was to Tab's credit that at this astounding piece of intelligence he
3398  did not make a violent demonstration; but he was not unaccustomed to the
3399  rapidity with which Jack came to a decision, and he had before been
3400  trained in accepting what his captain said. Now he only dropped his
3401  cigarette, and on picking it up put the lighted end between his lips,
3402  spluttered and smothered a profane comment, and hurled the offending
3403  butt as far as he could.
3404  
3405  "Have another?" asked Jack, unruffled, as he pushed his case across the
3406  little table by which they were sitting.
3407  
3408  "Thank you, no!" replied Tab with quite unnecessary emphasis.
3409  
3410  "You've no need to touch your lips with fire, Mr. Taberman," Mrs.
3411  Fairhew observed, opening and closing her fan in a way which she had
3412  when amused; "you have been sufficiently eloquent in compliments ever
3413  since you arrived. May we hope, then," she went on, turning to
3414  Castleport, "for the pleasure of your company on the journey?"
3415  
3416  "If you and Miss Marchfield do not object, I shall be delighted."
3417  
3418  "It will be a great pleasure to me. Of course I can't speak for
3419  Katrine."
3420  
3421  Jack turned to look at Katrine. On her face the soft light of a Japanese
3422  lantern fell between a couple of trees, but she at once moved so that
3423  the shadows hid her expression.
3424  
3425  "Nothing could please me more, Aunt Anne, than that you should be
3426  pleased," she responded.
3427  
3428  "Then you had better bring Mr. Taberman and your luggage ashore, and
3429  come to luncheon to-morrow," the aunt said, rising. "In that way we can
3430  take our time and be comfortable. Does that suit your plans, Mr.
3431  Castleport?"
3432  
3433  Jack detected the suspicion of mirth in her voice, but he felt that if
3434  she had disapproved she would not only have shown no amusement but that
3435  she was clever enough to have thwarted his scheme.
3436  
3437  "I don't want to abuse your hospitality," he said.
3438  
3439  "Oh, we shall make you useful as an escort, and get enough service out
3440  of you on the journey to pay that," spoke Katrine, with the air of
3441  feeling that she had been too noticeably silent.
3442  
3443  "We're only too delighted to come, of course," Jerry said with boyish
3444  enthusiasm. "Anybody'd be glad of a chance to lunch with you, Mrs.
3445  Fairhew."
3446  
3447  "Your compliments are rather direct, Mr. Taberman," that lady answered
3448  with a laugh. "We'll say 1.30, then. That will give us plenty of time. I
3449  hate to be hurried; it is so undignified."
3450  
3451  As Mrs. Fairhew had risen the others were of course on their feet, and
3452  as Jack stood aside for Katrine to pass him, the elder lady took his
3453  arm. By this she detained him an instant, until her niece and Jerry were
3454  a few yards away. When they approached the door of the hotel and it was
3455  light enough for him to see her clearly, she dropped his arm; and as he
3456  turned his face toward her at the movement, she regarded him through her
3457  lorgnette with a look quizzical though kindly.
3458  
3459  "You are a clever boy," she said after a little, and with a peculiar
3460  faint stress on the adjective. "Do you want to marry my niece?"
3461  
3462  Jack of course recognized that the question would never have been asked
3463  had there been any doubt of the answer, and even in the confusion of the
3464  moment he had a dim perception that Mrs. Fairhew was, with kindly whim,
3465  helping him to ask her sanction to his wooing. He felt his cheeks grow
3466  hot, but he faced his inquisitor frankly, and he spoke with a manner
3467  which though instinctively subdued was full of energy and feeling.
3468  
3469  "You know I do," he said. "You know I'd die the worst of deaths for
3470  her. I--As God's above me," he burst out, breaking off and feeling
3471  himself strangle with his emotion, "I'll win her or die trying! I--I--
3472  Of course I want to marry her! What do you suppose I came to Europe
3473  for?"
3474  
3475  Mrs. Fairhew's face softened, for no true woman could have heard the
3476  passion of his voice unmoved; but she laughed at the sudden change with
3477  which he ended.
3478  
3479  "I hope you may succeed," she said softly. "I think you will." Then she
3480  took his arm again, and spoke in her ordinary voice: "Come, we must go
3481  in."
3482  
3483  
3484  "Now, then, Jack, in the name of heaven," demanded Jerry, as soon as he
3485  and the captain were out of hearing of the ladies, "what is this awful
3486  josh of yours about leaving the yacht?"
3487  
3488  "I'll tell you when we get aboard," his friend answered. "Don't bother
3489  me now; I'm thinking."
3490  
3491  Tab snorted contemptuously, and in silence the pair held on until they
3492  reached the quay. The cutter awaited them, and still in silence they
3493  were pulled out to the Merle. There was not a breath of wind now; the
3494  stars blazed brilliantly above them, and not a cloud-blot was to be
3495  seen. In a stillness broken only by the rhythmical oar-strokes the pair
3496  watched the myriad star-points which dotted the heavens as they had
3497  adorned it centuries before when old Nice was new Nicæa, and some brown
3498  Sicilian pilot may have gazed up at them and made haven by their
3499  faithful guidance.
3500  
3501  No sooner were they aboard than Gonzague came to ask if they would have
3502  supper.
3503  
3504  "Oh, I don't know," Jack answered, still in a dream from the spell of
3505  Mrs. Fairhew's words.
3506  
3507  "Well, I do," put in Jerry. "We'll have some caviare sandwiches,
3508  Gonzague, and a glass of sherry."
3509  
3510  The supper was eaten almost in silence, and it was not until Gonzague
3511  had taken away the things and left them with pipes lighted that the
3512  inevitable explanation was reached.
3513  
3514  "Now then?" said Tab impatiently.
3515  
3516  His face wore a sober expression, full of expectancy, but not without a
3517  hint of annoyance and reproach. Jack blew a large smoke-ring at him, and
3518  laughed to see how in dodging it Jerry kept his solemnity unchanged.
3519  
3520  "Well, Tab," he began, "I don't suppose it's necessary to say that the
3521  idea of leaving the yacht never came into my head till I knew Mrs.
3522  Fairhew and Katr--Miss Marchfield were off to-morrow."
3523  
3524  "Heave ahead," grumpily retorted Jerry. "Don't mind me. Of course I
3525  shall be delighted to be left alone on the yacht."
3526  
3527  "Come, cheer up, old man," Jack exhorted. "Don't be grouchy. I'm awfully
3528  sorry to leave you; but of course it's only for a little while, and we
3529  shall both have compensations. I hope I shall be coming nearer
3530  to--to--well, to something definite, you know; and you'll have the Merle
3531  to do what you jolly well please with."
3532  
3533  "That's all very well, of course," Tab responded, his face relaxing a
3534  little; "but what's your game? We've beastly little money, you know; and
3535  this shore cruise of yours is bound to sop up a lot of tin."
3536  
3537  "We've money enough to carry us through," Jack declared. "I'll go to
3538  Genoa, of course. I know Italy pretty well, and I can make myself
3539  useful,--sort of 'guide, philosopher, and friend,' and courier all in
3540  one. When they go on to Naples,--well, from something Mrs. Fairhew said
3541  to-night, I think I shan't have any difficulty going on to Naples with
3542  them. A man's a handy article in traveling, you see, especially if he
3543  knows the language."
3544  
3545  Jerry regarded the captain as if his slower wits found it somewhat hard
3546  to follow the swift flights of his friend's mind.
3547  
3548  "But the Merle?" he objected. "It's bad enough for you to be skylarking
3549  about the world with the President's yacht, but when it comes to turning
3550  it over to me--Why, the old gentleman would throw five hundred fits at
3551  the bare idea."
3552  
3553  "Oh, I'll trust you there," Jack said lightly, consciously trying to
3554  make his confidence as flattering as possible. "You can manage, and do
3555  as you please for the next month. Who ever heard of a mate that didn't
3556  jump at the chance of taking command for a while. I'd advise you to
3557  stop, say, at Elba, if you're for doing the sights. Then, if you like,
3558  while you're on the Napoleonic tack, you might run 'round to Ajaccio.
3559  It's an out-of-the-way place, rather, but it's jolly when you get there.
3560  As for Elba, I've never been ashore there, though I've passed it and
3561  know the chap that owns it. I'll give you a letter in case you want to
3562  go ashore."
3563  
3564  "But, Jack--Damn it!" broke out Jerry, as if exasperated by the very
3565  feasibility of his friend's sudden change of tactics, "I can't speak a
3566  word of their blessed lingo!"
3567  
3568  "Pooh! Your French will carry you about well enough, and if worst comes
3569  to worst, you can fall back on Gonzague. At Naples you'll find them
3570  speaking English all over the lot."
3571  
3572  "Jack Castleport, you're certainly the damnedest man to handle I ever
3573  came across," Jerry said in despairing tones. "A fellow might as well
3574  try to bully-rag a sea-cow as to argue you out of any of your confounded
3575  schemes."
3576  
3577  "That's because they're so good," laughed Jack. "You see their profound
3578  wisdom carries me away so completely that objections can't touch me."
3579  Then he stretched his hand across the table corner, and caught hold of
3580  Jerry's. "I'm deuced sorry to give you the slip like this," he said,
3581  "but you know the reason."
3582  
3583  The good-natured Tab melted at once. He returned the pressure of his
3584  friend's hand and tried to quote
3585  
3586  
3587       "But when a woman's in the case,
3588       All other things, you know, give place;"
3589  
3590  
3591  but made so hopeless a mess of it that he could only break out into one
3592  of his boisterously jovial guffaws.
3593  
3594  "Well, by George," he cried, "if she only knew how devoted you are,
3595  Jack, she'd let you wait a dog's age, just to try you."
3596  
3597  They spent an hour or so in arranging details, going over charts,
3598  dividing their funds, and so on. Jack gave Tab addresses at Genoa,
3599  Florence, and Rome by which he might be reached, and told him that at
3600  Naples he should go to the Hôtel du Vesuve. On the twentieth of August
3601  Jerry was to inquire for him there. These and other affairs having been
3602  arranged, the pair smoked a final pipe, and turned in.
3603  
3604  Jack was very wakeful. He lay thinking of this and of that, restlessly
3605  tossing about in his berth. Just as at last he was dropping off to
3606  sleep, he was aroused by the voice of Jerry, who called softly across
3607  the passage:--
3608  
3609  "I say, Jack,--are you awake?"
3610  
3611  "Almost," replied Jack; "but I shouldn't have been, if you'd let me
3612  alone."
3613  
3614  "I say, Jacko, do you fancy the President came a cropper in that
3615  Tillington smashup?"
3616  
3617  "Don't know," Jack answered. "He's pretty shrewd, and Mrs. Fairhew would
3618  have been likely to hear of it, I should think, if he had come seriously
3619  to grief."
3620  
3621  "Well, you know, it struck me that perhaps that beastly letter from
3622  Tillington might have been something important, and"--
3623  
3624  "Oh, take a liver-pill!" interrupted Jack. "You've got an attack of
3625  _Conscientia Novanglicana_."
3626  
3627  "What's that?"
3628  
3629  "Forerunner of nervous pros.," replied the captain with a chuckle. "Go
3630  to sleep or you'll get it."
3631  
3632  "Well, good-night."
3633  
3634  "Good-night, boy."
3635  
3636  Silence again reigned, but Jack, once more aroused, threshed about
3637  uneasily until far into the night. Resolutely as he might determine not
3638  to think of the possible consequences of the carrying off of that big
3639  blue letter, he could not prevent doubt from recurring constantly to his
3640  mind, and something not so far removed from remorse mingled with his
3641  thoughts of Katrine and of the delight of traveling in her company. He
3642  was so long awake that on the next afternoon Mrs. Fairhew, when he had
3643  installed her and her niece comfortably in a first-class compartment on
3644  the 3.08 train, and they were beginning to see the olive groves and the
3645  villas slip picturesquely past the windows, noted the shadows beneath
3646  his eyes, and smiled to herself discreetly and unseen.
3647  
3648  [Illustration: Decoration]
3649  
3650  
3651  
3652  
3653  Chapter Nine
3654  
3655  THE DOLDRUMS
3656  
3657  
3658  For two weeks the Merle had been lying at anchor at Naples. From Nice
3659  she had run first to Elba; thence she had doubled north again and
3660  rounded Corsica; she had touched at Calvi and Ajaccio; and lastly,
3661  running through the Straits of Bonifacio, she had held on
3662  east-southeasterly to her present anchorage off the Castle.
3663  
3664  Despite the novel pleasures of command, Taberman felt Jack's absence so
3665  much as at times to be almost unhappy, even at times a little inclined
3666  to be resentful. He was still too boyish not to feel that to leave a
3667  yacht for a girl was the height of madness, if not of idiocy; and while
3668  he was too loyal to Jack to confess this feeling even to himself, it
3669  would at times rise in his mind, especially when he felt more than
3670  usually lonely. On his arrival at any port Jerry experienced to the full
3671  the excitement which even the oldest traveler feels in some degree at
3672  entering a new town. Whenever the port officer appeared in his official
3673  dignity, another sensation was added in the fear of detection and
3674  apprehension. A reaction would set in with the departure of the easily
3675  satisfied official, and Jerry would go mooning about with his hands in
3676  his pockets, whistling some spiritless tune until the time came to get
3677  up anchor and sail anew.
3678  
3679  At Naples, however, things went somewhat better with Jerry than at any
3680  of his previous ports. In the first place even Jerry, unæsthetic as he
3681  was, could not escape the magic of the beautiful bay and the
3682  surroundings which opened up before him in the morning light as he
3683  approached the city. He said to himself, half as if in excuse for being
3684  so much pleased by mere scenery, that it looked as it should. It had, as
3685  it were, kept faith with him; and its beauty was to him an honest
3686  fulfillment of its fame. The gray cone of Vesuvius, palpably and
3687  gratifyingly like the pictures, stood at the head of the bay, crowned
3688  with an inky cloud of smoke. Away from it to the south stretched the
3689  cliffs of blue Sorrento and bluer Capri, melting magically into a
3690  background of hills or of the azure sky. On the north of the smoking
3691  cone a stretch of shadow-wrought shore, and then Naples itself, from the
3692  old Spanish fort on the water-front to the Castle of St. Elmo, long and
3693  gray, crowning the summit of the ridge behind, and the stone-pines
3694  silhouetted like palms against the sapphire sky. Naples, with its great
3695  four-square houses of pink, and white, and yellow, heaped, as it were,
3696  one above another; its red-tiled roofs, its terraces tricked out with
3697  vines or fig-trees; Naples, with its church roofs of variegated tiles,
3698  its long quays yellowish gray about the shore--Jerry could well have
3699  believed himself in some enchanted picture city, a city which might
3700  almost be expected to vanish suddenly if one should close the book it
3701  graced.
3702  
3703  Behind the Government Mole were lying five Italian battleships, their
3704  big red, white, and green flags floating over their sterns, and
3705  everywhere over the liquid blue of the bay sailed fisher-craft and small
3706  boats, gilded with the morning light.
3707  
3708  Scarcely was the Merle's anchor down than the yacht was surrounded by a
3709  gay flotilla of boats, all laden with piles of fruit or vegetables, and
3710  manned by crews as noisy as they were picturesque. Baskets heaped with
3711  figs, great piles of green melons, lemons, citrons, plums, fresh
3712  vegetables of all sorts, were there; and each ware was extolled by the
3713  vendors with vociferous volubility, until the ears of Jerry fairly sang
3714  with the din. From the crowding boats screamed blowsy, dark-eyed women
3715  with brown oval faces and raiment of reds and yellows; boys with Greek
3716  faces and slim bare arms yelled with shrill voices; doddering old men,
3717  sitting in the stern-sheets of skiffs pulled by impish youngsters, waved
3718  impotent hands and moved toothless mouths whose sounds were lost in the
3719  feverish uproar; stalwart market-men, with brown, wrinkled faces and
3720  hairy bosoms exposed, fought their way through the press, disregarding
3721  age, sex, and condition in their effort to be nearest the possible
3722  purchasers on the Merle; all around the yacht the piratical
3723  water-peddlers made a floating Pandemonium, at which the Yankee crew
3724  stared not only in surprise but with some appearance of not unnatural
3725  alarm.
3726  
3727  As an opposing bulwark to this flood of southern vivacity, old Gonzague
3728  alone stood as the spokesman of the yacht. Requested by Jerry to make
3729  the vendors "stow their jaw," he laid about him right and left with a
3730  profane volubility which outdid even that of the assailants. The old man
3731  had not spoken Italian for so long that he might well be supposed to
3732  have forgotten it, but the occasion found him splendidly adequate to all
3733  the requirements of the situation. The Neapolitans raved and pleaded,
3734  execrated and lowered their prices, with appeals to the Madonna and all
3735  the saints to witness their honesty and their liberality; but once the
3736  floodgates of Gonzague's Italian were opened, he dealt with them so
3737  eloquently and so roundly, his objurgations were so much more
3738  picturesque and more emphatic than any they could compass, that one by
3739  one they drew away baffled, calling on high Heaven and the blessed
3740  Virgin to protect them when Vesuvius should belch forth a torrent of
3741  fire to overwhelm this blasphemous and impious _vecchiastro_.
3742  
3743  Gonzague was perhaps sustained under the volleys of curses which the
3744  defeated bumboat men and women threw back at him, by the admiration with
3745  which he was regarded by the crew of the Merle. They had come to idolize
3746  the old man, and to look upon him with roughly affectionate wonder. The
3747  beauty of the scenes through which they had been passing in the
3748  Mediterranean had of course impressed them very little æsthetically, and
3749  Naples with its matchless bay they saw only with the eyes of Isle au
3750  Haut fishermen. They were, however, never tired of wonders. The
3751  childlike sailor nature is always easily touched by the marvelous, and a
3752  real volcano was something worth seeing. As long as the Merle was in
3753  sight of Vesuvius they would hang over the rail and watch it for hours.
3754  If the smoke ceased they would cluster together and discuss the
3755  probable causes; they would talk of the mountain as if it were a
3756  conscious monster, lying in wait for prey, whose every movement was to
3757  be watched with a view to detecting the sinister design that must lie
3758  behind it. When a great dun cloud would suddenly puff up from the cone,
3759  the men would greet it with deep exclamations half of awe and half of
3760  applause. Continually they beset Gonzague with questions, as if he were
3761  the keeper or the high priest of this fiery monster. They apparently had
3762  complete confidence that Gonzague could explain it all if he would. His
3763  knowledge of the language and such use of it as he made in dispersing
3764  the voluble rabble of vendors were exactly in the line of their
3765  understanding, and they followed his every movement with an admiration
3766  amusingly tinged with something not unlike uncouth reverence.
3767  
3768  On the afternoon of his arrival at Naples Taberman had gone ashore. He
3769  had landed at the steamship quay, and passed half the night in an
3770  aimless ramble. There is something about Naples at night which goes to
3771  the head like wine; especially if the head is young and set on the
3772  shoulders of one who has never before known the life of southern cities.
3773  Jerry walked from the railroad station to the Public Gardens, and from
3774  the Mola to the Hôtel Britannique upon the heights. He attempted no
3775  systematic exploration, but simply wandered with no other object than
3776  the simple delight of rambling. By daylight the picturesque streets; the
3777  variegated rabble, ragged, dirty, beautiful, impudent, at once repulsive
3778  and enchanting; the crooked, crowded ways that climb the hill; the
3779  awnings, the heaps of fruit, the strange wares, the familiar air of the
3780  family life which made of the streets a home, and seemed to turn all the
3781  inhabitants of the town into one huge family; the unconsciously artistic
3782  groups, the tumbling _bambini_, the women, bold, piquant, handsome, or
3783  ugly with a hideousness of which Jerry had never conceived,--all these
3784  things passed before him like the whirling shows of an opium dream. As
3785  night fell, and the lights appeared, the scenes through which he went
3786  half dazed and wholly delighted took on a new quality of the weird and
3787  fantastic. The flaring lamps, the mysterious shadows, the blazing colors
3788  which not even the night could subdue, the theatrical effects seen down
3789  the narrow streets as on a stage set for opera, the inexhaustible
3790  vivacity, which seemed not to diminish with the lateness of the hour,
3791  all blended in an intoxicating experience such as Taberman had never
3792  known, and indeed such as had never come into his liveliest fancy.
3793  
3794  The next day Jerry went ashore in the morning, and set himself to more
3795  regular sight-seeing under the care of a professional guide. He went
3796  over the famous Museum, saw Vergil's Tomb, Posilipo, Sanazar's house,
3797  and Marti's _pozzo_. After a capital luncheon in one of the cafés in the
3798  Arcade, he rejoined his guide, who took him to the Aquarium. On the way
3799  they stopped at the Royal Palace and the Morro, Tab being duly impressed
3800  by the grandeur of royalty and the majesty of the law. Continually he
3801  wished that Jack were with him, for he had so fallen into the habit of
3802  depending on Jack for opinions that without his friend his impressions
3803  seemed to lack the clearness of sanction. When it came to the Aquarium,
3804  however, not only did the things he had seen in his day's explorations
3805  fade from his mind, but he was too delighted not to know exactly what he
3806  felt.
3807  
3808  The Aquarium of Naples is by far the most wonderful in the world. It is
3809  smaller and less elaborate than others, as, for instance, that of the
3810  Trocadero, but it outranks all in interest and impressiveness. The
3811  virtue of the place lies in its simplicity of construction and in the
3812  rarity of its exhibits. A sense of restful shadow and coolness
3813  succeeding to outside glare and heat; a dim greenish light in broad,
3814  glass-faced tanks of sea-water; an odd feeling of being fathoms deep in
3815  a tropical sea,--these are the sensations the visitor has first in this
3816  wonderful home of strange fish in exile.
3817  
3818  Tab made the rounds half a dozen times before he could bring himself to
3819  leave. Quite unscientific, but as enthusiastic as a boy, he stood in
3820  front of each tank, and tried vainly to determine which was most
3821  fascinating. Here were spiny lobster-like crustacea, spotted with a
3822  dozen colors; there were beautiful fish with shining iridescent sides
3823  and waving filmy, vaporous tails; one tank was inhabited by repulsive,
3824  warty octopi, splotched with dull browns and plague-spots of ugly red,
3825  which melted and slimed about, so disgusting that they seemed almost
3826  obscene; from another a huge sea python, with body as large as the thigh
3827  of a man and a head like that of a bald wolf, seemed to grin with
3828  sinister, snarling face at Jerry, while all about the monster bloated
3829  globe-fish and distorted marine shapes swam and circled; in a corner
3830  tank a brood of asp-like fish, with skins that seemed of richest velvet,
3831  dusky and wonderful in hue, lay heaped like incarnate poison; and near
3832  by the angel-fish went waving and trailing their way about the sand.
3833  Jerry was perhaps most impressed, however, by the mysterious life which
3834  went on in a tank to which he came among the last. Thin, slow-waving
3835  filaments of colorless jelly, crowned with diaphanous cups, not
3836  differing greatly from the poppy-flower in shape; and near them other
3837  forms, transparent, hardly more than condensed sea-water in appearance,
3838  yet with slow pulsations, continuous and wonderful, of phosphoric
3839  sparks,--as if one saw life itself throbbing rhythmically in the
3840  pellucid hairs of jelly.
3841  
3842  Jerry had not been so completely happy since he parted from Jack. He
3843  reveled in a boyish delight, and let no wonder of the place escape him.
3844  He tipped the keeper to feed the octopi with young crabs, lowered on a
3845  string; he took a smart electric shock from a morose torpedo which lay
3846  sulkily in a small open tub with a pebbly bottom; he had the big
3847  anemones and the coral-polyps "put to sleep," in the words of his
3848  guide,--an operation consisting simply of the moving in the water of a
3849  small stick which caused them to close in alarm; he did, in a word,
3850  everything his guide could think of for him to do, and went away in the
3851  end only half content to leave.
3852  
3853  After the Aquarium, Jerry turned a deaf ear to the alluring speeches of
3854  the guide, the burden of whose song was all of curiosities unseen and of
3855  pleasures untasted. He paid the importunate manikin, and made his way
3856  back to the Merle. The truth was that he had seen something which
3857  thoroughly pleased him, and after that it was impossible to return to
3858  the perfunctory seeing of regulation sights which really did not take
3859  hold of him in the least.
3860  
3861  Before the first week was ended, Jerry had visited Pompeii and Baiæ, and
3862  what was to be seen of Herculaneum. He had made some purchases; and then
3863  he began to wait about, ashore or aboard, for Jack. That gentleman had
3864  written no response to Tab's letter announcing the arrival of the Merle
3865  at Naples, and Jerry could only think of him as so absorbed in his
3866  wooing as to have forgotten all about his friend. Some not unnatural
3867  jealousy began to ferment in his mind, and did not add to his comfort.
3868  By the advice of Gonzague he took the market-boat, and setting out early
3869  one morning he sailed with a couple of the men across the bay to Capri,
3870  where he passed the day. The only thing which cheered him on his lonely
3871  expedition was a tarantella, which was danced for his diversion by a
3872  romantic-looking _raggaza_, with black eyes and short petticoats. The
3873  moonlight sail back would have pleased him more had it not been
3874  necessary to keep the men rowing for two thirds of the way. On the
3875  whole, Jerry could find nothing to please him on land or sea.
3876  
3877  The major part of the next week he had spent stretched out in a cane
3878  _chaise longue_ in the cockpit, drinking iced sangaree and reading
3879  Didron's _Artémise_. He had a fly stretched over the awning for
3880  increased coolness, and the "dusters" put up to shut out the glare from
3881  the water; there, like some melancholy monarch beneath his canopy, he
3882  read, dozed, and grumbled--without even the satisfaction of any fit
3883  audience--from morning to sundown.
3884  
3885  In the cool of the evening he usually went ashore, and one night he was
3886  strolling along the water-front, stick in hand and his Panama set well
3887  back on his head. As he passed the Hôtel du Vesuve, wondering when Jack
3888  would arrive, a small figure moved quickly in front of him and bowed. At
3889  first he was startled, but almost instantly he saw that it was the valet
3890  de place who had gone about with him in the early days of his stay at
3891  Naples.
3892  
3893  "Hello," said Jerry in surprise, yet not without a feeling of
3894  satisfaction at finding even this apology for a companion.
3895  
3896  "_Buon' sera, signor_," responded the little man vivaciously. "How do?
3897  You tek-a de night air? _É verament' un' bellissima notte._ It mek-a
3898  cool, eh?"
3899  
3900  And he waved his arms expressively.
3901  
3902  He might have been thirty or thirty-five, and had coarse black hair,
3903  with fiery eyes. He was not ill-looking, but his clothes were hopelessly
3904  threadbare and his face pinched. He bore dark circles under his eyes,
3905  and was in no way markedly different from others of his numerous and
3906  futile class, who, with a smattering of French, German, or English,
3907  struggle desperately for a livelihood by acting, not always very
3908  virtuously, as guides for traveling _forestieri_.
3909  
3910  "You busy?" Jerry asked, a sudden thought striking him.
3911  
3912  "No--no," replied the Neapolitan, his face as eager as his tone. "What-a
3913  you like see? Eh? Some of dose oder curiosities _forse_?" he asked with
3914  a suggestive smile.
3915  
3916  "Thanks, no," Jerry returned dryly; "but if you aren't busy, I wish
3917  you'd walk along with me. I'm bored--tired--'most to death, and I fancy
3918  you might tell me how I may best kill time for the next few days."
3919  
3920  The little guide was delighted. He suggested a multitude of things which
3921  might be done,--visits to Castellmare and Sorrento or Amalfi; wonders
3922  the signor had neglected in the museum; the _pasta_ shops; and so on for
3923  a variety of possible and impossible diversions. But still Taberman
3924  shook his head. He wanted to be amused, but he was lonely and rather
3925  homesick, so that while he regretted being so difficult, nothing
3926  appealed to him. Finally, the guide, quite at his wit's end but still
3927  bland, smiling, patient, obsequious, and apparently unruffled by the
3928  careless way in which the American rejected all his suggestions one
3929  after the other, mentioned Pesto.
3930  
3931  "Pesto?" queried Tab carelessly. "What is that?"
3932  
3933  "_Si!_ Pesto. It ees dere dey hav-a de gret-a temple; t'ree gret-a
3934  temple, all put een de row-a,--_uno, due, tre_." And he held up three
3935  fingers to make his statement at once clearer and more emphatic.
3936  
3937  "Temples? Real ones?" asked Jerry. "I mean are they old--Roman, that
3938  is--or just churches?"
3939  
3940  "_Ma verament'_," laughed the valet de place, "_ci son' tre templi_;
3941  bot-a dey not-a Roman; dey Gre'k. Fin-a, big-a temple; big-a like Hôtel
3942  du Vesuve!"
3943  
3944  He waved his spread arms as if he would embrace the universe. Jerry
3945  laughed at the little man's enthusiasm, but his interest was excited.
3946  
3947  "Greek, eh?" he said. "How far is it? How do you get there?"
3948  
3949  The guide explained volubly, told the time of trains to Pæstum,
3950  declared that the trip was easily made in a day, and proffered his
3951  services as escort. This Jerry declined, quite as much from motives of
3952  economy as from any other reason; but he invited the little guide to sit
3953  down at one of the small tables on the sidewalk before Zinfoni's, where
3954  he furnished him with refreshments and made him repeat his account of
3955  the temples, the details of the journey, and whatever information he
3956  could furnish. Jerry was really lonely enough to be amused by the
3957  company of the Neapolitan, and as he sat listening and watching the
3958  people drifting past, he was soothed with the feeling of being not so
3959  entirely alone. From Zinfoni's the pair sauntered down to the quay,
3960  where they parted. The Italian was profuse in his thanks and
3961  protestations, and Jerry was considerate enough to act in such a manner
3962  as to make the little man think him the most affable of _Inglesi_.
3963  
3964  When he was aboard again, Jerry got out a chart, and after some
3965  searching located Pæstum. As it was not too far from Naples to be
3966  possible in a day, he determined upon the expedition. Jack was not due
3967  for two or three days yet, and the time must be killed somehow. He
3968  summoned Gonzague, ordered an early breakfast, told him he should be
3969  absent all the next day, and that he should leave him in charge. He had
3970  a sort of mild exhilaration at his boldness in thus venturing off into
3971  the midst of a land whose language he could not speak, and he went to
3972  bed that night with a great feeling of relief. The doldrums were over;
3973  he had something to do to bridge the time until Jack came.
3974  
3975  [Illustration: Decoration]
3976  
3977  
3978  
3979  
3980  Chapter Ten
3981  
3982  MR. WRENMARSH, THE EXTRAORDINARY
3983  
3984  
3985  On the following morning, as, a few minutes after nine, the southbound
3986  train from Naples to Tarento drew out of the station, Taberman, winking
3987  a little at the sudden glare of the sun, began to look about him. The
3988  morning promised a hot day, and his comfort in traveling was likely to
3989  be lessened by the fact that in the second-class compartment with him
3990  were five Italians. They had already settled themselves back against the
3991  cushions, turning upward sunburnt, perspiring faces, and allowing
3992  themselves to be jolted by the train like so many dead-weights. Their
3993  ugly straw hats, high-crowned and narrow-brimmed, were set on their
3994  knees or wedged beside them on the seat; two of the travelers had gay
3995  bandannas tucked into their collars about their throats. One man--a
3996  pursy old codger in the corner--had lighted, after a mumbled "con
3997  permesso," a long Virginia, which filled the compartment with a thin
3998  blue haze and an acrid smell as of burning leather.
3999  
4000  The train rumbled along over a dubious roadbed, flanked by its
4001  cinder-strewn berms; and Tab, looking through the window on his right,
4002  recognized the line as that by which he had gone to Pompeii. At times
4003  the train went close to where the curling ripples of the sapphirine bay
4004  were breaking gently on the shore; sometimes it ran through small
4005  hamlets, and again passed country places where the busy peasants were at
4006  work in the rich vineyards, the orchards, or the tilled fields.
4007  
4008  At the end of half an hour, they stopped at Pompeii for a moment, and
4009  Jerry, through the opposite window, recognized the station and the
4010  paltry inn beyond. As the train drew out again, he caught brief glimpses
4011  of the ancient city, dull red-brown walls among the silver-gray of the
4012  olive-trees.
4013  
4014  The train sped on southward. It dipped into little vales, and wound its
4015  way up and into the hills that ring themselves around the plain of
4016  Pæstum. In an hour's time they pulled up at a small town on the left of
4017  the track. Jerry made out the name of the station, enameled in big white
4018  letters on a blue field, Battapaglia. The guard came by, unlocking the
4019  compartment doors, and as the men in his compartment got out and left
4020  their luggage behind them, Jerry concluded that here was to be a wait
4021  of some minutes. He therefore followed the example of his fellow
4022  travelers, and stepped down upon the sunny platform. It was very hot.
4023  Tab mopped his face with his handkerchief and turned down the brim of
4024  his Panama all around.
4025  
4026  "_Graniti, signor? Citron? Orang'?_"
4027  
4028  A small boy had singled him out, probably because he was the only
4029  _forestiere_ on the platform, and was offering him syrupy drinks cooled
4030  with cracked ice. For a soldo Tab secured a glass of sherbet,
4031  fruit-juice and water half frozen and very delicious. It was so
4032  refreshing that he bestowed an extra soldo on the vender in sheer
4033  gratitude. The lad rewarded him with a curt "grazie," and a look half
4034  grateful and half suspicious, and then hastened on to urge his wares on
4035  other travelers. Jerry looked after him in amusement at the fringe made
4036  by the tatters of his trousers, and in lazy admiration of the sinewy
4037  brown arms left bare by the sleeveless cotton shirt and of the jaunty
4038  poise of the curly head.
4039  
4040  The train still waited.
4041  
4042  Jerry lighted a cigarette and got into the shadow of the cars. Presently
4043  a big express came thundering out of the pass in the hills with a roar,
4044  and rushed away to southward on the main track.
4045  
4046  "_Pronto! Partenza! Partenza!_" cried the guard, with a blast of his
4047  horn.
4048  
4049  The road was again clear, the express-mail having passed. The passengers
4050  clambered aboard, and settled themselves in their former places. The old
4051  man with the Virginia had purchased a copy of "Il Papagallo," though it
4052  was a mystery how he could have got hold of it in such a place. He
4053  clucked oilily as he read, occasionally calling the attention of his
4054  nearest neighbor to some gaudy cartoon or some political pasquinade.
4055  Jerry speculated in regard to what it might all be about, and was filled
4056  with that vague sense of baffled irritation which comes from seeing
4057  others enjoying jokes in a language one cannot understand.
4058  
4059  Mile after mile of level track, flanked by the interminable
4060  cinder-covered berms. Once in a while the level was broken by clumps of
4061  dusty cactus, ugly and forbiddingly aggressive in the sun. To the right,
4062  beyond a flat, gorse-grown waste, relieved only by an occasional palm or
4063  oleaster, Tab could discern the blue shimmer of the sea. To the left, he
4064  could see only the same dull plain, bounded by bluish hills, which rose
4065  about it like the seats of some titanic amphitheatre. Now and again two
4066  or three buffaloes, their black hides caked with patches of yellow mud,
4067  lay in their wallows or stood contemptuously indifferent to the noisy
4068  train, which beside them seemed so impertinently modern.
4069  
4070  At last the train, with a screaming of gritty brakes on the wheels, and
4071  the inevitable clanking and banging of cars and couplings, drew up
4072  beside a tiny station on the right of the track.
4073  
4074  "Pesto! Pesto!"
4075  
4076  The guard unlocked the compartment door, and Jerry stepped out. The
4077  station was smaller than any they had passed, and Tab smilingly
4078  reflected that the lodge at the entrance of his father's place at Dedham
4079  was bigger. He was the only passenger to alight, and no sooner was he
4080  out than the guard, like an overgrown mechanical toy, called out his
4081  "_Pronto! Partenza!_" blew his toy horn, and swung himself aboard again.
4082  The long train, with bitter metallic complaint at being obliged to go
4083  farther, drew past the little station, and rolled away toward a gap in
4084  the southern hills, far beyond which lies Tarento.
4085  
4086  Taberman turned to the station master, a discouraged-looking individual
4087  who stood on the platform with his truncheon tucked under his arm,
4088  examining a batch of dispatches as if this were the first time such
4089  papers had ever come under his notice. Jerry's Italian vocabulary was
4090  limited to some score of words, with a few expressions, such as _dolce
4091  far niente_ and the like, more ornamental than useful. As, however, he
4092  could perceive no sign of any temples,--or town either, for the matter
4093  of that,--he determined to question the _capo_.
4094  
4095  "_Bonn giorno_," he began with a painful sense of effort, but with a
4096  mild self-congratulatory thrill at having said something in Italian.
4097  
4098  "_Buon' giorno_," responded the station master, turning a pair of dull
4099  eyes and an emaciated face from the dispatches to Taberman.
4100  
4101  Jerry spoke French moderately well, and resolved to address the official
4102  in that tongue, in the hope that the Italian might understand.
4103  
4104  "Peut-être vous parlez Français?" he began.
4105  
4106  "_Cosa?_" asked the Italian, obviously puzzled, as he stepped out of the
4107  sun into the shadow of the little station.
4108  
4109  "What?" demanded Jerry in English, and with much the same puzzled air.
4110  
4111  "_Non capisco_," said the man, with a sort of dull finality.
4112  
4113  Conversation languished. Jerry felt himself pretty well baffled, yet he
4114  had no choice but to go on with the unpromising attempt to elicit
4115  information here, as no other human being was in sight. He considered a
4116  moment, and then in an explosive tone, demanded:--
4117  
4118  "_Templi?_"
4119  
4120  "_Bruto Inglise!_" murmured the _capo_ under his breath. "_Che volete?_"
4121  he added aloud.
4122  
4123  "What?" asked Jerry, again scared over the dubious boundary of his
4124  Italian into English.
4125  
4126  "_Non capisco_," repeated the Italian morosely, wetting his dingy
4127  forefinger, and going over his papers for at least the third time.
4128  
4129  "Damn it!" cried Jerry, in complete exasperation, "if you say that again
4130  I'll punch your head!"
4131  
4132  The other started back in such obvious terror that Tab hastened to
4133  propitiate him by putting on quickly his most ingratiating smile, and
4134  nodding as if he had made a merry joke. The other seemed reassured,
4135  although he edged away a little, as if he were doubtful of the sanity of
4136  this foreign brute; and Tab fell again to the effort to rally all the
4137  words in his Italian vocabulary about one idea.
4138  
4139  "_Dove_," he began in one grand final attempt to wring information out
4140  of this sullen and taciturn official, "_dove_"-- He was so pleased with
4141  himself for having remembered the word that he came near forgetting all
4142  the rest, but with a desperate rally, he went blundering on. "_Dove_, I
4143  say, is--is--_la via per i templi_?"
4144  
4145  The _capo_ looked at him, apparently in mingled curiosity and disgust.
4146  Then he beckoned him to the edge of the platform on the other side of
4147  the station, whence stretched westward a ribbon of dust-heaped road.
4148  
4149  "_Ecco-la_" he ejaculated, waving his truncheon vaguely toward the
4150  distance.
4151  
4152  "Ah," said Jerry, "_grazie_."
4153  
4154  As the _capo_ responded to this speech not at all, Tab set out on the
4155  dusty road without more ado. The way was inches deep in loose, gray
4156  dust, and spiny cacti bristled on either hand. Jerry had not gone far
4157  before, turning a bend, he saw at no great distance ahead of him an
4158  arched gateway through which the road passed. The arch, broken and
4159  crumbled, was set in a ruined wall, which trailed away on either hand,
4160  now rising to the height of something like a dozen feet, now razed to
4161  the very ground.
4162  
4163  "That's a forlorn-looking piece o' work," commented Tab aloud.
4164  
4165  Had Jerry been blessed with the education of his forefathers, instead of
4166  having brought out of school and college a hodgepodge smattering of
4167  physics and economics, he might have known and reflected that the wall
4168  he thus carelessly characterized had been standing some two thousand
4169  years, and gloriously attested the puissance of old Rome. With no such
4170  thought, however, he passed beneath the crumbling gateway and continued
4171  his march. At some distance ahead he now perceived signs of life in the
4172  shape of a few dwellings.
4173  
4174  As he looked at them he became aware of two horsemen, who were cantering
4175  toward him on the crest of the little slope made by the road just inside
4176  the old gateway. Their horses' hoofs stirred up light clouds of yellow
4177  dust. Even at first glance the riders showed themselves to be ruggedly
4178  dressed, and with something of a thrill Jerry noticed instantly that
4179  slung across their shoulders they carried carbines. Wild tales of
4180  brigands flashed confusedly through his brain, and especially a tale the
4181  Neapolitan guide had related of the capture and murder at this very
4182  place of an English gentleman and his wife. The guide had said that that
4183  was sixteen years ago, but the place seemed sq lonely, so remote, Tab's
4184  ideas of rural Italy were so vague, the effect of the landscape and of
4185  these wild figures was so startling as, riding toward him, they stood
4186  out against the sky, that it was no wonder Jerry involuntarily cast a
4187  quick glance around to note the lay of the land and to see if any
4188  possible help were in sight in case of need.
4189  
4190  The horsemen rode down to him on a lazy lope. They were big, bronzed
4191  fellows, smoking cigarettes, and riding with their feet out of the
4192  stirrups. They nodded to him pleasantly and smiled, showing large white
4193  teeth. They had about them, these big fellows, a look so engaging that
4194  Tab was won at once, and the vague mist of his suspicions vanished like
4195  smoke in air. He grinned to himself at the idea of brigands.
4196  
4197  "_Dove templi?_" he asked, returning their salutation.
4198  
4199  The big men smiled more broadly, and one of them replied in French.
4200  
4201  "Vous ne parlez pas beaucoup d'italien?" he asked in a pleasant voice.
4202  
4203  "Ne pas de tout!" responded Jerry heartily, with a laugh.
4204  
4205  Having found some one with whom he could talk, he at once began a lively
4206  conversation. He found the two men to be the custodians appointed by the
4207  government to look after the temples and to collect the fees of
4208  travelers. They explained that at this season it was extremely rare for
4209  a visitor to appear, and that they were therefore not particular about
4210  being exactly at their posts. They had heard some rumor of the discovery
4211  of antiques by peasants, and were setting out to investigate. They
4212  explained, however, that the chances of finding out anything were very
4213  small; the peasants all held together, and would all lie for one
4214  another. Jerry inferred, moreover, that they were by no means anxious to
4215  make discoveries. It was part of their duty to investigate such a rumor,
4216  for the government claimed the right to have a hand in the disposal of
4217  any treasure-trove; but the custodians seemed to have a good deal of
4218  sympathy with the wretched peasants, who tried to conceal anything they
4219  might find, in order to sell it for a fraction of its value to any stray
4220  _forestiere_ who might appear. Now that a visitor had come, one of the
4221  men went alone on this errand, and the custode who spoke French returned
4222  toward the temples, which were near at hand, that he might formally take
4223  Tab's lira at the gate.
4224  
4225  The Italian walked his horse beside Taberman past the two or three
4226  ruinous and apparently deserted houses, and in a few minutes the pair
4227  came to where their road ended in a broad turnpike which ran at right
4228  angles to it. On the other side of this turnpike, a little distance to
4229  his left, Jerry saw the ruins of a couple of temples, and beyond them
4230  the sea. His guide disregarded them, and led him to the right hand,
4231  where, a hundred yards or so along the highway, they came to a square
4232  two-story building of gray rubble. On its dingy front was painted in
4233  black letters the word "Osteria."
4234  
4235  "V'là l'auberge," announced the jovial custodian. "If Michu is fatigued,
4236  he can get eggs and polenta within. The wine is rough, but not so bad as
4237  the water. This way, Michu."
4238  
4239  And leaving his horse to crop the rank grass by the doorway, he strode
4240  into the building, Tab following.
4241  
4242  The inn was a poor place, even for southern Italy. The floor was of
4243  trampled clay; the walls were unfinished within as without, but like the
4244  ceiling, from which hung bunches of garlic and black and dusty herbs,
4245  they were garnished with abundant cobwebs and a generous coating of soot
4246  and dirt. At the back of the room was a counter, above which a grimy
4247  sign announced the right of the proprietor to sell salt and tobacco. In
4248  the left-hand corner of the back of the place was one of the altar-like
4249  ranges of Italy, upon which glowed a minute heap of charcoal. Tab smiled
4250  to find himself recognizing its use from its resemblance to the
4251  cooking-places he had seen in the ruins of Pompeii, and reflected, with
4252  the superiority of a youth born in a young land, upon the conservatism
4253  which keeps its kitchen arrangements practically the same as they were
4254  two thousand years ago. The room was lighted simply by the door through
4255  which the visitors had entered. Another doorway at the left simply
4256  yawned blackly like the mouth of a cavern. The furniture consisted of a
4257  small square table and three stools. Over the entire place was spread an
4258  appearance of squalor and neglect, depressing, but in key with the air
4259  of poverty and of deadness which had been more evident to Tab with every
4260  step he had taken in Pæstum.
4261  
4262  The room was empty when they entered it, but after the custode had
4263  bellowed lustily once or twice for "Angelo," the innkeeper appeared
4264  suddenly. He was a little man doubled up as if with rheumatism, and with
4265  a face as yellow as a dried lemon. On seeing Taberman he croaked
4266  something to the custode, and bowed to his guest again and again,
4267  rubbing his hands and all but losing his crooked balance with each
4268  genuflection.
4269  
4270  With the air of an archduke ordering a banquet for his retainers,
4271  Jerry's companion gave some rapid instructions to the innkeeper, told
4272  the Michu to make the place his own, and then departed to attend to his
4273  horse and other trifles, saying that he would be back in half an hour.
4274  
4275  Tab seated himself on a stool to await his luncheon. His host puttered
4276  about the altar, occasionally mumbling to himself, like the devotee of
4277  some Stygian power making sacrifice. Jerry was watching him with
4278  amusement, and wondering what would be the outcome of his incantations
4279  in the way of food, when on a sudden the doorway was darkened, and a man
4280  entered the room. At a glance Jerry saw that the newcomer was, like
4281  himself, a traveler. The stranger was of medium height, rather inclined,
4282  hardly to stoutness, but certainly to plumpness; he was well
4283  proportioned, with broad shoulders, but had a carriage curiously
4284  shuffling and insignificant. He held a stiff-brimmed straw hat in his
4285  hand, and Tab could see, where the outer light fell upon his crown, that
4286  his hair was slightly touched with gray. His face, Jerry decided, would
4287  have been handsome, had it not been marred by two deep lines from the
4288  nostrils to the corners of the mouth, which gave an appearance of
4289  sinister suspicion not without a hint of selfish cruelty. Except for a
4290  very silky mustache, he was clean-shaven.
4291  
4292  The traveler threw Taberman a quick, almost furtive glance, and then,
4293  turning to the innkeeper, addressed that individual sharply in Italian.
4294  The crooked host bowed furiously, made apologetic and deprecatory
4295  gestures with the rapidity of a mountebank, skipped about in feverish
4296  excitement, and jerked his head more and more frantically. The
4297  gentleman--for he seemed one--continued his objurgations unappeased by
4298  all these demonstrations, and ended by swearing roundly in English.
4299  
4300  "Oh!" exclaimed Taberman involuntarily.
4301  
4302  The stranger turned to him.
4303  
4304  "I beg your pardon," he said in a curious sing-song voice with a
4305  markedly rising inflection, "but this brute has not prepared my
4306  luncheon. Do you mind sharing the table with me?"
4307  
4308  "Not the least in the world," replied Jerry. "I'm sure it will give me
4309  great pleasure."
4310  
4311  "Good," said the stranger. "I see you are an American," he flung out as
4312  an addition.
4313  
4314  "I am," returned Taberman, feeling a simple pride in the fact.
4315  
4316  "Thank God I'm not," remarked the stranger. His voice showed no trace of
4317  truculence; it was murmured as if to himself. Before Jerry had time to
4318  explode the gentleman continued: "I'm English. What does that mean?
4319  Celt, Angle, Saxon, and ages of tradition--ages of it. By the bye, you
4320  mustn't mind the things I say, you know; your pernicious self-respect
4321  would force you to resent them if you did. May I ask your name?"
4322  
4323  "My name is Taberman," Jerry replied, struggling with a mingling of
4324  indignation, amazement, and amusement, "Jerrold Taberman. I live in
4325  Boston."
4326  
4327  "Dedham rather," returned the other easily. "I knew a Taberman when I
4328  was in college. Curious chap. I-- My name's Wrenmarsh, Gordon Wrenmarsh.
4329  Fact is, I was an American, but I couldn't stand the place. Bostonians
4330  have good manners; but New York is a vile spot. So is Boston; that is--
4331  Well, perhaps you see the difference."
4332  
4333  The tricks this extraordinary man played with his voice were
4334  astonishing, and as he went on talking he quite dizzied Tab by the
4335  cryptic, baffling nature of his nervous speeches. He had, too, a curious
4336  and disconcerting habit of displaying great emotional intensity--opening
4337  his eyes to their greatest extent and distending his nostrils--in
4338  dealing with trifles of the slightest consequence; while whenever, as
4339  happened once or twice in the course of the luncheon, they touched even
4340  remotely on subjects of really vital importance, the extraordinary Mr.
4341  Wrenmarsh fairly oozed indifference. His conduct was so thoroughly
4342  strange that once or twice Jerry felt a puzzled doubt whether the man
4343  were entirely sane.
4344  
4345  "I'll tell you," said Mr. Wrenmarsh, when their slight repast was over,
4346  "we'll do the temples together. I've been camping in this abominable
4347  hole of an _osteria_ for over a week, so that I know them pretty well.
4348  One of them is in my period, moreover."
4349  
4350  Jerry looked at him as if to ask if the stranger claimed to be a
4351  contemporary of the ruins.
4352  
4353  "Your period?" he echoed confusedly.
4354  
4355  "Yes; you see, I'm an archæologist--collector, in fact. Hello; here's
4356  the custode."
4357  
4358  The custodian entered as Mr. Wrenmarsh spoke, and Taberman had somehow
4359  the idea that the look he gave the Englishman was not very friendly.
4360  
4361  "Ah, Michu, have you found a friend?" he asked in his queer French.
4362  
4363  "I don't know," Jerry returned, with a half laugh.
4364  
4365  "Well," responded the Italian, "if Michu is ready to see the temples, I
4366  am waiting."
4367  
4368  "Bien," responded Jerry; and then turning to the archæologist, he asked,
4369  "Are you coming?"
4370  
4371  "Of course," the Englishman answered. "Never mind this custode; he's
4372  only an ignorant pig."
4373  
4374  Jerry secretly felt that, ignorant or not, the big Italian, with his
4375  merry face and open smile, would be a much more companionable guide than
4376  the eccentric collector; but without comment he paid the reckoning, and
4377  they set out. They went down the road to a gate, paid a lira each to the
4378  custode, and entered upon a field of ploughed land, planted with maize.
4379  The Italian, who had more and more the air of not liking the Englishman,
4380  made some remarks to the effect that Michu l'Anglaise was a very learned
4381  man, and one much better fitted to explain the marvels of ancient
4382  architecture than he, a plain man who had had to pick up his education
4383  in the army. On these grounds he excused himself and went into a little
4384  lodge, while the others walked on to the temples which stood before
4385  them, ideal in their beauty.
4386  
4387  The two pushed their way across the field and entered the nearest
4388  temple. Jerry's was not an impressionable nature, and in one way to him
4389  these august colonnades meant little; yet despite a certain sophomoric
4390  exuberance which he had never outgrown, his nature was fundamentally too
4391  refined to fail to respond to the silent grandeur of this solemn harmony
4392  in stone. The roofless enclosure, after all the indignities a score of
4393  centuries had been able to inflict upon it, possessed still a nobility
4394  and a beauty which seemed almost personal and conscious. One feels in
4395  seeing the ruins at Pæstum as if a certain inherent and indestructible
4396  loveliness would pervade the very stones were they thrown down to the
4397  last one; and while the columns stand, the place is one to make the
4398  visitor catch his breath with admiration and almost with awe. Taberman
4399  did not analyze, and indeed he was instinctively so occupied in
4400  concealing from his companion how profoundly he was impressed as to have
4401  little attention left for introspection; but he was more deeply stirred
4402  than he could have conceived possible.
4403  
4404  He walked about with Mr. Wrenmarsh, who talked along in his curious
4405  voice, expatiating upon styles and orders, influence and epochs, with
4406  all sorts of things of which Jerry understood at best not more than a
4407  quarter; until at last, instead of going on to the neighboring temple,
4408  the strangely assorted pair sat down on the western steps of the ruin
4409  through which they had come. Taberman looked away westward, where the
4410  rim of the sea shone like a fillet of molten silver. For some time
4411  neither spoke; but at length Mr. Wrenmarsh broke in upon Tab's train of
4412  thought with a question.
4413  
4414  "Are you traveling alone?" he asked quite suddenly.
4415  
4416  Taberman explained that he had come over from America in a yacht. It is
4417  to be feared that it was vanity which led him to make the unlucky
4418  addition that he was in command of her until his friend should rejoin
4419  him at Naples.
4420  
4421  "Ah," commented the archæologist, with a new appearance of interest;
4422  "you're cruising."
4423  
4424  "Yes," said Jerry.
4425  
4426  The spell of the temple was upon him, and he had no inclination to talk.
4427  He was conscious of a half-defined desire to have this stranger take
4428  himself off, and not bother him further with questions.
4429  
4430  "And what do you suppose I am doing here?" queried the collector in a
4431  tone of almost fierce intensity.
4432  
4433  "Why," Jerry responded rather absently, "I supposed you were studying or
4434  something."
4435  
4436  "Why, yes, to be sure I am; haven't I told the custode so?" chuckled Mr.
4437  Wrenmarsh. His laughter was as extraordinary as his speech and manner.
4438  He would double up as if with a sort of a spasm and snigger gastrically.
4439  "But that's not all," he went on, as Jerry turned to look at him
4440  questioningly; "that's not all. I'm doing something else. I'm waiting."
4441  
4442  "What for?" asked Taberman, seeing that he was expected to speak.
4443  
4444  "Help," replied Wrenmarsh laconically.
4445  
4446  "Help?" repeated Jerry blankly.
4447  
4448  "Yes, help; waiting. Collecting is nothing but waiting anyway,--waiting
4449  for news, waiting for funds, waiting for auctions, waiting for old
4450  countesses to die, waiting for some fool of a peasant to discover
4451  something; waiting, waiting, waiting all along the line. It's the man
4452  who waits with his ears and eyes open and his mouth shut that gets what
4453  he wants. He's the man."
4454  
4455  "But--but what sort of help do you want now?" Tab inquired.
4456  
4457  He was sympathetic by nature, and this extraordinary individual had
4458  aroused not only his curiosity, but in some mysterious manner stimulated
4459  him to a desire to be of service. He had come to Pæstum for amusement.
4460  He felt that in meeting the collector he had been amply repaid. The
4461  unwonted emotion which had been stirred by the temple melted in his
4462  boyish heart before the warmer human interest which the collector
4463  aroused, and it was perhaps with some unrealized relief at getting back
4464  to more familiar levels of feeling that he now began to enter into the
4465  affairs of his companion. It came over him that he was being appealed
4466  to, and he was ready to take the position that if any aid of his could
4467  bring relief to Mr. Wrenmarsh, that eccentric gentleman should no longer
4468  need to go on waiting for help.
4469  
4470  "I'll tell you the whole business," said the archæologist, in a sudden
4471  burst of frankness. "You look trustworthy. I've been here ten
4472  days--waiting. I've written, of course, for help; but it doesn't seem to
4473  come. Three weeks ago I was in Naples, and heard--no matter how--that
4474  somewhere down here a lot of good stuff had turned up. I kept coming
4475  down here daily until, by dint of discreet questions--discretion's the
4476  backbone of the game--I found out what had happened. A peasant here had
4477  been spading over some ground. One day the earth sunk suddenly under
4478  him, and down he went into a hole. He found, as soon as he could get his
4479  wits together, that he had broken through the roof of an ancient _cella_
4480  of some sort. He got out without much trouble, pulled himself together,
4481  and did what any peasant would know enough to do,--covered the place
4482  with brush and dirt so that no news of the thing should get to the
4483  custodi. Then he went on with his spading."
4484  
4485  "Without investigating?" asked Jerry, full of interest.
4486  
4487  Mr. Wrenmarsh looked at him curiously.
4488  
4489  "Of course," he responded. "If he had let his curiosity get the better
4490  of him, or his tongue wag, he'd be a good deal poorer than he is at
4491  present. They are stupid louts, these peasants, but they do learn
4492  enough not to take the government into their confidence when they find
4493  anything. They know that they'd get nothing out of it if they did.
4494  Besides, they are as stolid as buffaloes. They can wait well enough."
4495  
4496  "But what did he find?" demanded Taberman, his interest thoroughly
4497  aroused by this tale of treasure-trove, which appealed to every boyish
4498  and every adventurous fibre in him.
4499  
4500  "He went by night with a lantern and a couple of panniers. He filled his
4501  baskets twice, filled them with priceless things in a perfect
4502  condition--beautiful kylixes and glass bowls. There's one that measures
4503  at least half a metre across the top. Think of that! Why, it's the
4504  finest glass I've ever seen or heard of! It's the finest glass there
4505  is!"
4506  
4507  "Great Scott!" cried Jerry, alive with excitement. "It must be awfully
4508  old!"
4509  
4510  "Old!" retorted Wrenmarsh with scorn; "do you know where you are?"
4511  
4512  Jerry twisted his head to look up at the tall columns and broken
4513  pediment above him, on the pinkish-gray stones of which the afternoon
4514  sun fell with loving warmth.
4515  
4516  "Yes, of course," he said. "But what did he do with the things?"
4517  
4518  "I kept at him till I wormed the whole business out of him," the
4519  collector answered, "and I bought his things--damn him!"
4520  
4521  He brought out the objurgation with amazing vigor; then stopped and
4522  stared gloomily before him.
4523  
4524  "Well?" said Jerry. "What are you waiting for? More?"
4525  
4526  "More!" exploded the collector, disgust and indignation in his face.
4527  "Man, I've got hold of a collection that is all but unique! More! Don't
4528  you see--I can't get away with it! Piece by piece I could run it out of
4529  the country, but I don't dare to leave anything behind me. If only my
4530  men were at hand--but they're not, they're not. One's off the track in
4531  the T road, and the other's in America."
4532  
4533  He passed his hand before his eyes with a gesture so expressive that it
4534  was even more impassioned than his tone.
4535  
4536  Taberman was moved, both by the enthusiasm of this man for his work and
4537  by the exciting romance of the finding of this treasure. He knew vaguely
4538  of the laws that forbade the taking of works of art out of Italy and
4539  Greece, but he had no conception that they were strictly enforced. It
4540  gave him a new sensation to be thus brought in contact with the actual
4541  working of a statute which was aimed to prevent a man from removing his
4542  own possessions from one country to another. He had been too well
4543  brought up under a high protective tariff to have any moral scruples
4544  about smuggling anything. A Mugwump atmosphere had acted upon the
4545  natural inclination of youth to defy authority, and had bred in Jerry
4546  the feeling that smuggling, however little its true nature was
4547  appreciated in high places, was really in its essence a maligned virtue.
4548  In the present instance, moreover, the boyish feeling that what one owns
4549  is his to do what he chooses with despite all fiats of principalities,
4550  potentates, and powers, helped to make the idea of this especial case of
4551  an attempt to defy the laws one of particular merit. He gave himself
4552  eagerly to considering how it could be done.
4553  
4554  "Can't you take your traps to Naples, and ship 'em from there?" he at
4555  last demanded of the archæologist.
4556  
4557  "You don't understand, I'm afraid," replied the other. "My reputation in
4558  itself compels me to lie close. Besides that, there's the awkward
4559  problem of the octroi and the export examinations. I couldn't take the
4560  things into Naples without running into the one, or out of it without
4561  getting afoul of the other. They'd be no end sharp in examining
4562  anything I tried to pass. I'm hideously notorious in Italy." His pride
4563  in this last statement was entirely evident, but Jerry was impressed by
4564  the deeds of archæological daring which were implied in such a
4565  reputation. "I simply can't get these things away without help," he
4566  continued. "I've written and telegraphed to every mortal I can count
4567  on,--there are only five or six of them,--and not one of them can help
4568  me out just now. Meanwhile I starve on eggs and polenta, under the
4569  suspicious eyes of the custodi--damn 'em! They'd have got me a week ago
4570  if they'd had any brains."
4571  
4572  "Upon my word," cried Jerry, the idea suddenly striking him for the
4573  first time, "it's extraordinary you should tell me all this, and I a
4574  stranger."
4575  
4576  "I count on your helping me," responded Mr. Wrenmarsh in keenly incisive
4577  tones.
4578  
4579  "My helping you!" ejaculated Tab in amazement. "What in the world have I
4580  to do with the business?"
4581  
4582  "You practically said so," returned the collector. "At least your face
4583  did." He looked at Jerry, and then turned away to the brown expanse of
4584  plain in a manner so stricken and so reproachful that Taberman could not
4585  help feeling convicted of consummate wickedness. "I counted on you," he
4586  added, in a tone of profoundest pathos.
4587
