4588  Jerry was completely nonplussed. He felt that he was being played with;
4589  he was angrily conscious that the whole affair was no concern of his,
4590  and that he had no business to be dragged into it. Yet he felt no less
4591  but rather more keenly that he could not endure the imputation of having
4592  encouraged a man in difficulties with a hope of assistance and of having
4593  then refused to fulfill them. His youthful blood, moreover, was stirred
4594  by the flavor of adventure which came alluringly to his inner sense. For
4595  a moment there was a strained silence, and then it was broken by Tab.
4596  
4597  "You've mistaken my interest for something else, I'm afraid," he said,
4598  trying to speak lightly, and feeling that he was making a mess of it.
4599  "It never even occurred to me that I could help you out of this blessed
4600  muss; and I don't see that there's anything I can do anyway, except to
4601  keep mum about it. Of course that I'd do anyway."
4602  
4603  "No use," retorted the archæologist. "If you can help me and won't,
4604  after my taking you into my confidence, you--you ruin me."
4605  
4606  "Hmm," Jerry observed rather coldly, "that's too subtle for me. I fail
4607  to see it in that light. You're no worse off than you were before."
4608  
4609  "I'm sure, Mr. Tableman"--
4610  
4611  "Taberman," Jerry corrected.
4612  
4613  "Pardon me, Mr. Taberman; but you don't see the _catena logica_ by which
4614  I arrive at my conclusions!" Mr. Wrenmarsh, both in speech and gestures,
4615  was momentarily growing more and more theatrical. "Suppose you should,
4616  knowing my story and the law against taking works of art out of the
4617  country, tell my case to the police. What then?"
4618  
4619  "It would be the trick of a blackguard, of course," Jerry replied
4620  promptly, "but"--
4621  
4622  "_Momento!_" interrupted the other, holding up his hand. "Now suppose
4623  things to be as they are, and you learn that the custodi are on my
4624  track"--
4625  
4626  "They've heard something of the find," interposed Jerry; "they told me
4627  that."
4628  
4629  "There! You see!" Wrenmarsh said, with a gesture which seemed to appeal
4630  to all humanity to bear witness that in whatever he had said he had been
4631  completely right. "Suppose, now, that you have--with perfect security to
4632  yourself, mind--a chance to give me a friendly word of warning, and
4633  don't do it. What then?"
4634  
4635  "Why," Tab answered, feeling every moment more and more as if he were
4636  being snarled up in a web, "it would be, in such a case as you suppose,
4637  a pretty shabby trick, of course. At the same time"--
4638  
4639  "Wait a bit," cried Mr. Wrenmarsh, again interrupting him, and growing
4640  visibly more excited still; "wait a bit. I want you to consider the
4641  present case. You say yourself the secret is leaking out, and of course
4642  every moment makes my danger greater. With practically no bother and
4643  with absolute safety you can help me out of the whole tangle. If you
4644  don't, I shall be caught; I shall lose this incomparable treasure and
4645  all the money I paid for it,--and that's no small sum, let me tell
4646  you,--and all because you, my forlorn hope that I've confided in _in
4647  rebus angustis_, won't devote twenty-four hours of your time to saving
4648  your own self-respect. By Jove!" he cried, starting to his feet, "if you
4649  don't help me you betray me as much as if you went straight to the
4650  custodi with my story."
4651  
4652  "Sit tight!" cried Jerry, startled by the violence of the other's
4653  demonstration. "Sit tight!"
4654  
4655  "Will you help me?" demanded Mr. Wrenmarsh, his brown eyes blazing.
4656  "Will you help--help me to dodge these Italian robbers and get my
4657  things--my antiquities that I have paid for with hard cash--out of this
4658  rotten country? Will you help, or will you desert me, and take sides
4659  with those that are waiting to rob me?"
4660  
4661  "By George, I've a mind to try!" incautiously ejaculated Jerry, for the
4662  moment carried off his balance by the enthusiasm and the persuasive
4663  personality of the other.
4664  
4665  "Good man!" cried the antiquarian in a rapture; "good man! I knew you
4666  would. We'll beat 'em! I"--
4667  
4668  "Hold your horses a bit!" put in Tab hastily, taken aback by the force
4669  Wrenmarsh gave to his unconsidered words. "Go slow, please. I may
4670  have"--
4671  
4672  "Oh, that's all right," returned the collector impetuously. "We'll take
4673  a turn down the road, and plan it all out. I can think better when I'm
4674  walking--sort of peripatetic, you see. Ha, ha!--and it'll look queer if
4675  you don't go down to see the other temple. Come on."
4676  
4677  Mr. Wrenmarsh made his way toward the road, trampling impetuously over
4678  the wild thyme and the acanthus, while Taberman followed in a mixture of
4679  amused amazement and indignation, but with a full determination to
4680  expostulate. He found, however, that he was not allowed any opportunity
4681  for remonstrance. Every sentence he began was choked off with some fresh
4682  exclamation of gratitude from the collector, or by some burst of
4683  delight that out of the skies, as it were, he had fallen to be the
4684  savior of the perplexed archæologist. By the time they had walked around
4685  the third temple, which stands at some distance from the other two,
4686  Taberman had given up protesting. He merely listened to his companion's
4687  bewildering flow of talk, and felt as if he were being drawn into a
4688  whirlpool. He was helped by his own secret delight at the thought of
4689  having a share in a real adventure, and perhaps pushed on by a boyish
4690  shame at the idea of seeming to draw back and to fail another in an
4691  extremity. He had not much chance to speak,--but he soon found that what
4692  he did say was in the line of his having accepted the position into
4693  which Mr. Wrenmarsh had been endeavoring to force him.
4694  
4695  As they returned from the third temple they found the custode beside the
4696  fountain which stood across the road from the inn. He was trying to
4697  teach his horse to shake hands.
4698  
4699  "Ah, Michu," the Italian said as they came up to him; "I hope you were
4700  pleased with the temples."
4701  
4702  "Much," Taberman assured him. "They are magnificent."
4703  
4704  Seeing his companion fee the man, he in turn slipped a coin into the
4705  brown hand. His conscience gave him a little twinge at the thought of
4706  plotting to outwit this frank, big creature; but he reflected instantly
4707  that the matter was entirely impersonal, and it was not in a
4708  tariff-hating youth like Jerry to have any scruples over tricking the
4709  Italian government in a matter of this sort.
4710  
4711  "How long would it take you to sail down here from Naples?" asked
4712  Wrenmarsh, as they took the road toward the station.
4713  
4714  Tab considered.
4715  
4716  "Five or six hours with a good breeze," was his conclusion.
4717  
4718  Mr. Wrenmarsh wrinkled his brows and quickened his pace. Those
4719  uncomfortable lines from the nostrils to the corners of his mouth
4720  deepened, and he half shut his eyes. After a little meditation he spoke
4721  again.
4722  
4723  "Very good," he said decisively. "This is the way we'll put the thing
4724  through. You go back to Naples now. Be off the shore here by eleven
4725  o'clock, and send a boat ashore for me and my boxes. They're rather big,
4726  and fairly heavy; and they've got to be handled tenderly. I couldn't get
4727  proper means of packing the things, and I've had to take what there was.
4728  Once we get the stuff on board, we must run back so as to be in Naples
4729  by sunrise. Does that suit you?"
4730  
4731  "You seem to be running this cruise," laughed Jerry. "I suppose it's all
4732  right; but there's one thing I must know. There's no chance of getting
4733  the yacht into a scrape, is there?"
4734  
4735  "Oh, no danger whatever."
4736  
4737  "You're sure?" Tab insisted. "It wouldn't be exactly pleasant to get my
4738  friend's boat confiscated, you know, or into any sort of a mess of that
4739  kind."
4740  
4741  "Bosh!" retorted Mr. Wrenmarsh brusquely. "You may make your mind easy.
4742  The worst that could happen is that I might lose my things. But we must
4743  walk a bit faster, if you're to get your train."
4744  
4745  "It's better to say to-morrow night," Tab remarked, as they took their
4746  way down the road and beneath the old Roman arch. "You see I might be
4747  late in getting back, and"--
4748  
4749  "Of course, of course," interrupted the collector. "You can't count on
4750  getting here to-night. To-morrow night, of course."
4751  
4752  At the station the _capo_ was standing almost where Jerry had left him,
4753  looking at the hills. When the two came up, he merely turned his head
4754  and nodded.
4755  
4756  "The _facchino_ must be doing ticket-duty," the collector remarked.
4757  "We'll go in and get your ticket."
4758  
4759  A tall, yellow, broken-looking man was behind the little wicket in the
4760  ticket-office, puttering with some sort of repair work on a shelf. Mr.
4761  Wrenmarsh addressed him in Italian. The man took a blue and green ticket
4762  from a pigeon-hole on the wall, placed it under the stamp, on the knob
4763  of which he then brought down his fist with a nervous bang. Instantly he
4764  broke out into a violent exclamation.
4765  
4766  "_Sacro sangue della Madonna!_" he shouted, and began to rave
4767  hysterically.
4768  
4769  "What's the matter?" asked Taberman. "What is he saying?"
4770  
4771  "He is cursing quite well," returned the archæologist coolly. "His hand
4772  was unsteady, and he's broken the stamp. He wants to know what will
4773  become of him when the _capo_ finds the punch is broken."
4774  
4775  "Is he tight?" inquired Jerry inelegantly.
4776  
4777  "Oh, he's only bally-rotten with malaria. Look at his face."
4778  
4779  "Tell him he ought to take some quinine," suggested Taberman, genuinely
4780  sorry for the wretched-looking fellow.
4781  
4782  Mr. Wrenmarsh interpreted, but the Italian replied in a tone of mingled
4783  despair and contempt, and went out to show the broken punch to his
4784  superior.
4785  
4786  "What does he say?" asked Jerry.
4787  
4788  "Says he took twenty-four grains this noon," answered Wrenmarsh,
4789  chuckling as if it were funny.
4790  
4791  "Gad!" exclaimed Tab. "No wonder his hand shook. What a country!"
4792  
4793  "You say that?" returned the other. "You may remember that I'm tied to
4794  it till I can get my things out."
4795  
4796  They went out to the platform, and at the moment the train came in.
4797  Jerry took his seat in an empty compartment, and the collector stood
4798  outside the window.
4799  
4800  "You'll surely come?" asked Mr. Wrenmarsh, in a voice almost
4801  threatening.
4802  
4803  "I can't see that I should," Taberman returned; "but wind and weather
4804  permitting, I suppose I shall."
4805  
4806  "I can't attempt to argue with you here," the other said; "but
4807  mind--you'll come."
4808  
4809  "_Pronto! Pronto!_" called the guard in his hoarse sing-song.
4810  
4811  "I shall come," Jerry said reassuringly. "You may bet on it."
4812  
4813  "_Partenza! Partenza!_" the guard bawled, blowing his horn.
4814  
4815  "Good-by. Don't miss it!" cried Wrenmarsh, giving Jerry's hand a
4816  farewell grip.
4817  
4818  "To-morrow night," returned Taberman.
4819  
4820  "I show a light," the collector vociferated, running along the platform
4821  beside the now moving train, and repeating the details he had already
4822  arranged. "A white light."
4823  
4824  "Right-o!" shouted Taberman, as the train bore him beyond the reach of
4825  further communication.
4826  
4827  He threw himself back into the corner of the compartment, and all the
4828  way to Naples he kept wondering over and over what there was about Mr.
4829  Wrenmarsh that had induced him to promise to have a share in a scheme so
4830  mad.
4831  
4832  [Illustration: Decoration]
4833  
4834  
4835  
4836  
4837  Chapter Eleven
4838  
4839  A LONE-HAND GAME
4840  
4841  
4842  On the morning after his return Jerry rose at an hour comfortably late,
4843  took a swim, shaved, and having finished his breakfast, sat down to
4844  write a short note to Jack. As the captain might put in an appearance at
4845  any moment now, Taberman did not wish to go away from Naples without
4846  leaving some explanation and a hint as to his whereabouts. He found the
4847  letter somewhat difficult to write, since to give Jack a satisfactory
4848  reason for his errand to Pæstum, especially in brief space, was no easy
4849  task. He had been more or less troubled ever since his preposterous
4850  promise to Mr. Wrenmarsh; but now that he was confronted with the
4851  difficulty of making his course appear rational to Jack, he felt himself
4852  so completely a fool that he groaned as he wrote, and then tore up the
4853  note, with a curse. On the whole, he decided to say no more than that he
4854  had gone to take a short run down the coast, as he was bored at Naples.
4855  
4856  He went ashore with the note himself, and leaving the cutter at the
4857  quay to wait for him, he set out on foot for the Hôtel du Vesuve, where
4858  Jack was to report on his arrival. The morning was already well
4859  advanced, and the heat was becoming fervent; but Jerry, freshened by his
4860  recent swim, went blithely on his way. At the hotel he said to the
4861  porter that he wished to leave a letter for a gentleman who was soon to
4862  arrive, and produced his note. The official glanced at the
4863  superscription, and observed that the traveler was already there.
4864  
4865  Jerry stared at him dumfounded.
4866  
4867  "Arrived?" he gasped. "When?"
4868  
4869  "He came on the night train from Rome," replied the porter, whose
4870  English was almost as good as that of Taberman. "He came on the train
4871  that gets in at half-past eight in the morning. He is escorting two
4872  ladies. They are now at breakfast."
4873  
4874  Tab stood for a moment plunged in perplexity. This unexpected arrival of
4875  Jack made his scheme of aiding Wrenmarsh dreadfully difficult, and
4876  perhaps even impossible. He felt himself pledged, however, and he
4877  reflected that whatever were Jack's plans the captain would hardly
4878  hinder him from keeping a promise which he had made on the strength of
4879  the supposition that the Merle was to be in his hands a full month. Jack
4880  had come back before his time, but Tab said to himself that this would
4881  surely make no difference in his fulfilling his obligations to the
4882  archæologist.
4883  
4884  He asked for the breakfast party, and was shown into the carefully
4885  shaded dining-room where they were seated. Hearty greetings followed,
4886  and he sat and talked with them while they finished their repast.
4887  
4888  All three looked a bit fagged. Even Mrs. Fairhew, accustomed as she was
4889  to European travel of all sorts, had dark circles under her keen eyes.
4890  She was dressed, not according to her wont in black, but in a soft gray
4891  which well set off her brilliant complexion, so that in spite of the
4892  look of fatigue she appeared much as she had when the travelers had met
4893  at Nice. Jack was clad in a suit of white linen, with a collarless
4894  jacket such as is worn by naval officers in hot climates. His hair had
4895  been recently cut, and in such a manner as to cause each separate spike
4896  along the parting to stand up in stiff defiance. Jerry politely told him
4897  he looked more like a criminal than usual, but Miss Marchfield protested
4898  rather indignantly. In Katrine Jerry seemed to detect more alteration
4899  than in the others. Her air had grown more sedate, as if the widening
4900  of her mental horizon had, even in these few weeks, given her a new
4901  maturity and self-poise. The heat had perhaps told on her more than on
4902  the others, but in spite of some appearance of fatigue she had an air of
4903  joyous alertness which showed her buoyant and happy.
4904  
4905  "How is it that you are here so soon?" Taberman asked, after a minute of
4906  general talk. "I thought you'd be late, if anything."
4907  
4908  "There was a good deal of sickness at Rome," Jack answered, "and when a
4909  man died of typhoid fever in the very hotel we were at, it seemed time
4910  to move on."
4911  
4912  Mrs. Fairhew gave a little shudder.
4913  
4914  "Only fancy," she said,--"we knew nothing about it until he had been
4915  dead an hour. They told us after breakfast yesterday morning. It was
4916  rather unpleasant, you'll grant."
4917  
4918  "It must have been ghastly," agreed Tab, "but I hope you'll do better in
4919  Naples. It has at least the advantage of being on the sea."
4920  
4921  "And of being one of the dirtiest places in Italy," she responded
4922  grimly. "However, I'm not one to borrow trouble, and we'll trust in the
4923  sea air."
4924  
4925  "You're really becoming amphibious, Mr. Taberman," Katrine observed,
4926  with a smile. "I half fancy that if you were blindfolded you could
4927  smell your way to the water like a turtle."
4928  
4929  "The man that piloted the Merle from North Haven to the Island said he
4930  went by smell," responded Jerry.
4931  
4932  He caught Jack's eye as he spoke, and cast down his glance in confusion.
4933  Mrs. Fairhew regarded him curiously.
4934  
4935  "How did Mr. Drake like that sort of a pilot?" she asked.
4936  
4937  "He didn't hear the remark," Jack put in hastily. "Uncle Randolph
4938  wouldn't have approved of that sort of work, I rather fancy."
4939  
4940  Jerry made a grimace, and echoed the sentiment, but he added that Dave
4941  was really an excellent sailor, and that personally he'd trust the
4942  fellow's sense of smell sooner than he would the skill of most pilots.
4943  The dangerous moment passed without further allusion to the President,
4944  and the talk turned to other matters.
4945  
4946  "Is there any one here we know?" inquired Mrs. Fairhew. "I suppose it is
4947  hardly possible at this time of year."
4948  
4949  "I don't believe there is," answered Tab, "unless," he added, a sudden
4950  thought striking him, "you know where Pæstum is?"
4951  
4952  "Certainly. I've been looking forward with dread to dragging Katrine
4953  down there to see the temples, though really the time of year ought to
4954  excuse us."
4955  
4956  "Well, there's a sort of Anglo-American lunatic archæologist down there,
4957  named Wrenmarsh. Have you ever heard of him? He has relatives in Boston,
4958  I understood him."
4959  
4960  Mrs. Fairhew set down the coffee-cup she was just raising to her lips,
4961  and looked at Jerry with a keen glance in which amusement and surprise
4962  seemed to be mingled.
4963  
4964  "What is his Christian name?" she asked.
4965  
4966  "Gordon."
4967  
4968  "Gordon Wrenmarsh at Pæstum! Well, the world is small, and he might be
4969  anywhere,--at least anywhere where he was not expected to be. Did you
4970  never hear of him? But no, you wouldn't; you're too young. He is one of
4971  my contemporaries, and he has been on this side of the water for ever so
4972  long."
4973  
4974  "Is it possible?" Jerry cried gallantly. "I shouldn't have suspected
4975  that he was so young!"
4976  
4977  "Nobody can mistake you when you wish to pay a compliment," she said,
4978  with a smile that had in it a tinge of satire. "But did you really see
4979  Gordon Wrenmarsh? I haven't heard of him for years. What is he doing? At
4980  one time he was a friend of Mr. Fairhew; they were in the same class at
4981  Harvard."
4982  
4983  She showed a genuine interest, Jerry thought; and at any rate this
4984  seemed to him a good time to prepare Jack for the plan evolved between
4985  him and the archæologist, so he launched forth on the narrative of his
4986  visit to Pæstum. He did not particularize, but he did not hesitate to
4987  say that the archæologist had chanced upon a rich find which he was
4988  guarding in the hope of running it safely out of the country.
4989  
4990  "Why shouldn't he take it out of the country if he's bought it?" Katrine
4991  asked, with an air of interest.
4992  
4993  "The Italian law says he shan't," Jack answered, with a smile.
4994  
4995  "Why, if it's his, he has a right to do what he pleases, I should
4996  think," she responded.
4997  
4998  "But there's a law against carrying works of art out of the country."
4999  
5000  "What a horrid, unjust law!" she protested. "If they were mine, I'd take
5001  them out; you may be sure of that."
5002  
5003  "I'd help you," Jack assured her lightly.
5004  
5005  Jerry was secretly so pleased at this passage that he endeavored to keep
5006  the conversation in the same line by inquiring of Mrs. Fairhew further
5007  particulars about the strange creature with whom he had made tryst.
5008  
5009  "Was Mr. Wrenmarsh always as peculiar as he is now?" he asked.
5010  
5011  "I'm not able to tell you that," she returned, "as I have no means of
5012  knowing how much he has changed; but when I knew him he was the most
5013  extraordinary creature. He was always offended if people didn't notice
5014  his eccentricities, and if they did he jibed at their provincialism. He
5015  said he had to become an Englishman because our civilization was so
5016  crude, and he never forgave Bostonians for being so little concerned by
5017  his change of nationality."
5018  
5019  "You seem to have picked up rather a choice acquaintance, Jerry,"
5020  observed Jack good-naturedly.
5021  
5022  "Oh, Mr. Wrenmarsh became utterly impossible," Mrs. Fairhew continued.
5023  "He really had a lot of ability, and I'm told that now he's done some
5024  remarkable things in getting antiques for the British Museum. His own
5025  people couldn't get on with him at all."
5026  
5027  "What an extraordinary creature he must be!" commented Katrine. "Did you
5028  take him for a wild man, Mr. Taberman, when you found him wandering
5029  about among the ruins of Pæstum?"
5030  
5031  "No," Jerry returned, rather regretting that he had continued the talk
5032  about Mr. Wrenmarsh. "He came into the little hovel of an inn there
5033  while I was trying to get something to eat."
5034  
5035  "Well, anyway I hope he'll get his things safe," she added. "They're
5036  his, and the government has no right to interfere with him."
5037  
5038  "I hope he may," Tab responded rather dispiritedly.
5039  
5040  Breakfast being ended, the ladies betook themselves to their rooms to
5041  rest after the fatigues of their night of travel.
5042  
5043  "If I were a billionaire," Mrs. Fairhew observed, "I would never go
5044  anywhere by night except on my own private car. All sleepers are an
5045  abomination, and I hate the thought of who may have been in the
5046  compartment when I have to sleep in it. I hope we shall see you at
5047  dinner, Mr. Taberman?"
5048  
5049  "Thank you," Jerry answered, "but I have business to-night. I assure you
5050  I regret it tremendously."
5051  
5052  "Well," the lady returned over her shoulder as she departed, "at least
5053  we shall expect to see you to-morrow; and I hope you'll leave us Mr.
5054  Castleport.
5055  
5056  "Glad to," laughed Jerry, with a nod; and the men were left to
5057  themselves.
5058  
5059  Jerry turned quickly to Jack the moment they were alone, with a look of
5060  earnestness and concern in his face.
5061  
5062  "Cap'n," he said urgently, "come somewhere where we can talk, will you?
5063  We've got heaps to say, and my time's precious."
5064  
5065  "Jerry," cried the other, catching him by the arm, "something has
5066  happened to the Merle!"
5067  
5068  "Not a thing, Jacko. She is as right as a trivet, but I'm in a hurry.
5069  Come on!"
5070  
5071  "Hurry?" echoed Jack, following him in evident disquiet; "what in the
5072  world's up? It can't be mutiny, and if the yacht's all right, I don't
5073  see"--
5074  
5075  "I'll explain," Taberman responded. "I know a jolly little place just
5076  round the corner. Come on."
5077  
5078  Jack suffered himself to be led to a small café which bore the rather
5079  incongruously ambitious name _Albergo del Sole_, and which displayed on
5080  the yellowish wall above its entrance a rising sun, blood-red and most
5081  magnificent as to its rays. At one of the little tables which covered
5082  the sidewalk before this establishment, the pair took their places. Tab
5083  produced his cigarette-case and ordered a glass of vermouth as he
5084  offered his friend a smoke. Jack, with a hardly perceptible compression
5085  of the lips which showed that he was controlling his impatience and
5086  waiting for Tab to speak, rolled his cigarette between his thumb and
5087  forefinger to loosen it, tapped it on the table-top, and lighted it with
5088  great deliberation. Jerry did the same, but with evident nervousness.
5089  
5090  "Jack," said he, "I have been, and gone, and done it, for fair!"
5091  
5092  "What?" inquired Jack in a tone mildly incisive.
5093  
5094  "Well, you see--it's this way," Tab answered. "Of course I haven't
5095  really done anything yet, but I think I'm bound to, and if you don't
5096  think so--Well, you can see it'll be devilish hard on me as well as
5097  him."
5098  
5099  Jack blew a smoke-ring, and looked at Jerry with a queer smile.
5100  
5101  "It must be something pretty bad, Jerry," he said, "if you don't dare
5102  tell me what it is."
5103  
5104  Jerry looked at him a minute, and then broke into a grin.
5105  
5106  "Why," he said, more at his ease, "it's that damned archæologist, that
5107  bedlamite Wrenmarsh I was talking about at the hotel. Well, not having
5108  anything else to do, I went down to Pæstum to see the temples and kill
5109  time, and I fell into his clutches. I had a lot of talk with him, or he
5110  did with me. He knows a pile about the temples, and he did the showman
5111  in great shape. Incidentally he told me all about his own affairs. I
5112  didn't ask him, mind you. He just did it off his own bat. I couldn't
5113  help that, now could I?"
5114  
5115  "I don't see how you could," Jack assented; "and no more do I see why
5116  you should want to."
5117  
5118  "Why, a chap down there--a Dago peasant, you know--has turned up a
5119  dreadful mess of stuff Wrenmarsh has bought. I told you all that at
5120  breakfast."
5121  
5122  "Yes," Jack said imperturbably.
5123  
5124  "You see, Wrenmarsh turned to and bought the whole slithering lot of it,
5125  and he's just crazy over it; but as I said at the hotel, he's up against
5126  the government, and he doesn't know how under the heavens he's going to
5127  get the loot out of Italy."
5128  
5129  "Great Scott, Tab, did you undertake to run his things out of the
5130  country for him? In the Merle, too?" cried Jack, at last showing some
5131  consternation.
5132  
5133  "It's not quite so bad as that," Jerry protested; "but I did tell him
5134  I'd help him out of Pæstum and up here. Naples is all I agreed to.
5135  That's all he asked."
5136  
5137  Castleport smoked in silence a moment, looking decidedly grave.
5138  
5139  "Jack, old man," Jerry said pleadingly, "I've been an awful ass, but the
5140  way that beastly Wrenmarsh snarled me up with his talk was perfectly
5141  inconceivable. He'd have talked the tail off a brass monkey. He kept
5142  appealing to my sense of honor and heaven knows what, until I felt that
5143  I'd be a perfect cad not to help him."
5144  
5145  "That's all right, Tab," Jack answered thoughtfully. "It's only the
5146  Merle--I should hate awfully to get her into a mess."
5147  
5148  "He assured me that nothing could happen to her, and I don't think he'd
5149  lie."
5150  
5151  "Well, if that's so, there's no great harm done, old man. What are you
5152  worrying over?"
5153  
5154  "I'm not worrying at all, Jacko, if you don't object to my keeping my
5155  word. Just continue my letters of marque until to-morrow. I promised him
5156  I'd go down this afternoon. You will be in command, of course, now
5157  you're here; but I'd hate to think of the poor wretch waiting down there
5158  in the marshes for me--it's an awful place for malaria!--and I not
5159  coming at all."
5160  
5161  "Oh, I shan't interfere," Jack said quickly. "I had made up my mind to
5162  stay on shore one night more anyway, and I really gave you the yacht
5163  till the twentieth. You shall run this thing yourself; but, by Jove, to
5164  think of Uncle Randolph's Merle in business like that!"
5165  
5166  "We started out to be pirates anyway," laughed Jerry, "and we haven't
5167  lived up to our reputation so far. Well, I'll try it. I shall be rid of
5168  the beggar by ten o'clock to-morrow, wind and weather permitting. It's
5169  awful good of you, old man. I thought you'd think I was a bally-ass to
5170  let myself be bamboozled that way; but when he was talking to me I felt
5171  as if he was being awfully bully-ragged, and I ought to help him out."
5172  
5173  "Of course," was Jack's response. "Didn't you notice how Katrine had
5174  exactly the same feeling, just from your telling about it?"
5175  
5176  Tab felt like winking to himself, but he preserved a grave countenance,
5177  and only asked,--
5178  
5179  "What will you tell Mrs. Fairhew about the Merle's being away?"
5180  
5181  "Oh, that 's simple enough. I'll tell her you wanted to visit Pæstum
5182  again, and you can say afterward that you ran across Wrenmarsh and
5183  brought him up to Naples. Twig it?"
5184  
5185  "Clear as a bell. Come down and see me off."
5186  
5187  He sprang from his chair with animation, greatly relieved that the
5188  captain had not prevented him from carrying out his plan. As Jack rose
5189  also, Jerry laid his hand affectionately on his shoulder.
5190  
5191  "It's awfully good of you, old man," he said.
5192  
5193  "Nonsense. It's a mighty little thing to do for you, when you came
5194  across the Atlantic for me."
5195  
5196  "Oh, rats!" Tab rejoined inelegantly. "I came for the fun of it."
5197  
5198  They paid the reckoning, and made their way to the quay, where for an
5199  hour and a half the boat had been waiting for Jerry. The men were
5200  lolling about in the stray corners of shade available, smoking and
5201  sleepily exchanging occasional remarks; but at the sight of the captain
5202  they woke up at once.
5203  
5204  "Here's the skipper," cried one, jumping to his feet and saluting.
5205  
5206  The others followed his example with alacrity, and Jack could not but be
5207  gratified by the unmistakable pleasure they showed at seeing him again.
5208  
5209  "How are you, boys?" he said cheerily. "Glad to see you all. You seem to
5210  be in fighting trim, the whole lot of you."
5211  
5212  "We're bang up, sir," responded Dave, with a grin. "'Tain't the kind o'
5213  weather we left home in, sir."
5214  
5215  "Not exactly," Jack responded laughingly, as he took his place in the
5216  stern-sheets; "but I hope you don't miss the fog too much. Oars!"
5217  
5218  Jack stayed on the Merle for an hour and a half, reading the log and
5219  exchanging with Jerry all the news that either could rake up. Gonzague
5220  made errands into the cabin evidently for the purpose of feasting his
5221  eyes on his master, and beamed with delight at every word Castleport
5222  spoke to him. When the old man found that the captain had not come to
5223  remain, he looked so doleful that Castleport rallied him about not
5224  liking Tab as a skipper.
5225  
5226  "Eet ees not dat," Gonzague responded, with eloquent hands and
5227  shoulders; "he ees fine as de seelk, but--but Mistaire Taberman he ees
5228  not zee capataine you."
5229  
5230  Jerry was anxious to make an early start for Pæstum, as the wind was
5231  light, so Jack took his leave with hearty wishes for a prosperous run.
5232  Jerry went with him to the steps.
5233  
5234  "By the way, Jack," he asked in an undertone, as the captain was about
5235  to descend to take his place in the cutter, "are congratulations in
5236  order?"
5237  
5238  Castleport looked away from his friend toward where, across the bay, in
5239  a dim haze of purple, stood Capri. Then he glanced quickly into Jerry's
5240  eyes.
5241  
5242  "I--I haven't said anything to her," he answered simply.
5243  
5244  He ran down the steps to the cutter. Gonzague himself had taken the
5245  boat-hook to hold the craft steady. Castleport put his hand kindly on
5246  the old man's shoulder.
5247  
5248  "Good-by, Gonzague," he said. "I'm coming aboard for keeps to-morrow.
5249  Good-by, Jerry."
5250  
5251  "Good-by, and--good luck," called Tab in reply, as the cutter started
5252  away.
5253  
5254  
5255  It lacked a quarter of an hour to twelve that night when the Merle hove
5256  to a cable's length off Pæstum. The wind had freshened at sundown, and
5257  was blowing a smart breeze from the west. Jerry had the cutter lowered,
5258  and, leaving Gonzague in charge, with stringent orders to keep the yacht
5259  lying where she was, had himself pulled toward the shore. The men had no
5260  notion what was going on, but they obeyed orders with a prompt alacrity
5261  which showed that they felt that something of unusual import was in this
5262  business. When the cutter was within about a hundred feet of the shore,
5263  Tab ordered the men to lie on their oars, and keep watch for a light. In
5264  silence and utter darkness, for though the stars were shining there was
5265  no moon, they tossed about in the black troughs of the sea for twenty
5266  minutes. Then Dave uttered a guarded exclamation.
5267  
5268  "There's a light, sir," he said. "See, there it is again."
5269  
5270  "Lay her head for it, and pull!" commanded Jerry, feeling as if he were
5271  in a pirate novel. "No noise, mind!"
5272  
5273  The light had appeared for an instant some two or three hundred feet up
5274  the shore from the point off which the cutter lay rolling. They pulled
5275  quietly for the spot, the oars sounding softly, the water lapping the
5276  bows of the boat, and the wind bringing to their ears the muffled rote
5277  as of a sand beach.
5278  
5279  "Let her run," ordered Tab in an undertone. "Can you see the light?"
5280  
5281  For a minute they rolled in darkness as before, and then again sighted
5282  the signal, this time straight in shore. Jerry felt his heart beat as he
5283  gave the order to run in, and a consciousness of romantic adventure,
5284  lawless and wild, was like a sweet and exhilarating flavor in his mouth.
5285  Such a deed on his native shores would have had an atmosphere of secret
5286  villany about it, but here, in alien waters, on a foreign coast, under
5287  the darkness of night, the romantic side was intensified a
5288  thousand-fold. A whimsical feeling flitted through the back of his head
5289  that he ought to be dressed differently for such an occasion; that he
5290  should have had a shaggy black beard, a red sash stuck full of pistols,
5291  and half a dozen cutlasses disposed promiscuously about his person. He
5292  was not without a fleeting consciousness that some time he might at
5293  home, to the old crowd of college boys, find a keen joy in telling of
5294  this night, and--But the light flashed out again, this time so near that
5295  the cutter lay full in the middle of the dark, fire-sprinkled path it
5296  illumined; and Jerry's entire mind was called back to the business in
5297  hand. He could in the light see the cheeks of the men in front of him as
5298  they swayed with their rowing, the brass rowlocks of the cutter, and the
5299  dripping blades of the oars. He strained his eyes toward the land, but
5300  was blinded by the glare into which he looked; and on the instant a
5301  voice, eager but subdued, hailed from the shore some twenty feet away.
5302  
5303  "Hallo! Are you there, Mr. Taberman?"
5304  
5305  "Here all right," answered Jerry. "Eyes in the boat!" he added sharply
5306  to the men, every one of whom except Dave had turned to look ashore.
5307  "Three good strokes now: Stroke! Stroke! Stroke!... Let her run!"
5308  
5309  The nose of the cutter ground on a sand-beach; the bowsman sprang ashore
5310  with the painter and held her, while Jerry clambered forward, steadying
5311  himself with a hand on the shoulder of the rowers. On leaping to the
5312  land, he was confronted by Mr. Wrenmarsh. That gentleman shifted the
5313  lantern he held from his right hand to his left, and shook hands with
5314  Taberman fervently.
5315  
5316  "You're just in time," he said hurriedly. "We haven't a second to lose.
5317  The boxes are right here on the edge of the grass. Come on with your
5318  men. It'll take four of them for that biggest box."
5319  
5320  Jerry called the four men who were nearest, and telling the rest to
5321  stand by, he hurried up the beach. In the sand, by the light of the
5322  lantern with which the archæologist came after him, he saw the print of
5323  wheels leading up to a pile of rude wooden cases. Three of them were of
5324  moderate size, but the fourth looked to Tab huge in the semi-darkness.
5325  
5326  "How big is that thing?" he asked, touching it with his foot.
5327  
5328  "Don't kick it!" Wrenmarsh responded quickly and sharply. "It's only
5329  about a metre square and half as deep. I couldn't make it any smaller."
5330  
5331  Jerry whistled with dismay.
5332  
5333  "We may lose it overboard on the way to the Merle," he remarked cruelly.
5334  Then without heeding the dismayed exclamation of the collector, he
5335  ordered the men to take that first. "Put it as far astern as you can,"
5336  he said. "I'm afraid you'll have to wade in with it."
5337  
5338  "For God's sake hurry," cried Wrenmarsh. "I know that beastly carter has
5339  put the custodi up to the job by this time. Only don't drop that case!"
5340  he added, running along by the side of the bearers with the lantern
5341  swinging wildly to and fro and bumping against his legs.
5342  
5343  The case was evidently pretty heavy, and the men breathed deep as they
5344  carried it across the loose sand. By dint of the men's wading in beside
5345  the cutter the big box was safely deposited in the stern-sheets, and the
5346  sailors went back for a new load. A second box was stowed without
5347  trouble, but as the two others, which were fortunately the smallest,
5348  were being lifted by two men each, Wrenmarsh clutched Taberman by the
5349  arm.
5350  
5351  "Look there!" he cried. "Look there! Quick, men! For God's sake, quick!"
5352  
5353  Not more than a hundred yards away on the beach to the southward was an
5354  advancing lantern. Suddenly it stopped.
5355  
5356  "What is it?" asked Tab.
5357  
5358  The men, spurred on by Wrenmarsh, were fairly running across the sand,
5359  and Tab skurried along with them toward the boat.
5360  
5361  "Hurry! Hurry!" was the breathless response of Wrenmarsh. "It's the
5362  custodi and the police--those cursed _carabinieri_! I told you the
5363  carter'd sell me out."
5364  
5365  It was only a minute before the men had reached the boat, and hurriedly
5366  stowed the boxes they carried. Taberman and Wrenmarsh scrambled in, and
5367  Jerry, sitting in a distorted and cramped position behind the big box,
5368  got hold of the lines. The men pushed off, and got into their places
5369  anyhow. Just as Tab opened his lips to order the men to give way, a
5370  peremptory voice came to them from the shore to the south. The light had
5371  not advanced from where they had seen it stop, but it had gone waving
5372  wildly up and down the beach as if the bearers had encountered some
5373  impassable obstacle and sought in vain for a place which would allow a
5374  passage.
5375  
5376  "_Aspetta!_" bawled the voice. "_Aspetta nel nomme del Re!_"
5377  
5378  "What's that?" asked Jerry.
5379  
5380  "They're calling us to stand--in the king's name," Mr. Wrenmarsh
5381  returned with sullen nervousness.
5382  
5383  "Head the boat 'round," cried Tab. "Why the devil don't they come down
5384  if they want us?"
5385  
5386  "I can't imagine," the collector answered.
5387  
5388  "Perhaps they're afraid of us; but I don't think that can be it."
5389  
5390  "_Aspetta!_" thundered the voice on shore more savagely. "_Aspetta o
5391  tiriamo!_"
5392  
5393  "By Jove! The sands!" cried Wrenmarsh. "There's a brook there--the
5394  bottom's quicksand. They daren't try to cross."
5395  
5396  "Quicksand?" echoed Tab. "How'd they come there, then?"
5397  
5398  "They must have thought we were on the other side of the stream. They've
5399  come up on the wrong bank, and now they can't get over."
5400  
5401  Bang! There was a quick, loud report, and Jerry heard the _wht_ of a
5402  carbine ball close astern.
5403  
5404  "Great Scott!" he shouted. "Douse that glim! Pull! Pull!"
5405  
5406  Wrenmarsh seized the lantern and dipped it overboard, an effective if
5407  irregular way of quenching it.
5408  
5409  Bang! Bang! Two more shots. One of the men, Hunter, pulling on the third
5410  thwart, afterward swore that he felt the wind of the second bullet.
5411  
5412  Bang!
5413  
5414  "Pull hard, men! Steady!" cried Jerry.
5415  
5416  A man of race and training, while in a crisis of this sort he feels more
5417  excitement than his thicker-skinned fellows, displays more outward
5418  coolness. Social development means the power of self-control, especially
5419  when any sense of responsibility is involved. Taberman was inwardly wild
5420  with the stirring emotions of an experience such as he not only had
5421  never encountered but of which he had heard in a hundred ways which lent
5422  associations to heighten the effect; yet he did not lose for a moment
5423  his sense of having the men to care for. He kept his head, and called
5424  the stroke for the rowers. They showed by their tendency to pull wildly
5425  how near they were to demoralization, and Jerry urged them to steadiness
5426  with language of the most picturesque emphasis.
5427  
5428  Bang! Bang! Bang! Three shots. At the third there was a sharp rap, as if
5429  the cutter had been hit by a pebble, and a queer little squeak of
5430  splintering wood. Tab started up, but instantly sat down again, catching
5431  at the yoke-line he had half let fall.
5432  
5433  "Close call," Wrenmarsh said nervously.
5434  
5435  "Yes," Jerry answered laconically. "Stroke! Stroke! Steady!"
5436  
5437  At the instant he had heard the sound of the ball on the wood of the
5438  boat, he had felt a sharp twinge in his left arm, as if the muscle had
5439  been suddenly tweaked off the bone by a pair of white-hot pincers. The
5440  pain was exquisite, but he forced himself to keep calm, and beyond the
5441  first involuntary spring he gave no indication that he had been hit. In
5442  a sort of double consciousness he kept saying to himself that he
5443  wondered how severe the hurt was, and at the same time he seemed to be
5444  lifted by sheer will and excitement above even the physical feeling of
5445  the moment.
5446  
5447  "Steady!" he said, and was queerly conscious of a sort of exultation
5448  that his voice was so strong and natural. "We're 'most out of range."
5449  
5450  Other shots followed, but they splashed harmlessly astern. The darkness
5451  was a shelter, and although the carbines flashed again and again from
5452  the shore, no more damage was done on board the cutter. Ahead of them
5453  Tab, holding himself together grimly, saw the red and green
5454  sailing-lights of the Merle, and realized that at the sound of the
5455  firing Gonzague must have run the yacht in shore.
5456  
5457  "Ahoy!" Jerry called.
5458  
5459  Tears of pain suffused his eyes in spite of him, and made the colored
5460  lights big and blurry, as if they were the glaring orbs of some huge
5461  dragon.
5462  
5463  "Hollá!" came Gonzague's voice. "A'right, sair!" and with a deafening
5464  boom of canvas the schooner luffed up.
5465  
5466  Jerry put his right arm behind him, his left hanging limply, and
5467  getting hold of the rudder-yoke he laid the cutter alongside the yacht.
5468  He and Wrenmarsh got up to the deck, a davit was turned out-board as a
5469  crane and the boxes hoisted, and then the boat slung up.
5470  
5471  Faint and savage with pain, Jerry still fought with himself to keep up,
5472  and to fulfill his duties as commander. He remembered that his order for
5473  the Merle to lie to where she was had been disregarded; and though he
5474  was inwardly glad that the yacht had been brought to meet the cutter, he
5475  felt that discipline was discipline, and he was in no mood to let any
5476  infringement of orders go unnoted. He called Gonzague.
5477  
5478  "What's the meaning of this?" he demanded fiercely. "Didn't I give
5479  orders to keep the yacht hove to till I came out?"
5480  
5481  "Yes, sair," Gonzague answered contritely, stroking his stiff white
5482  mustache with nervous fingers, "bot I heer de shotin' ashore, an'"--
5483  
5484  "That made no difference. I'm ashamed that an old seaman like you should
5485  disobey orders simply because he heard a row ashore. Go forward. I shall
5486  mark you in the log."
5487  
5488  The old man took himself off without a word. However much he was likely
5489  to feel the sting of this reproof, he was not the man to fail to
5490  respect the mate for it, and of this Tab might be assured when he had
5491  the calmness to think things over.
5492  
5493  Jerry gave the helmsman the course for Naples, and the Merle swung off
5494  on her return. Then he started to go below, but now that the need of
5495  immediate action was over he suddenly turned sick and dizzy. He put out
5496  his uninjured arm with a quick clutch at Mr. Wrenmarsh.
5497  
5498  "Give me--your arm," he said weakly. "I'm--I'm hit, you know, and things
5499  go round."
5500  
5501  "Hit!" echoed the collector. "Where? Is it serious?"
5502  
5503  "Arm," answered Jerry. "Help me get below."
5504  
5505  The archæologist supported Jerry to the companion, and then almost
5506  carried him down the steps. He tried to place him on the transom, but
5507  Taberman stubbornly walked half the length of the cabin, and sank into a
5508  chair by the table. His lips seemed to him queerly stiff as he twisted
5509  them into a wry smile.
5510  
5511  "Mustn't bleed on the cushions, y' know," he said feebly. "Call
5512  Gonzague."
5513  
5514  Wrenmarsh shouted the name explosively, hovering solicitously over
5515  Jerry, and in a moment the Provençal appeared. Jerry made a mighty
5516  effort to pull himself together.
5517  
5518  "Here, Gonzague," he said, "get the medicine-chest, and strip my coat
5519  off. I've got to be fixed. I want some hot water and a b. and s. Beg
5520  your--pardon," he added, turning slowly to Mr. Wrenmarsh, and confusedly
5521  wishing that the cabin would not turn so much faster than he could. "I'm
5522  forgetting. This gentleman's to have Jack's--the captain's stateroom.
5523  Will you have anything to drink? 'Fraid I'm poor host, but"--
5524  
5525  "No, no," cried the archæologist. "That's all right. The brandy,
5526  Gonzague, quick!"
5527  
5528  A brandy and soda put fresh life into Jerry, who still tried to be
5529  polite, and protested that the collector should not bother.
5530  
5531  "You'll find me a first-class chirurgeon," responded the other. "Where's
5532  the medicine-chest, Gonzague?"
5533  
5534  He proved remarkably ready and efficient and kindly withal. He stripped
5535  off Jerry's jacket and cut away the shirt-sleeve, to discover a two-inch
5536  sliver of African oak from the gunwale of the cutter stabbed into a
5537  jagged hole in the forearm. He probed and cut and trimmed with the skill
5538  of a trained surgeon, while Jerry, pale and with set teeth, bore it all
5539  with Spartan firmness until everything was over, and then, as he tried
5540  to rise when the last bandage was in place, fainted dead away.
5541  
5542  When the plucky mate had been brought round and stowed away in his
5543  berth, Gonzague again took charge of the Merle, and dropped her anchor
5544  once more in the harbor of Naples at about eight o'clock in the morning.
5545  
5546  Just before Mr. Wrenmarsh turned in for the night, he put his head into
5547  the door of Jerry's stateroom to ask if he could do anything for him.
5548  
5549  "No, thank you," Jerry returned. "Much obliged; but the man by my door
5550  will hear if I want anything. I'm all right now. I'm jolly much obliged
5551  to you for fixing me up."
5552  
5553  "'Pon my word, Table--Taberman, you're the most extraordinary man for a
5554  Bostonian I ever saw. Good-night."
5555  
5556  "Good-night," Jerry responded. Then he chuckled, and added, "But
5557  Boston's full of better men than I am, if you'd only stayed there to see
5558  'em."
5559  
5560  [Illustration: Decoration]
5561  
5562  
5563  
5564  
5565  Chapter Twelve
5566  
5567  AT VERGIL'S TOMB
5568  
5569  
5570  "I never could touch it," Katrine said, with an emphatic shake of her
5571  head. "I should think a baby brought up on goat's milk would run round
5572  and bleat. Why, I think the idea of it is horrid!"
5573  
5574  Her eyes sparkled and her whole air was full of a delicious animation,
5575  so that it was no wonder Jack threw back his head and laughed, as much
5576  in sheer admiration as from amusement. He was in high spirits this
5577  morning, the excitement of a mighty resolve stirring in his blood.
5578  
5579  "How do you know that you haven't been having goat's milk at the hotel?"
5580  he demanded. "Aren't you afraid you'll begin to break out in a baa
5581  yourself all of a sudden?"
5582  
5583  "Why, how rude you are!" she cried, her dimples deepening and shoaling.
5584  "Of course they wouldn't dare to give it to us, and we should know it if
5585  they did!"
5586  
5587  The young people were being driven in a Neapolitan _vettura_ to the
5588  tomb of Vergil. Jack had mentioned the spot that morning at breakfast as
5589  being well worth a visit, if only for the view, and said that the ladies
5590  ought to see it. Mrs. Fairhew had, for reasons perhaps not wholly
5591  unconnected with remembrances of her own youth and the late Mr. Fairhew,
5592  declined to make the jaunt, on the score that it was too hot and that
5593  she had a thousand trifles to attend to. She had refused her niece's
5594  prompt offer of assistance, and so left that young woman free to accept
5595  Jack's invitation that she take the drive with him.
5596  
5597  Their talk was light enough, the lighter because Jack at least hardly
5598  dared to venture to be serious lest he betray how terribly in earnest he
5599  was. The sight of a little flock of goats, which had scattered at the
5600  pistol-like crack of their driver's whip, had given them a theme for a
5601  moment. The agile brown animals skipped along the gutters, assailed by
5602  the effervescent profanity of their conductor, a half-naked, slim-limbed
5603  lad browner than the beasts themselves; and with more detonations of the
5604  whiplash the carriage whirled up the hill with hardly diminished speed
5605  as the grade grew steeper. Through picturesque, squalid streets, braver
5606  in their poverty than many a splendid thoroughfare, through nooks that
5607  seemed to be private courtyards with entire families disposed about
5608  them, the carriage took its way noisily; it turned now to the left, now
5609  to the right, continually ascending; it brought them to the top of
5610  narrow ways down which they looked as through a kaleidoscope gleaming
5611  with a confusion of gay colors; it seemed about to land them on the roof
5612  of some building which lay directly before them, and then at the last
5613  moment whisked around some unseen corner and carried them still higher.
5614  
5615  "Isn't it wonderful," Katrine said. "I never saw such a city. I feel
5616  almost as if we were in a flying-machine,--we keep going up so and see
5617  such wonderful sights all the time. Oh, do look down that street! Did
5618  you ever see such colors?"
5619  
5620  "It is stunning," Castleport answered, his eyes on her face.
5621  
5622  "You didn't look at it at all," she said half pouting, as the carriage
5623  whirled them past.
5624  
5625  "Oh, I could see it all in your eyes," he returned. "You don't know what
5626  excellent mirrors they are."
5627  
5628  "What nonsense! How silly you are this morning!"
5629  
5630  Her color deepened, however, and Jack did not feel that his remark had
5631  missed fire. He smiled to himself, and just then the carriage brought
5632  up with a jerk on the left side of the way, in front of a small green
5633  door in a gray retaining-wall. Over the door was printed in black
5634  letters: _Tomba di Virgilio_.
5635  
5636  "Here we are," Jack said.
5637  
5638  He got out with the field-glasses he had brought, and extended his hand
5639  to assist Katrine. She hardly touched his arm with her finger-tips, but
5640  the air was electric, and he felt the thrill like a pulse of warm blood
5641  from head to foot. He did not speak to the driver, but with a manner
5642  that made that piratical Neapolitan regard him with a new respect simply
5643  ordered him in the sign-language of the town to remain in waiting. A
5644  soldier came slouching out of a shop near by wherein he was evidently
5645  lounging, took the prescribed gate-fees, and then opened the narrow
5646  door. This disclosed a staircase, strait and steep, cut from the living
5647  rock, which led upward and to the right.
5648  
5649  They climbed the stone stairs without speaking, but at the top the
5650  wonderful beauty of the view which burst upon them called from Katrine
5651  an involuntary exclamation of surprise and delight. Below them,
5652  red-roofed and multi-colored, Naples lay bathed in the strong white
5653  light of the southern sun; beyond, marvelously blue and ruffled by a
5654  gentle breeze, the waters of the bay flashed and sparkled; and beyond
5655  again, farther yet, stood purple Capri and the piled-up southern shore,
5656  luminous and mistily azure. To the eastward, brooding and tragic, yet
5657  with a thrilling beauty of its own in softly flowing curves and wavering
5658  outline, showed Vesuvius, and stupendous as it was, seemed crouching
5659  sinister and awful, the incarnation of pitiless power.
5660  
5661  Jack focused the glasses, and handed them to Katrine. Then he began to
5662  point here and there, showing her the different things of interest
5663  visible from the spur of the hill on which they were standing. As she
5664  was looking toward the Mole and the New Harbor, suddenly she uttered a
5665  little cry of surprise.
5666  
5667  "There's the Merle," she said. "I'm sure it is. At least she's flying
5668  the American flag."
5669  
5670  "Yes," Jack responded. "That's she, fast enough."
5671  
5672  "Doesn't it seem like a bit of home to see her down there?" Katrine went
5673  on. "I think it was perfectly wonderful that Mr. Drake let you take her
5674  this summer."
5675  
5676  Jack gave a quick movement of the shoulders, and then set his lips
5677  together more firmly.
5678  
5679  "I shall have to tell her the whole thing," he thought to himself. Aloud
5680  he said, "I shouldn't have been here when you were if it hadn't been
5681  for having the Merle."
5682  
5683  "I suppose not," she answered, and the change in her tone showed most
5684  clearly that she understood in the words more than met the ear.
5685  
5686  After they had stood for a time in admiration of the magnificent view
5687  before them, they turned to go to the tomb, twenty yards away. The
5688  uneven path, bordered by beautiful wild poppies and violets, was shaded
5689  by gnarled fig and plum trees. A splendid stone-pine rose superb on the
5690  left, crowned by its dome-shaped cluster of branches.
5691  
5692  "Oh," Katrine cried, "it's perfectly beautiful, isn't it? It makes you
5693  feel solemn, it's so lovely."
5694  
5695  "Yes," he assented, and unwonted emotion left him with no word to add.
5696  
5697  "Just look at those flowers," she went on. "What a pity it is that we
5698  don't have them like that at home."
5699  
5700  "It's a fitting place for Vergil to be buried in, isn't it?" Jack said.
5701  "I thought you would like it."
5702  
5703  "It is a place I shall remember all my life," she replied. Her eyes met
5704  his as she spoke, and her glance fell with quick consciousness. Before
5705  he could speak, she added hurriedly, "Is this the tomb?"
5706  
5707  "Yes," he answered, entirely undisturbed by any chilling scholastic
5708  doubts on the subject, "this is the tomb."
5709  
5710  Before them was a lowly structure of old rubble, four square, and a
5711  narrow door, at which the path, with a sudden dip, came to an end.
5712  
5713  "Will you go in?" he said, standing aside.
5714  
5715  Katrine entered, and he followed. The place was as simple within as
5716  without. The floor seemed to be of beaten earth; the single room, or
5717  _cella_, was lighted by a small window, and it contained only two or
5718  three cinerary urns of dark red clay, which leaned against the wall
5719  opposite the door. Above these, in brown letters on a tablet of white
5720  marble, was an inscription set there by the Academy of France.
5721  
5722  The pair stood silent for a minute, Katrine reading the tablet, and
5723  Jack, his head bared, standing beside her. As she turned her head she
5724  caught for a second time his glance. She colored, and moved quickly to
5725  the small window.
5726  
5727  "Isn't the view wonderful!" she said, as if she had caught at the first
5728  words that came into her mind.
5729  
5730  "Yes," he returned absently. "Fine, isn't it?"
5731  
5732  She looked a moment out of the window, and then, avoiding his eyes, she
5733  turned back to the Latin distich cut in the tablet, and by tradition
5734  assigned to Vergil himself:--
5735  
5736  
5737       Mantua me genuit, Calabri rapuere, tenet nunc
5738       Parthenope. Cecini pascua, rura, duces.
5739  
5740  
5741  "You'll think I am unspeakably stupid," she said, "but I confess I
5742  cannot make it out. 'Mantua gave me birth,' I can read that."
5743  
5744  "'The Calabrian winds carried me away,'" Jack went on.
5745  
5746  "Oh, yes; but I don't understand the Parthenope."
5747  
5748  "That's Naples," he answered. "'Naples holds me.'"
5749  
5750  "Oh, is that it? I know the rest. 'I sang pastures, fields, leaders.'"
5751  
5752  "Good! You shall have an A in the examination in spite of Parthenope,"
5753  he assured her. "Perhaps 'heroes' is a better word for _duces_, though."
5754  
5755  "I'm afraid I don't deserve an A," she laughed, "but I am satisfied if I
5756  pass at all."
5757  
5758  As they came out of the tomb Jack picked a spray from the beautiful
5759  laurel growing beside the entrance, and held it out to her. She took it
5760  with a murmured word of thanks, and put it in her gown. Not far away on
5761  the right of the path was a rude seat or bench, shaded by fig and olive
5762  trees, and partially screened from the path by dwarf plums. It was
5763  slightly higher than the way by which they had come.
5764  
5765  "Here," Jack said, "let's go up and rest a bit. The view is worth
5766  seeing."
5767  
5768  They turned to the seat and took their places in silence. The view was
5769  not perceptibly different from that which they had on the path, but as
5770  Jack looked at Katrine and Katrine cast down her eyes, this was not a
5771  matter which they were likely to notice.
5772  
5773  "Katrine," the captain began,--for they had come, almost by insensible
5774  degrees, to call each other by their Christian names,--"I've got to tell
5775  you something. It isn't altogether pleasant for me, but it's only fair
5776  that you should know."
5777  
5778  She looked up at him in evident surprise and with some disquiet.
5779  
5780  "Why, what is it?" she asked. "I hope it isn't anything really
5781  terrible."
5782  
5783  He hesitated, and began to scrape the ground with his foot nervously.
5784  
5785  "I--er--Well, to be honest, I don't know exactly how to tell you so you
5786  won't be too hard on me," he answered frankly.
5787  
5788  "Is it so bad?" she queried in a tone which showed some concern under
5789  its assumed lightness.
5790  
5791  "What in the world have you been doing? You haven't been murdering
5792  anybody, I hope."
5793  
5794  "What would you say," asked Jack, "what would you think of a man that
5795  acted like this? Suppose a case. Suppose the chap was, in the first
5796  place, in America. Suppose he had a friend, a friend he cared a lot
5797  about, one he thought more of than anybody else in the world, and that
5798  friend was on this side. Suppose the man's property was all tied up,--in
5799  trust, you know,--and he'd promised not to borrow, so he couldn't
5800  honorably raise the money to come over unless his trustee would let him.
5801  The trustee, we'll say, is a nice old fellow,--really nice, you know,
5802  only rather crotchety,--who wouldn't hear a word of the chap's going."
5803  
5804  He stopped as if for encouragement, and Katrine, with evident
5805  appreciation of this, murmured, "Yes, I understand."
5806  
5807  "And suppose," Castleport went on, a new hesitancy coming into his
5808  voice, "that this trustee--of course the chap is his nearest relative,
5809  you know--has an able schooner yacht. Now if the chap simply couldn't
5810  stand it, but captured that yacht--not violently, of course, but by
5811  stratagem,--and came over to see his friend, and to ask her"--
5812  
5813  "Why, Jack Castleport!" cried Katrine, with eyes open to their widest.
5814  "You don't mean that you ran away with the Merle! I never can believe
5815  it!"
5816  
5817  "It's true, though," he responded. "Do you blame me so very much?"
5818  
5819  Her glance dropped before his, and her manner instantly lost its
5820  boldness.
5821  
5822  "I--Why, of course that depends," she murmured.
5823  
5824  "Depends on what?"
5825  
5826  "On--how--how necessary it was for him to see his friend."
5827  
5828  "Oh," Jack cried. "I had to see her! You know I had to come, Katrine! I
5829  had to tell you I love you, and I stole Uncle Randolph's yacht because
5830  he wouldn't let me come any other way. I had to come."
5831  
5832  He sprang up in his excitement, and stood before her, his hands twisting
5833  each other in a way odd enough for one of so much self-control.
5834  
5835  "You must have known how I cared for you, Katrine. I couldn't tell you
5836  without making a clean breast of this, but don't be too hard on me. I
5837  had to come."
5838  
5839  She flashed up at him the merest hair's-breadth of a glance, and with
5840  her hands pressed to her bosom, said softly, "I never could have
5841  forgiven you if you hadn't come."
5842  
5843  He simply stooped over and took her unceremoniously in his arms, and it
5844  was several moments before she had breath and presence of mind to
5845  protest.
5846  
5847  "Heavens!" she cried with mock terror. "Am I in the arms of a pirate?
5848  Jack, I never knew anything so shocking in my life! How could you do
5849  it?"
5850  
5851  "I had to get across the Atlantic to you," he answered, as if that were
5852  an excuse all-sufficient.
5853  
5854  And the sun shone down on the sea and on Vesuvius and on Vergil's tomb,
5855  and on that which is more enduring than all these,--the sweetness of
5856  young love.
5857  
5858  [Illustration: Decoration]
5859  
5860  
5861  
5862  
5863  Chapter Thirteen
5864  
5865  A BID FOR THE ODD TRICK
5866  
5867  
5868  While the captain was looking with Katrine down on the Merle, as the
5869  yacht lay quietly at anchor in the harbor, a notable conversation was
5870  taking place on board. At no very early hour Tab had risen, tubbed with
5871  difficulty, and, with some aid, got into his clothes. His left arm was
5872  stiff and very sore, but beyond that he felt no discomfort. His
5873  magnificent physique, improved by the hardy life he had been leading,
5874  saved him from any consequences more serious; so that the archæologist,
5875  who was in capital spirits, rallied him on the prodigious appetite he
5876  displayed at breakfast.
5877  
5878  "I have to eat double to make up for the blood I lost last night," Jerry
5879  said, with a grin. "I find there's nothing for the appetite like a
5880  regular brush with the police. I've found it so before, when I was in
5881  college."
5882  
5883  After breakfast the two went on deck, and seated under the awning, with
5884  the beautiful bay before them and a soft air to bring a delicious
5885  coolness, they talked over the adventure of the previous night. Then
5886  from this they branched off to more general matters. Mr. Wrenmarsh was a
5887  man of wide experience and of good observation, and was well informed on
5888  almost every topic the talk touched upon. His tricks and eccentricities
5889  had been for the time being laid aside, or showed only as a flavor of
5890  personality piquant and attractive. Jerry found himself soothed and
5891  entertained, although, remembering his previous experience with the
5892  collector, he was not without a feeling that Wrenmarsh had a propensity
5893  to use speech as a squid does his ink, to conceal his course, and so
5894  wondered what the collector had still to gain. Wrenmarsh suddenly took
5895  to intricate and unintelligible sentences without warning and equally
5896  without apparent excuse, when Jerry brought him back to earth with a
5897  question what he intended to do next.
5898  
5899  "Do?" exclaimed Wrenmarsh, as if shocked and astonished by such an
5900  inquiry. "Of course I shan't think of setting foot on shore again till I
5901  get to England."
5902  
5903  Jerry hardly suppressed an instinctive whistle, and for a brief instant
5904  he had nothing to say; but after all he was not without a shrewdness of
5905  his own. He was still chagrined to remember that the archæologist had
5906  played upon him once for his own purposes, and he had at least learned
5907  that in dealing with this man it was necessary to be cautious.
5908  
5909  "To England?" he repeated in a voice so casual as to rouse Wrenmarsh and
5910  to tickle himself inwardly. "How do you go?"
5911  
5912  "Go?" once more echoed the other. "With you, of course."
5913  
5914  "Oh, are we going to England?" Jerry asked more carelessly than before.
5915  
5916  "Surely you are," Wrenmarsh retorted with some sharpness.
5917  
5918  "Are we really?" was Jerry's comment. A refrain from a song in a Pudding
5919  play popped into his head, and he hummed it in derision hardly
5920  disguised,--
5921  
5922  
5923       "You surprise me!"
5924  
5925  
5926  "Will you--er--say that again?" asked the collector most courteously.
5927  
5928  "Oh, quite unnecessary," Tab returned, not to be trapped into an
5929  apology. "It was only a bit of a song."
5930  
5931  He was filled with a pleasant feeling that he was bothering the
5932  collector, astute as that person was, and he determined, as the
5933  circumstances certainly were in his favor, to hold his own with him
5934  this time at least.
5935  
5936  "I don't think you have a very clear view of the case," Wrenmarsh said,
5937  after a moment of silent musing with contracted brow. "If you had, you'd
5938  see that it isn't possible for me to go ashore now, after that beastly
5939  business of last night. I assure you, I'm awfully sorry for that mess.
5940  There's another thing,--I couldn't get those boxes ashore from the yacht
5941  without their being examined, and then there'd be the devil of a row."
5942  
5943  "That must have occurred to you before you left Pæstum," Jerry remarked
5944  with coolness.
5945  
5946  Mr. Wrenmarsh did not move a muscle.
5947  
5948  "So it did," he said blandly; "but of course I knew it must have been
5949  evident to you also."
5950  
5951  Jerry laughed in spite of himself at the cool impudence of this.
5952  
5953  "I confess that it wasn't," he responded.
5954  
5955  "Even if it wasn't," the other went on, as smoothly as ever, "I never
5956  for an instant supposed that when once you'd started out to help me,
5957  you'd funk. That is a contingency, I confess, never occurred to my mind.
5958  I thought you were made of different stuff. You were clear game last
5959  night."
5960  
5961  Jerry looked at his guest and burst into deep-throated laughter.
5962  
5963  "Well, for clean cheek!" he cried. "Do you think I'm going to tote you
5964  about in a yacht I don't own for the rest of my life?"
5965  
5966  "Would you like to?" asked the collector, with a fresh aspect of
5967  interest. "Because in the Ægean Sea I've a"--
5968  
5969  "Whatever it is, please keep it to yourself, or you'll insist that I
5970  promised to help you with it," interrupted Tab grimly. "As for going to
5971  England in the present case, that's quite out of the question. What are
5972  you going to do? If you stay on board, you'll land in Boston."
5973  
5974  Mr. Wrenmarsh's face took on for an instant a look distinctly ugly. It
5975  suddenly occurred to Taberman that the collector was in rather an evil
5976  plight,--worse, indeed, than that from which the Merle had rescued him.
5977  
5978  "Surely you're not serious?" Wrenmarsh asked slowly.
5979  
5980  "I think I am," Jerry responded pleasantly. "What are you going to do?"
5981  
5982  "Damn!" the other broke out explosively, lying back in his chair and
5983  running his fingers through his gray-sprinkled locks.
5984  
5985  Jerry was too soft-hearted not to be touched by the other's perplexity,
5986  but an involuntary movement of sympathy which he made happened to give
5987  him a painful twinge in the arm, and he hardened his heart. There was a
5988  silence of some minutes, during which he tried to make out from the face
5989  of his companion what thoughts were passing behind that mask. Suddenly
5990  the cloud lifted from the face of Wrenmarsh, and he flashed a bright
5991  glance on Jerry.
5992  
5993  "Bless me," he cried gayly. "I might have thought!
5994  Plutus--Mammon--filthy lucre! But how extraordinary in an American--not
5995  to ask for it, you know! What'll you take for it?"
5996  
5997  "For what?" responded Tab, not catching his drift.
5998  
5999  He had a dreadful feeling that by becoming incomprehensible, the other
6000  might be getting the better of him.
6001  
6002  "What's to pay for a passage of myself and my boxes to--let us say
6003  Plymouth?"
6004  
6005  Indignation for the instant flared up in Jerry.
6006  
6007  "This is not a passenger ship," he responded brusquely.
6008  
6009  "Oh, of course not, my dear fellow; but as every man has his price, I
6010  suppose a yacht has too."
6011  
6012  Common-sense and indignation worked together now to keep Taberman from
6013  an angry retort. It flashed upon him that here was a chance, one in a
6014  thousand, to pay off the hands of the Merle without troubling the
6015  President; it was a chance, too, to score off this cheeky archæologist.
6016  Taberman had already noted that Wrenmarsh was a penurious soul who hated
6017  to part with money, and he felt something of the godly joy of the
6018  departing Israelites when Moses announced the project for the spoiling
6019  of the Egyptians. England was not such an impossible distance off. They
6020  might take the Great Circle track home. Surely if Jack--
6021  
6022  "Don't you see my position, Mr. Wrenmarsh?" he asked. "I haven't the
6023  power to dispose of the Merle. I'm simply in charge of her while the
6024  captain's ashore, don't you see? Still"--
6025  
6026  He paused dramatically.
6027  
6028  "Well?" ejaculated Wrenmarsh, apparently keeping his gaze fixed in the
6029  closest interest on the red sails of a big felucca that was standing in
6030  toward the Mole.
6031  
6032  "Well, I think I might be right in making a sort of conditional--a
6033  purely conditional"--he repeated the word for caution, wondering if he
6034  ought to make it any stronger--"arrangement. It wouldn't be valid
6035  without the sanction of the captain. You see that, of course."
6036  
6037  "Well?" repeated the other.
6038  
6039  "Do you see--merely conditional?" insisted Taberman.
6040  
6041  "Yes, I suppose so," assented the other grudgingly.
6042  
6043  "I might make a sort of conditional arrangement, then, to go to
6044  Plymouth, or perhaps to any other English port not too much out of the
6045  way, for a consideration of"--He paused again.
6046  
6047  "Ten pounds," suggested the archæologist.
6048  
6049  "Two hundred," said Jerry coolly.
6050  
6051  He could have hugged himself with joy at the sound of his own voice
6052  naming the sum in such a matter-of-fact fashion. He knew well enough
6053  that but for the enormous handicap which circumstances had put upon the
6054  archæologist he would have had no chance whatever to outmanoeuvre him,
6055  but this he did not bother to reflect on at the moment and might have
6056  had scruples about if he had. He gave himself up to the delight of
6057  feeling that he had distinctly the better of the man who had so carried
6058  him off his feet at Pæstum, and who had involved him in an affair of the
6059  seriousness of which Jerry had had good reason to meditate in the times
6060  in the night when his arm kept him awake. It was certainly something to
6061  have the upper hand now; and two hundred pounds, which he had named
6062  almost at random, multiplied itself in his head into a most satisfactory
6063  number of dollars.
6064  
6065  "Two hundred pounds!" cried out the archæologist, nearly jumping out of
6066  his chair.
6067  
6068  His affected surprise was dramatic, but unfortunately for its effect it
6069  was overdone, so that even Jerry felt it to be theatrical.
6070  
6071  "Shall we call it two hundred and fifty?" the mate asked, enjoying
6072  himself more every minute.
6073  
6074  "Two hundred and fifty devils!" shouted Wrenmarsh, who appeared more
6075  irritated, it seemed to Jerry, on account of being outmanoeuvred than
6076  because the price was so high.
6077  
6078  "Not devils--pounds," Tab responded, smiling at his own wit.
6079  
6080  "Leave off the two hundred," begged the collector.
6081  
6082  "The agreement is only conditional anyway," Jerry said, with something
6083  of an air, "but if it seems to you fairer, we'll leave off the fifty,
6084  and call it an even two hundred--one for you and one for those precious
6085  boxes, to be paid on arrival. I'm not a Neapolitan. Will you go ashore
6086  here or wait for the captain?"
6087  
6088  "I'll wait for the captain, Mr. Taberman," Wrenmarsh replied. He
6089  glowered across the bay for a moment, and then added, "He may not be so
6090  infernally exorbitant as you are."
6091  
6092  Jerry smiled secretly to himself, and resolved that at least Jack should
6093  be persuaded to make no easier terms. Then he went to write a note to
6094  summon the captain to come aboard to consider this proposition of taking
6095  a passenger.
6096  
6097  [Illustration: Decoration]
6098  
6099  
6100  
6101  
6102  Chapter Fourteen
6103  
6104  CLEARING THE DECKS
6105  
6106  
6107  When Jack appeared on the Merle, rather late that afternoon, Jerry met
6108  him by the steps, his arm in a sling.
6109  
6110  "Good heavens, Tab," cried the captain, "what's the matter? What have
6111  you done to your arm, boy?"
6112  
6113  "Nothing much," Jerry answered. "Just got a little piece of the cutter
6114  in it in a night engagement. What the deuce kept you so long?"
6115  
6116  "But was it last night?" Jack insisted. "Did you get into trouble?"
6117  
6118  "We were under fire," Jerry laughed; "but I had the only casualty."
6119  
6120  "The devil you did! What sort of a trap did your infernal Englishman
6121  lead you into?"
6122  
6123  "That's just what I want to tell you before you see him. What in the
6124  world made you so late? I've been waiting all the afternoon."
6125  
6126  The captain's face grew radiant.
6127  
6128  "Well, you see," he returned, with a little laugh in his throat, "time
6129  passed so quickly, and Katrine and I had so much to talk about"--
6130  
6131  "Jacko! You've done it!" shouted Tab, loud enough to be heard from one
6132  end of the yacht to the other.
6133  
6134  The captain grinned warmly, and nodded with sparkling eyes.
6135  
6136  "Oh, good man!" cried Tab, wringing his hand. "Good old Jack! Long life
6137  and all happiness to you, you dear old pirate!"
6138  
6139  His words tumbled out helter-skelter, and his honest blue eyes were
6140  moist with pure joy at his friend's happiness. He admired Miss
6141  Marchfield from the bottom of his heart, and Jack was the dearest friend
6142  he could ever have. He rejoiced as sincerely and as warmly as if the
6143  good fortune of the captain had been his own.
6144  
6145  "Thank you, old man," laughed Jack, bubbling over with good spirits;
6146  "but if it hadn't been for you, I--I'd never have done it."
6147  
6148  "Tush!" flouted Jerry. "Don't talk bosh! It was only a matter of time
6149  anyway. But I'm glad it's all right."
6150  
6151  They had been standing at the head of the steps, and now the captain
6152  moved along the deck.
6153  
6154  "What did you send for me to come out in such a hurry for?" he
6155  inquired.
6156  
6157  "Hurry!" ejaculated Jerry. "Do you call this coming out in a hurry? If
6158  it hadn't been that you left a born diplomat in charge, you might have
6159  lost two hundred pounds by being so slow."
6160  
6161  "Two hundred pounds?" the other echoed. "What on earth are you talking
6162  about?"
6163  
6164  "Come into the cabin before you go aft," was Jerry's answer. "I want to
6165  tell you about that."
6166  
6167  "And about your arm, old man. What is the matter with you?"
6168  
6169  "That's part of it," Tab returned, as they went below together. "I'm
6170  trying among other things to recover damages."
6171  
6172  When some little time later the two friends came on deck and went aft to
6173  where the guest was sitting, Jack was in full possession of the whole
6174  situation.
6175  
6176  "Jack, Mr. Gordon Wrenmarsh; Mr. Wrenmarsh, Captain John Castleport,"
6177  Jerry said.
6178  
6179  "Pleased to meet you, Mr. Wrenmarsh," Jack said, extending his hand.
6180  
6181  He was evidently in the best of humor. His spirits on that day could
6182  hardly be other than at their highest, and he had been vastly amused by
6183  Jerry's plan of raising funds to pay off the men.
6184  
6185  "Thanks," responded the archæologist. "I was afraid the pleasure was
6186  largely mine. I've been expecting you all day."
6187  
6188  "Well," Jack said, seating himself comfortably, "I am here at last. I am
6189  sorry if I kept you waiting. You might have arranged anything with Mr.
6190  Taberman, though."
6191  
6192  "I tried to," Mr. Wrenmarsh responded dryly, "but he seemed to me so
