6193  unpractical in his ideas that I thought it better to wait for you."
6194  
6195  "I hope you won't find me unsatisfactory in the same way," Jack
6196  returned. "At least I am practical enough to know that in this weather
6197  it will be more comfortable if we have something."
6198  
6199  He summoned Gonzague, and the trio were soon furnished with tall glasses
6200  of sangaree, which they sipped with relish.
6201  
6202  "Mr. Taberman has suggested,--though I fancy he's half in jest," began
6203  the collector, when these preliminaries had been attended to, "that two
6204  hundred pounds is a fair price for such a trivial service as running up
6205  to England and landing me and my boxes."
6206  
6207  "I am glad you think the matter trivial," observed Jack, with a smile;
6208  "it makes it so much easier for me to say that I do not find it
6209  convenient to go to England at all."
6210  
6211  "Oh, I say now," Wrenmarsh responded, with a sudden keen glance at Jack
6212  as if he were surprised at the quickness with which his remark had been
6213  met and turned against him; "of course you'll go to England. That was
6214  settled long ago, you know."
6215  
6216  "Was it? I supposed that I, as captain of the Merle, had some voice in
6217  such a matter."
6218  
6219  "Of course nothing was settled," broke in Jerry. "I made a conditional
6220  arrangement--entirely conditional, mind you--with Mr. Wrenmarsh that you
6221  would take him to England."
6222  
6223  "Yes; that is what I said," the collector asserted imperturbably. "Only
6224  the price that you named"--
6225  
6226  "Seems to me a very reasonable one," interpolated Jack.
6227  
6228  "Not seriously?" Wrenmarsh said, evidently determined not to show that
6229  he was at all ruffled. "Only consider, if I go ashore here, I may get--I
6230  might become a national complication. And you wouldn't want to be mixed
6231  up in that sort of a thing," he added, with a chuckle. "An international
6232  complication," he murmured to himself, as if the idea appealed so
6233  strongly to his vanity that he was half tempted to be put on land at
6234  once to take up the part. Then he recalled his wandering thoughts, and
6235  looked Captain Castleport in the eye. "If you land me in any country
6236  except England, I am quite done for, as you Americans would say. It
6237  stands to reason if there is any paying to be done, you should pay me
6238  for keeping you out of a scrape; for of course if I go ashore it will be
6239  known that the Merle ran away from the _carabinieri_ at Pæstum, and"--
6240  
6241  "Rubbish!" interrupted Jack brusquely. "Don't talk that kind of
6242  poppy-cock! Even if there were any truth in it, it wouldn't be decent
6243  for you to say so after getting the Merle into the scrape."
6244  
6245  "And giving me your word that the yacht was in no possible danger," put
6246  in Jerry indignantly.
6247  
6248  "Oh, no real danger, of course," Wrenmarsh said hurriedly, "only it
6249  might be unpleasant for you, and you might not like to be detained."
6250  
6251  "Why must you go to England?" asked Castleport. "Why not to Malta or
6252  Cyprus or Korfu even? They're protectorates and English ground."
6253  
6254  "The sun never sets, you know," responded Wrenmarsh, with his
6255  extraordinary ventral chuckle. "The truth is they won't do. Korfu and
6256  Cyprus would be as bad for me as Naples, on account of my reputation.
6257  I'm known to have run out a lot of things, you see. Gibraltar or Malta
6258  would suit me well enough--if it weren't for the same reason. There
6259  isn't a hotel on the entire shores of the Mediterranean that I could put
6260  up at with those boxes in safety."
6261  
6262  "I hardly suppose I'm expected to take that too literally," Jack said,
6263  with a smile.
6264  
6265  He reflected a moment. He could see that the collector certainly had
6266  good reason for wishing to remain on the yacht, and that it could not
6267  but be of very great convenience to him to be taken to England. He was
6268  no less convinced from what Jerry had told him that the antiquities
6269  which the archæologist had on board must be worth thousands of pounds,
6270  and that their possessor could afford to pay well for their safety. He
6271  was thoroughly stirred up, moreover, by the thought of the episode of
6272  the night before. That Jerry should have been put in actual peril of his
6273  life by Wrenmarsh for his own purposes was to Jack so outrageous that he
6274  was half tempted to order the collector and his boxes off the Merle at
6275  once to take his chances with the officials on the quays of Naples. As
6276  Jerry had planned reprisals along another line, however, and as after
6277  all Jack could not have brought himself to desert a man in extremity,
6278  the captain determined to go on as they had begun.
6279  
6280  "Two hundred pounds strikes me as fair enough," he said.
6281  
6282  "Too much--too much! Make it fifty," responded Wrenmarsh.
6283  
6284  "Two hundred!" repeated Jack.
6285  
6286  "I'm sorry; I can't do that," the collector said, with a great show of
6287  decision. "You'll have to take me to Malta. What'll you do that for?"
6288  
6289  "Three hundred," Jack returned quietly, although he could not refrain
6290  from a secret exchange of glances with Jerry.
6291  
6292  "What!" the other cried, in an exaggerated shriek. "A run like that?
6293  Three hundred pounds! It's not a twentieth the distance to England."
6294  
6295  "That's so," was the captain's answer, "but you see we should have a
6296  good deal less value in your company. Besides, you'd get your boxes _ex
6297  territorio_ a great deal quicker."
6298  
6299  He had by this time become so interested in the game he was playing that
6300  the beating of the collector seemed in itself a thing worth straining
6301  every nerve to gain.
6302  
6303  "They're _ex territorio_ now," Mr. Wrenmarsh said, "as they're on a
6304  foreign yacht. But no matter about that. What'll you take to set me over
6305  to Gibraltar?"
6306  
6307  "Oh, that would cost you three hundred and fifty, because there you're
6308  so much nearer England than you'd be at Malta."
6309  
6310  He glanced again at Jerry, with an inward chuckle at the utter
6311  balderdash he was talking and a consciousness how closely it resembled
6312  the nature of the arguments with which Wrenmarsh had beguiled Tab. For a
6313  minute there was silence, and then the archæologist spoke angrily.
6314  
6315  "You're too commercial," he said, with an unconcealed sneer. "I see no
6316  way in which we can come to an agreement. I never was equal to trading
6317  with a dollar-getting Yankee."
6318  
6319  Tab started and looked to hear Jack break out at an insult so gross, but
6320  the captain merely smiled.
6321  
6322  "As you are our guest," he said, "there's no chance for me to answer you
6323  properly, but you must remember we're not looking for a job. Shall I
6324  send you ashore now, or would it suit you to take a boat with me in half
6325  an hour? Or perhaps," he added, his manner most elaborately courteous,
6326  "on account of your boxes, it would suit you better to be set ashore
6327  after dark."
6328  
6329  "Give you one hundred pounds," the collector said, still fighting, and
6330  ignoring the captain's words entirely.
6331  
6332  "We need not go on with the wrangle," Jack said, rising. "I'm not
6333  bargaining with you. If it's worth two hundred pounds to you, all right.
6334  If it isn't, we'll part here, and hope you have the gratitude to
6335  appreciate what has already been done for you at the risk of Mr.
6336  Taberman's life. Come, we've wasted too much time over this already."
6337  
6338  "Do you think my time isn't worth anything?" cried the
6339  other,--apparently losing all control of his temper. "I've wasted too
6340  much already. Get up your damned anchor, you mercenary Yankee"--
6341  
6342  "Come, sir!" broke in Jack sharply, "apologize at once! At once! You
6343  have been insulting us this half hour like an utter cad, and I've made
6344  all the allowances I'm equal to."
6345  
6346  The collector regarded him with furious eyes, but seemed struggling with
6347  himself until he could command his manner and his voice.
6348  
6349  "I--I beg your pardon," he said in a hard tone. Then he added, in a
6350  voice softer and more grave, "Indeed, I beg your pardon most sincerely.
6351  My cursed temper got the better of me. Does your offer still hold?"
6352  
6353  "If you wish," Jack answered stiffly.
6354  
6355  "Then--two hundred pounds--I accept it. Two hundred pounds sterling, to
6356  be paid on our safe arrival in port at Plymouth." He sighed, and put
6357  out his hand to the captain. "Will you pardon my tongue?" he asked.
6358  
6359  There was more ingenuousness in this trifling act than in anything Tab
6360  or Jack had yet seen in him. The real man seemed for a moment to show;
6361  and as Jack accepted the collector's apology and took his hand, Jerry
6362  had a fleeting glimpse--short as a flash of changing light--of another
6363  and franker Wrenmarsh, accustomed to hide under a veil of shams and
6364  mockeries made necessary by his difficult vocation.
6365  
6366  Wrenmarsh then asked if he might have some letters mailed ashore, and
6367  Jack offered to take them himself in half an hour's time. While the
6368  collector was below writing these, the captain and the mate talked
6369  things over on deck. Tab had to congratulate Jack again, and over and
6370  over, fairly beaming with delight whenever he thought of the happy stage
6371  to which affairs had been brought. When he discovered that the captain
6372  had confessed the lifting of the Merle, he was for a moment
6373  disconcerted.
6374  
6375  "Oh, Jacko, how could you give that away?" he cried.
6376  
6377  "I had to be honest," Jack replied, and added, with a little shade of
6378  unconscious patronage, "You'll see how it is yourself, old man, when it
6379  comes your turn. You have to make a square deal, of course."
6380  
6381  "Yes, I s'pose so," assented the mate humbly. "I hope she won't tell
6382  Mrs. Fairhew."
6383  
6384  "Oh, we told her together," Jack stated cheerfully. "Katrine thought
6385  we'd better. I'm glad I did, too; for she's written home about meeting
6386  us, and it's sure to get round to Uncle Randolph sooner or later."
6387  
6388  "How did she take it?"
6389  
6390  "Oh, do you know," returned Jack, laughing at the remembrance of his
6391  talk with Mrs. Fairhew, "I think she was more bothered that she hadn't
6392  guessed it than she was shocked at us. She couldn't help letting me see
6393  that she thought it an awfully good joke on Uncle Randolph. She said she
6394  should write to him to-day and remind him that she'd often told him he
6395  tried to keep me in leading strings. She said she did have a suspicion
6396  from your jocoseness when we first came over that there was some joke
6397  about our coming, but we parried her questions so well she forgot all
6398  about it. She said nobody could have dreamed of anything so
6399  preposterous, so of course she didn't guess it."
6400  
6401  "Didn't she say it was on account of her age she didn't see through us?"
6402  queried Jerry, with a grin.
6403  
6404  "By Jove, she did; and then turned it off by saying she never supposed
6405  a Marchfield would be engaged to a pirate. She says, though, that I've
6406  got to cut back at once. She won't have me going about with Katrine in a
6407  stolen yacht."
6408  
6409  "It's time to start anyway. It'll be getting late by the time we're
6410  across, and if she's written home, the sooner the Merle is in Boston
6411  harbor the better. I suppose we can get off in a week?"
6412  
6413  "We go to-morrow," Jack answered calmly.
6414  
6415  "To-morrow! Great Scott! What are we sitting here for? There are oceans
6416  of things to be done."
6417  
6418  "Of course we can get stores at Plymouth if we need to, and I've already
6419  ordered a lot of things to come out to-night. We have to get Wrenmarsh
6420  safe, of course, and that'll take some time."
6421  
6422  "He's a windfall," commented Jerry.
6423  
6424  "And like most windfalls, not entirely sound? Tell Gonzague to fix up
6425  the stateroom Bardale had, the one next mine. I must get ashore now;
6426  she'll be waiting. You're to come to dinner."
6427  
6428  "I'll come fast enough. Oh, you bully old pirate, I'm awfully glad for
6429  you!"
6430  
6431  [Illustration: Decoration]
6432  
6433  
6434  
6435  
6436  Chapter Fifteen
6437  
6438  IN THE CATTEWATER
6439  
6440  
6441  The Merle was at anchor off Plymouth.
6442  
6443  By the round brass ship's clock placed over the passageway door, in the
6444  saloon, Jerry could see that it was a little after ten o'clock. The
6445  yacht had come to anchor in the small hours, and the gentlemen had in
6446  consequence slept late. The dull light of an English morning in
6447  September came through the big skylight, and showed the captain, the
6448  mate, and Mr. Wrenmarsh lingering over their breakfast.
6449  
6450  "On my word, Mr. Wrenmarsh," said Tab, "we'll be sorry to lose you.
6451  You've been aboard so long and your"--he almost blurted out
6452  "eccentricities," but fortunately had the unusual luck to stop in time
6453  to substitute a better word--"your--er--conversation has such--er--has
6454  been so very entertaining, that is, that we're sure to miss you."
6455  
6456  "Ah, well," said the collector, "I'm in hopes that you've improved so
6457  much by contact with me that you'll be able to entertain each other."
6458  
6459  "Wouldn't you like to take passage across?" suggested Jack.
6460  
6461  "Your rates are too high," the other rejoined grimly. "Gonzague, _'n'
6462  altro bicchier' d' aqua fresca_."
6463  
6464  The old steward, who had come in while Jerry was speaking, served the
6465  archæologist with the ready alacrity which marked all he did, and then
6466  departed with a handful of dishes.
6467  
6468  "Why do you always speak to Gonzague in Italian?" inquired Jerry. "You
6469  said yesterday that you always had a reason for everything you do."
6470  
6471  "Oh," the guest returned, fixing his eyes not on the questioner but on
6472  the ceiling above him, "I speak to him in Italian because he understands
6473  it."
6474  
6475  "But he isn't an Italian," Tab objected.
6476  
6477  "No, but then I'm not either."
6478  
6479  "But he understands English, French, and Spanish, for the matter of
6480  that," Jerry persisted.
6481  
6482  Whenever Wrenmarsh began to talk in this whimsical fashion, Taberman had
6483  always a teasing desire to push him into a corner.
6484  
6485  "Ah, but, my dear fellow," Wrenmarsh replied, unaccountably addressing
6486  Jack, and making his words seem more distraught by one of his most
6487  earnest, almost burning glances, "I do not speak Spanish, you see."
6488  
6489  "Then why not French or English?"
6490  
6491  "Because they're so different," returned the collector.
6492  
6493  "Why, what rot!" Jerry burst out rudely; then as usual he added
6494  apologetically, "I beg your pardon, but I'm afraid I don't follow you."
6495  
6496  "Oh, no; I suppose not," Mr. Wrenmarsh rejoined with much sweetness. He
6497  rose, and with an entire change of manner, added briskly, "Well, I'm
6498  ready. As I wish to catch the eleven thirty-four for London, we must
6499  make haste; otherwise I shouldn't have time to take Mr. Castleport to
6500  the bank, and settle my financial obligations. Can we get ashore?"
6501  
6502  "Yes," answered Jack, rising also. "The cutter's ready, and your boxes
6503  are on board. By the by, you said you'd tell me how you dodge--pardon
6504  the word, we use it on the other side--the customs."
6505  
6506  "Simplest thing in the world," returned Wrenmarsh, lighting a cigarette.
6507  "Address my boxes to a good friend of mine in the British Museum. They
6508  go through the customhouse as things for the museum, you know."
6509  
6510  "Does your friend do that sort of thing as a business?" inquired Jerry
6511  with a laugh. "I wish you'd give me his name, so I could come that
6512  game."
6513  
6514  "His name is Gordon Wrenmarsh," said the collector quietly; "but his
6515  charges are high. Shall we go?"
6516  
6517  "Yes," Jack responded. "It is high time we were off. I'm not anxious to
6518  speed the parting guest, but a good send-off means an early start."
6519  
6520  Jerry left his place, and the three went on deck. The cutter, already
6521  manned, was by the steps. The bleak English air struck chill and raw to
6522  these men fresh from the warm sunshine of the Mediterranean. The harbor
6523  and sound, crowded with shipping as they were, seemed flat and dull; the
6524  Citadel, the Battery, the various docks and buildings were depressing. A
6525  great volume of dun coal-smoke overhanging the "Three Towns," from the
6526  Hamoaze to Sutton Pool, added to the general air of gloom. To cap all
6527  this, the fog was coming in from seaward, and already its ghostly
6528  echelons had floated past the north end of Drake Island. As the three
6529  men came on deck the cutter was bobbing up and down in the wash of the
6530  ferry which plies to and fro across the Cattewater, and which had just
6531  gone heavily past.
6532  
6533  "Dear England!" ejaculated Mr. Wrenmarsh fervently under his breath in
6534  the face of all this. Then turning to Taberman, "You're not coming
6535  ashore with us?"
6536  
6537  Jerry shook his bare head, and gave an exaggerated shiver for reply.
6538  
6539  "No?" the collector said. "Well, we'll say good-by here, then. Lucky we
6540  met, wasn't it? Those combinations--they make the world go round; stop
6541  it sometimes. Good-by. Pity, great pity, you weren't at Oxford, Mr.
6542  Taberman. It would have done you good, made a man of you."
6543  
6544  "Not if Harvard's failed to," retorted Jerry loyally. "Good-by, and good
6545  luck. Hope we'll meet again some day."
6546  
6547  They shook hands, and Mr. Wrenmarsh and Jack descended to the waiting
6548  cutter.
6549  
6550  "_Adio, Signor'_," called out old Gonzague, who was standing by the
6551  main-rigging.
6552  
6553  "_A riverderla forse_" returned the collector from the stern-sheets of
6554  the cutter.
6555  
6556  "_Il mondo è piccolo, Signor'. Spero_," answered the Provençal.
6557  
6558  "Oars!" cried Jack. "Bear away,--let fall,--ready,--pull." And the
6559  cutter bore away the strange collector toward the shore of his adopted
6560  country.
6561  
6562  Jerry watched the boat for a moment, his big heart not untouched by a
6563  sympathetic friendliness for the lonely man, whose life seemed to him so
6564  warped and melancholy. He half expected Wrenmarsh to look back to nod or
6565  to wave his hand, but the collector's eyes were turned steadily to the
6566  shore. It was chill on deck, and Tab went below.
6567  
6568  Gonzague was just taking away the last of the breakfast things. He set
6569  his tray on the table, and approached the mate deferentially.
6570  
6571  "Mistaire Taberman, sair," he said, putting his hand in his pocket, and
6572  drawing out a small square blue box and a note, "Mistaire Wrainmairsh he
6573  geeve me de box and de lettair--also a crown in extrair dat I geeve dem
6574  to you when he have leef."
6575  
6576  "Eh? what?" asked Jerry. "Oh, I see. Thank you."
6577  
6578  He sat down on the port transom, and opened the box. It contained a
6579  small object carefully wrapped in tissue paper. He unfolded the paper,
6580  and between his fingers a gold finger-ring slipped on to the green
6581  corduroy cushion of the transom.
6582  
6583  "Great Scott!" he ejaculated. Then he picked it up and examined it
6584  carefully.
6585  
6586  In a thin band of red gold was set a carnelian of beautiful tone, the
6587  color of a red hyacinth blossom. The stone was oval, cut with an
6588  exquisite design in intaglio. It represented a god holding a trident in
6589  his left hand, and on his right a small winged figure. His right foot
6590  rested on a stone, and he was gazing at the figure he held. The gem was
6591  inscribed with the Greek letters [Greek: LIL].
6592  
6593  Jerry tore open the note. It read as follows:--
6594  
6595  
6596       Really, my dear fellow, had you viewed me more as a friend and less
6597       as a curiosity, you might have found it to your advantage. But to
6598       the point. I hope you will wear the ring in memory of our little
6599       escapade. The figure represents Poseidon, holding a victoriole in
6600       his hand; and is, as the letters signify, designed to commemorate
6601       the naval victory of Lilybæum (Capo Boao), in which some of the
6602       original wearer's ancestors (more likely pretended than real) were
6603       evidently supposed to have taken part. Of course the wearer, though
6604       not the cutter, was a Roman; but you won't mind that. Not a bit. So
6605       no one gets hurt--your arm, you know--in my behalf without cause to
6606       remember the fact--pleasantly. The stone is by no means the best
6607       that I obtained, but it seemed appropriate. Poseidon with a
6608       victoriole--usually an attribute of Zeus Soter (see your
6609       Furtwängler's A. G.)--is rare enough to give the thing value.
6610  
6611       With merriment,
6612  
6613       WRENMARSH.
6614  
6615  
6616  "By Jove!" cried Jerry to himself, gloating over the ring, "what a calf
6617  I was to that--that white man! By Gad, though, he was a stunner, and no
6618  mistake!"
6619  
6620  He slipped the gold band on his finger. After a time of admiration he
6621  took a book from the shelf, and tried to read; but every minute or two
6622  he stopped to look again at the jewel.
6623  
6624  He had not turned many pages when he heard a boat alongside, and a
6625  strange voice hailing.
6626  
6627  "Hallo," he thought. "I wonder what that is. It can't be the port
6628  officer; we satisfied him at daybreak."
6629  
6630  He tossed aside his book, and went on deck. A shabby jolly-boat was
6631  lying alongside. Jerry noted instantly and with consternation that she
6632  was manned by six men in uniform, in charge of a burly old fellow
6633  liberally adorned with brass buttons and gold braid, who looked to be
6634  every inch a sea-dog. At a second glance Tab decided that these men were
6635  not government employees, such as coast-guards, but belonged to some
6636  sort of a company. With one stunning blow, sudden as the bursting of a
6637  waterspout, the truth flashed over him; at the last, at the very last,
6638  when they had escaped so long that they had practically ceased to think
6639  of the danger, the agent of Lloyd's was upon them.
6640  
6641  "Hello there, what d'ye want?" called out the man doing anchor-watch.
6642  
6643  "Captain aboard?" demanded the burly officer in charge.
6644  
6645  "No," answered the hand suspiciously. "What will you have?"
6646  
6647  "I want to see the officer in charge, my spruce little sea-cook,"
6648  returned the big man genially; and the grating of the steps being handy,
6649  without further ceremony he came aboard.
6650  
6651  The sailor keeping the deck, although of a slow and plodding
6652  disposition, might have resented the coolness of the stranger, had Jerry
6653  given him time; but with a commendable promptness and a sinking heart
6654  the mate advanced. He told Jack afterward that he felt as if he were
6655  leading a forlorn hope, and had not the remotest idea of what he had
6656  better do or say.
6657  
6658  "I am in charge here," he said in a perfectly neutral voice. "What do
6659  you want?"
6660  
6661  "You are Captain Castleport?" inquired the big man, giving Jerry a keen
6662  glance not without a suspicion of kindly humor.
6663  
6664  He was a fine, strapping creature of perhaps forty-five or fifty, with
6665  fair hair, and a large bushy beard tawny as a lion's mane.
6666  
6667  "Captain Castleport is ashore, sir. I am the mate."
6668  
6669  "Mr. Taberman, eh?" asked the other. "May I see you in private for a
6670  minute or two, sir? I'm Lloyd's deputy inspector for Plymouth. I've been
6671  hunting about in the fog for you these thirty minutes past. I thought
6672  you were nigh out o' the Cattewater, over toward the Hoe."
6673  
6674  "Will you come below?" said Jerry grimly.
6675  
6676  Inwardly he groaned for the arrival of Jack. This was a task he felt
6677  himself unable to deal with. Had the emergency called simply for
6678  physical powers or for manual dexterity, the chances were large that he
6679  could rise to the occasion; but in a pass where the demand was for
6680  mental adroitness and nimble wits, Jerry knew the captain to be
6681  infinitely his superior. He determined to devote himself to gaining
6682  time, and to refrain from committing himself until his comrade should
6683  come aboard.
6684  
6685  Jerry escorted the burly guest to the cabin without further speech, and
6686  turned to ask him to be seated. The visitor at once drew over his
6687  jovial face like a veil a serious expression, and regarded Taberman
6688  with the greatest gravity. Unbuttoning the top of his serge jacket, he
6689  thrust his hand into an inner pocket as if it were a dip-net, and
6690  brought it up again full of dismally official-looking documents.
6691  
6692  "This is bad business, sir," he remarked, eyeing the mate as if to be
6693  sure he was producing a proper impression.
6694  
6695  "Eh?" ejaculated Jerry, trying to look like consolidated innocence.
6696  
6697  "P'haps you'll be so good's to look these through, sir," the Englishman
6698  went on, proffering his batch of papers.
6699  
6700  "Are they for me or the captain?" asked Taberman, fencing to gain time.
6701  
6702  "Why, as to that," the official replied, "I expect what they contain's
6703  ekally to your int'rest and 'is."
6704  
6705  "Sit down, please," Jerry said, with a confused wave of the hand, which
6706  seemed to invite the visitor to occupy all the seats in the cabin at
6707  once. "You may be right, but I shouldn't want to look any important
6708  papers over until the captain'd seen them."
6709  
6710  "Oh, that don't matter," the other said easily, as he settled himself in
6711  a chair. "I don't think you 'ave any cause to mind, sir. You represent
6712  'im aboard."
6713  
6714  "Yes," Jerry returned, obstinately determined that nothing should make
6715  him go through the papers without Jack; "but if you're not too much
6716  pressed for time, I'd much rather wait for the captain. He'll be here
6717  presently."
6718  
6719  "Why, sir, for the matter o' that, I dunno's I've much to 'urry me this
6720  mornin'; an' I must say I'd rather like a look at 'im. 'E must be a rare
6721  one."
6722  
6723  "Then," Jerry said, with infinite relief, "we'll wait till he gets
6724  aboard."
6725  
6726  He rang, and Gonzague appeared. The old Provençal stood stroking his
6727  mustache and watching the Englishman furtively out of the corners of his
6728  eyes, as if he appreciated the situation and hoped to have orders to
6729  assist in throwing him overboard. The glance of the bluff Briton at the
6730  same time lighted up in evident anticipation that the appearance of the
6731  steward meant refreshments.
6732  
6733  "Gonzague, I'll have a little Scotch and soda. Will you take a glass of
6734  anything, sir?"
6735  
6736  "Why, sir, seein' 's I 'ave to wait a bit, I'm not strong agin a finger
6737  or two."
6738  
6739  "What will you have?" asked Jerry, enormously relieved to get on ground
6740  so safe as that of playing the host.
6741  
6742  "I like red rum 's well 's most, sir," replied the other, his jolly eyes
6743  twinkling. "It's sort o' oilin' to the in'ards."
6744  
6745  They were soon served, and Gonzague, on leaving the cabin, placed the
6746  spirits and a siphon in most engaging proximity to the guest. Time
6747  passed in the exchange of more or less nautical chit-chat for half an
6748  hour or so; when, to the great comfort of Jerry, who had been listening
6749  with one ear to the talk of his companion and with the other for the
6750  coming of the captain, Jack's hail sounded outside. Jerry, listening
6751  acutely, heard Castleport pause on deck, and at the companion-way caught
6752  a syllable or two in the unmistakable tones of Gonzague, so that he
6753  apprehended that the captain would come to the interview forewarned.
6754  
6755  The captain came briskly into the cabin, his blue pea-jacket beaded with
6756  little globules of moisture from the fog, his hair damp and clinging to
6757  his temples.
6758  
6759  "Hallo, Tab," he said. "The fog's as thick as it was the night we
6760  started. Ah!"
6761  
6762  The exclamation cleverly conveyed the impression that he perceived the
6763  guest for the first time, and apologized for not being prepared to meet
6764  him.
6765  
6766  "Jack, this is Lloyd's deputy inspector, Mr. ----?" Jerry began, and
6767  stopped with an interrogative inflection.
6768  
6769  "My name, sir, 's Tom Mainbrace."
6770  
6771  "Mr. Thomas Mainbrace," Jerry concluded his presentation. "Mr.
6772  Mainbrace, Captain Castleport."
6773  
6774  "Pleased to know ye, cap'n," the Englishman said cheerfully, as Jack
6775  bowed. "Yes, sir; I'm Lloyd's deputy inspector."
6776  
6777  "I saw your boat alongside," Jack returned pleasantly. "We haven't any
6778  deputies aboard that need inspecting, though."
6779  
6780  "'Aven't ye?" the visitor asked, his eyes twinkling so that the laugh
6781  with which he followed his words seemed a sort of overflow of their
6782  merriment. "I kind o' thought there might be a deputy owner or som'thin'
6783  o' the sort 'ere."
6784  
6785  Jack apparently tried to look grave, but ended by grinning in spite of
6786  himself. He put out his hand and laid his fingers on the papers.
6787  
6788  "You have business with us?" he asked.
6789  
6790  "Yes, sir. The mate 'ere, 'e said 'e 'd rather not begin on it till you
6791  come aboard, sir."
6792  
6793  "Quite right," Jack responded quietly. "Shall I read these papers?"
6794  
6795  "Yes, if ye'll be so good, sir," Mr. Mainbrace said seriously, and not
6796  without a trace of regret in his jovial, weather-beaten face.
6797  
6798  The captain seated himself with deliberation, and began to read; the
6799  Englishman applied himself afresh to his glass, and Taberman watched
6800  closely for a lead. Jerry was not clear what line was to be taken in
6801  this difficult situation, and was keenly anxious to back up the captain
6802  in any way possible. To his surprise Jack began first to smile, then to
6803  grin; from that to chuckle gleefully, and at last he broke out into
6804  full-throated laughter.
6805  
6806  "By Jove!" he cried, striking his knee with the hand that held the
6807  papers. "But that is one on Uncle Randolph, and no mistake!"
6808  
6809  The deputy inspector looked up with an expression of bewilderment, and
6810  Jerry felt that he was no more enlightened as to what Jack had in mind
6811  than was the guest.
6812  
6813  "What is it?" Tab asked.
6814  
6815  "Oh, we're run down at last! Think of our being nabbed at the last
6816  moment, when we've done all we wanted to with the yacht!" And he fell to
6817  laughing again, as if being caught red-handed in a pirated yacht were
6818  the merriest jest in the world.
6819  
6820  Taberman was still completely bewildered, but he at least perceived that
6821  Jack was bound to carry off the matter with laughter; and by way of
6822  assisting as well as he could, he began also to laugh. He took the
6823  papers, and glanced at them enough to see that one was a letter from
6824  Lloyd's, containing a notification of the Merle's disappearance, with a
6825  description of the yacht and a specification of her captors; the other a
6826  warrant for search and apprehension. He followed Jack's lead, and if his
6827  efforts did not ring as true, he at least made more noise.
6828  
6829  "That's rich!" he roared. "Ha! Ha! Ha!"
6830  
6831  He thrust the papers back to the captain, who tossed them on the table,
6832  and both together they broke out afresh.
6833  
6834  "Excuse our laughing," Jack said, turning to the inspector, who gazed
6835  from one to the other as if he thought they had gone mad; "but really
6836  it's too ripping!"
6837  
6838  "Ain't ye the parties?" demanded the official sternly.
6839  
6840  "Oh, we're the parties all fast enough; but--Well, now, look here. This
6841  yacht belongs to my uncle, you see."
6842  
6843  "Yes, sir," replied the honest Mainbrace, evidently puzzled, as he would
6844  have put it, to make out the other's numbers, but still Britannically
6845  deferential to the nephew of a man who was able to own a yacht such as
6846  the Merle.
6847  
6848  "Well, you see, I ran away with her because he wouldn't let me come
6849  across, and he's had no good of her the whole summer. From your papers I
6850  judge he looked for me on the other side six weeks before he notified
6851  you at all. You see how much of the summer that leaves him; and now,
6852  just as I'm starting to carry her back as fast as the wind will take
6853  her, you step in and stop us."
6854  
6855  "Why, ye see, sir," began the inspector, evidently endeavoring to
6856  accommodate himself to the new light thrown by the captain on the
6857  situation, "the fact is 'e says 'e wants 'er in a 'urry."
6858  
6859  "He won't get her, then," Jack said with a grin. "By the time you've
6860  red-taped her, and charged for her, and negotiated her, and sent her
6861  over with a hired crew, it'll be December at the very earliest--to say
6862  nothing of the twenty or thirty pounds he'll have to pay you and the
6863  cost of the crew you send her over by. It is hard lines for Uncle
6864  Randolph."
6865  
6866  "It is so," Jerry agreed, fervently glad to be at last in possession of
6867  the way Jack meant to work.
6868  
6869  "I'm really sorry for Uncle Randolph," Jack continued, sobering down.
6870  "But then, he might have trusted me to bring the Merle back."
6871  
6872  "Ye ain't takin' it too much to 'eart, are ye, sir?" queried the big
6873  Englishman, with a look so humorous and quizzical that Jerry was seized
6874  by a dreadful suspicion that the twinkling eyes saw through the whole
6875  scheme of bluff.
6876  
6877  "Not I," Jack assented blithely; "though of course I'd rather have taken
6878  the yacht home myself. What's the next move? Do you put us in irons, or
6879  hang us to the crosstree-ends?"
6880  
6881  "Why, they sent word from Lloyd's," replied Mainbrace, with the
6882  unmistakable grin of a man who regards himself as a humorist, "that the
6883  owner said not to be too 'ard on ye. I expect 't'll be no worse nor
6884  transportation for life." Then he put on a graver and more professional
6885  look, and added, "I'm afraid we'll 'ave to be more serious, sir. Will ye
6886  kindly show me your papers and the log? I suppose you 'ave 'em 'andy."
6887  
6888  "Certainly," the captain said, also assuming an official air. "Jerry,
6889  will you give the inspector the papers? I'll get the log."
6890  
6891  The examination of the papers was a short matter, and then they took up
6892  the log. It was at once evident that the Englishman had a keen curiosity
6893  to discover what the young men had been doing with the Merle, and that
6894  he was no less eager in his interest in all things nautical. Jerry sat
6895  by in almost open-mouthed admiration to see bow the captain took
6896  advantage of both these characteristics. Jack could be most attractive,
6897  and from the start it was evident that he was doing his best to please
6898  Mr. Mainbrace. He explained all the manoeuvres of that memorable night
6899  when the Merle had been spirited away in the fog, while the jolly face
6900  of the deputy inspector became more and more radiant with each new
6901  development of the story. The charts were produced, each detail of
6902  seamanship carefully brought out, and the whole episode lived over
6903  again. Jack warmed to his subject as he went on; Jerry threw in a word
6904  now and then when the captain in his eagerness seemed in danger of
6905  forgetting to mention some detail; the Englishman listened with chuckles
6906  and with laughter which soon came to be devoid of the slightest pretense
6907  of official dignity; and, in a word, the three became as merry and
6908  companionable over the log as if they were all pirates together.
6909  Mainbrace had been a sailor and a mate in his day, and showed the
6910  keenest zest for every nautical experience. There is no surer bond of
6911  comradeship than mutual love of the sea; and despite differences of
6912  race, age, and social position, Jack, Jerry, and the deputy inspector
6913  fraternized over the Merle's log as only sailors can.
6914  
6915  The log-book was read to the last entry. Over the account of the gale
6916  the yacht had encountered on her way across the Atlantic Mainbrace
6917  became as excited as if he had had a personal stake in the safety of the
6918  Merle. His ejaculations became more and more emphatic and more and more
6919  picturesque, and his rejoicing over the safe weathering of the storm
6920  almost as fervid as if he had been in it himself. The race at Nice Jack
6921  told of with as little reflection on the unsportsmanlike conduct of Lord
6922  Merryfield as was possible; but the jovial countenance of Mainbrace
6923  darkened, and he expressed an opinion of the absent nobleman which was
6924  sufficiently tonic to satisfy even Taberman. Jack said afterward that by
6925  the time they got through the log a quotation from "Horatius" popped
6926  into his head, and he came very near breaking out with it:--
6927  
6928  
6929       With weeping and with laughter
6930         Still is the story told.
6931  
6932  
6933  To which Jerry replied that he couldn't think of quotations, he was so
6934  carried away by the enthusiastic delight of the jolly old inspector and
6935  the quaint ways in which it was expressed.
6936  
6937  When at last the record was closed, the conversation still at first ran
6938  on the cruise, but soon it began to take a turn which made Jerry prick
6939  up his ears anew. The inspector remarked, with an exceedingly droll
6940  twinkle of his eyes, that duty was duty, but that he would be summarily
6941  dealt with if he wouldn't feel bad to have to bear on hard on a couple
6942  of fellows that had played the biggest joke he ever heard of in his
6943  life, and had carried the whole thing through with so much cleverness
6944  and grit. To this Jack responded that he was most appreciative of the
6945  kindness of Mr. Mainbrace, but that of course duty was duty--although it
6946  would really have been luck for the owner of the Merle, quite as much as
6947  for himself and his mate, if the yacht could have gone on her way
6948  uninterrupted. To this in turn Mainbrace gave his assent, and went on to
6949  say that he must, of course, carry out instructions, and that he was
6950  legally empowered to leave a keeper on board until he could come out
6951  again to-morrow with directions he expected to receive from London.
6952  
6953  "Though I dunno," he added drolly, "'s it's safe to trust a man with ye.
6954  Ye're cap'ble o' runnin' off with 'im."
6955  
6956  "We might," Jack responded brightly. "I wouldn't be responsible."
6957  
6958  "Or we might throw him overboard," suggested Jerry, with the broadest
6959  possible grin.
6960  
6961  "Most o' my men kin swim some," Mainbrace retorted. "I should 'ave to
6962  tell 'im 'f 'e got overboard to tow the yacht in shore."
6963  
6964  The jest was not of the first water, but they had got to a merry mood,
6965  and it was properly laughed over. Then Mainbrace, in high good humor,
6966  went on to say that he'd been so well treated, and he had so enjoyed the
6967  log, that he thought on the whole he would not put a man in charge. He
6968  added that it was late, and he must be on his way ashore now, but that
6969  they might expect him out again to-morrow.
6970  
6971  "I'm sorry I 'ave to bother ye, gentlemen," he added, as they went on
6972  deck. "I've been to sea myself too many years not to 'ate this bloody
6973  red-tape business,--an' they do reel it off by the cable-length when
6974  they 'ave 'arf a chance."
6975  
6976  The inspector's jolly-boat, the most appropriate of conveyances for the
6977  jovial sea-dog, was still alongside. The fog had lightened somewhat, and
6978  watery beams of the sun leaked through it overhead. As Mr. Mainbrace was
6979  about to descend the steps to the boat, he paused a moment and pulled at
6980  his thick beard as if meditating profoundly.
6981  
6982  "I'm 'most afraid if you gentlemen took it into your 'eads to give us
6983  the slip we shouldn't know it on shore in this 'ere fog," he observed,
6984  casting a queer, sidling glance at Jack.
6985  
6986  "It is trusting somewhat to luck to leave us," the captain responded
6987  coolly, "and I want to say now that I appreciate your kindness in not
6988  forcing a keeper on us."
6989  
6990  "Well, cap'n," continued the inspector, gazing out over the water with
6991  the look of one who has no personal interest in the matter under
6992  discussion, "I was goin' to say, if you get a good chance, you'd better
6993  shift your berth. You'll find it kind o' snugger ridin' some ways along
6994  to the west'ard, I expect. But you know best, o' course. All is, you're
6995  in a tightish place here. I alers liked more sea-room myself. Good-day,
6996  sir."
6997  
6998  "Good-day. Maybe you'll find we've shifted by to-morrow. If we have,
6999  it'll be to westward."
7000  
7001  "I'll come out to-morrow," said the old sailor in his most official
7002  manner. Then he looked from one to the other with his merriest twinkle
7003  and an emphatic nod. "Duty is duty," he remarked. "Good-day, sirs."
7004  
7005  He turned to descend, but suddenly Jack arrested him.
7006  
7007  "Oh, you've forgotten your pipe," he said.
7008  
7009  "My pipe?" echoed Mainbrace, stopping short.
7010  
7011  "Yes, I'll get it."
7012  
7013  The captain dashed into the cabin, and reappeared with a silver-mounted
7014  briarwood, colored just enough to suggest a comfortable chimney-corner
7015  and a mind at ease.
7016  
7017  "You left it on the table," he said, presenting it to the big inspector.
7018  
7019  The other took it with an expression queerly compounded of surprise,
7020  awkwardness, amusement, and delight.
7021  
7022  "Thank ye, sir," he said. "It's 'ansome of you to fetch it up
7023  ye'self,--most 'ansome. I'm mortal fond o' that pipe."
7024  
7025  He regarded it affectionately a moment, and then stowed it away inside
7026  his jacket. Then he turned again to go down to the waiting jolly-boat.
7027  
7028  "I'll come out to-morrow," he called up to them. "Duty is duty.
7029  Good-day, sirs."
7030  
7031  "Good-day," they called in concert; and off went the deputy inspector
7032  toward the hardly perceptible shore through the fog.
7033  
7034  "By George, he's a brick!" Jack cried.
7035  
7036  "Right-o," assented Jerry, "but it took you to cement him."
7037  
7038  "Atrocious! If you're going to pun like that you must be taken home to
7039  your family at once. 'Duty is duty'! Did you see the solemn wink the
7040  old fellow tipped me when he spoke of shifting to westward? I thought I
7041  should burst out laughing on the spot, and give the whole thing away.
7042  How's the water?"
7043  
7044  "Tanks chock-a-block. Gonzague had them filled from the water-boat this
7045  morning. Did you get your money?"
7046  
7047  "Every pound of it. Wrenmarsh took me to the bank and identified me, and
7048  was mighty nice about the whole thing. Provisions are O.K. Off we go.
7049  Call the watch."
7050  
7051  "Yes, but see my ring first," Tab said, holding it out.
7052  
7053  In half an hour the Merle was changing her berth to the westward.
7054  
7055  [Illustration: Decoration]
7056  
7057  
7058  
7059  
7060  Chapter Sixteen
7061  
7062  STORM!
7063  
7064  
7065  A gray sea, a gray sky, and the Mid-Atlantic Ocean in September. Over
7066  the heaving waters the Merle, under reduced canvas, was staggering
7067  westward on the port-tack with a stiff southerly breeze. Jack, clad in
7068  his yellow oil-skins like the rest of the hands, was standing just
7069  outside the cockpit on the windward side of the yacht. Jerry was asleep
7070  below. Having had the early morning watch, he had turned in directly
7071  after breakfast. The captain glanced aloft uneasily, and wondered if
7072  they were going to encounter on their return such a gale as they had
7073  weathered while going over. He reluctantly admitted to himself that
7074  there was every appearance of dirty weather, and thought he had better
7075  step below to take a look at the glass.
7076  
7077  He pushed back the companion, and descended. The cabin was stuffy and no
7078  warmer than the air without. The racks were on the table, and the lamps
7079  swung in erratic circles in their gimbals. The barometer, a beautifully
7080  finished instrument of the columnar type, was placed against the
7081  after-bulkhead of the saloon on the starboard beside a closet door, its
7082  slender length enclosed in bronze. It gyrated wildly, in unison with the
7083  Thom's list-indicator above it. Jack steadied the tube with his hand,
7084  and looked anxiously to see if the mercury had fallen.
7085  
7086  "Good God!" he burst out.
7087  
7088  At eight bells that morning the vernier of the glass had been set at
7089  29.32. With staring eyes, Jack saw that now, little more than two hours
7090  later, the mercury had sunk to 27.09,--a drop portentous of a furious
7091  gale. For one brief moment, in the face of approaching danger, and
7092  filled with a quick sense of his great responsibility, he stood
7093  appalled. He put his hand to his forehead as if he were dizzy and found
7094  it hard to think.
7095  
7096  "How's the glass, Jack?" asked a voice beside him. He turned with
7097  troubled eyes to see Tab in his pajamas, a freshly lighted cigarette
7098  between his fingers. "What's the trouble?" the mate demanded instantly,
7099  seeming bewildered at the captain's appearance.
7100  
7101  "What brought you out here?" the captain retorted, though why he should
7102  have asked he could not have told.
7103  
7104  "Heard you exclaiming. What's the trouble?"
7105  
7106  "Look!" Jack answered, pointing to the glass.
7107  
7108  "All that!" gasped Jerry.
7109  
7110  "Get your togs on," was the only reply Jack offered. "Be quick, and come
7111  on deck."
7112  
7113  Jerrold left him without a word, and padded off to his cabin. Jack reset
7114  the vernier, and went out. To his disturbed mind it seemed as if in the
7115  brief interval during which he had been below the whole appearance of
7116  nature had grown more ominous. In five minutes Jerry was with him.
7117  
7118  "Well, Jack?"
7119  
7120  "I've made up my mind what to do," the captain announced. "It's going to
7121  blow fit to take your hair out by the roots: that much is sure."
7122  
7123  Jerry nodded soberly, and looked his friend straight in the eye.
7124  
7125  "We'll have to lay-to before we see the end of this, and I'd rather do
7126  so at sea-anchor 'n any other way. What do you think?"
7127  
7128  "That's right enough. I suppose we'd better make ready now?"
7129  
7130  "We sha'n't have much time when it does come. We must get a mess of
7131  things together up for'ard fit to hold a liner. We'll need it."
7132  
7133  Jack got the hands together around the winch forward, and set them at
7134  once, under his direction, to the making of the "sea-anchor." The
7135  spinnaker-boom and the two shorter boat-booms were first lashed firmly
7136  together with inch rope in a rough isosceles triangle.
7137  
7138  "Now," Jack ordered, "fetch the old staysail, and bend it on in the
7139  frame."
7140  
7141  "How are you going to ballast the thing?" asked Tab. "It'll float flat
7142  if you don't give it a sinker."
7143  
7144  "I fancy the market-boat's killock would be about the right thing if we
7145  could get at it," Jack answered. "Do you know where"--
7146  
7147  "Yes, yes," interrupted Jerry hastily. "It's with the rest of her gear.
7148  I'll get it." And he went aft.
7149  
7150  Although the wind had not as yet increased in violence, Jack, standing
7151  as he did almost at the peak of the vessel, felt the motion much more
7152  than he had farther aft. The great gray-green seas heaved hard about the
7153  plunging yacht, and every now and then she ran bowsprit under. She was a
7154  rather dry boat, fortunately, of the "hollow bow" model, and in the
7155  fifteen or twenty minutes that the men had been working on the anchor,
7156  she had not taken any waves aboard. The spindrift, it is true, flew
7157  across her by the bucketful, but the men, dressed in their oilers,
7158  blinked the cold water out of their eyes and went on with their work.
7159  Before Jerry returned, however, as the crew were bending the old
7160  staysail to the triangular frame, the captain, to his consternation, saw
7161  that the Merle was just working her way up the breast of a mighty hill
7162  of water with all likelihood of burying herself in the rising wall of a
7163  wave ahead.
7164  
7165  "'Ware water!" he shouted.
7166  
7167  The men dropped their work and caught at whatever was nearest at hand.
7168  Some threw an arm about the bollard by the knighthead; some jumped for
7169  the winch; two men got a tight grip on the large ring-bolts by the port
7170  cat-heads; Jack himself leaped for the winch and put his right arm
7171  around the drum.
7172  
7173  The Merle labored to the crest of the hill of water. It sank away
7174  beneath her instantly, and she shot down the slope of the wave into the
7175  trough of the sea with a headlong, staggering rush. Towering above her
7176  was the roughened, foam-blotched face of the succeeding wave. She tried
7177  bravely to climb it, but she was too near, the angle was too sharp; she
7178  could not so quickly recover from the impetus of her downward plunge.
7179  She seemed to tremble--to hesitate--for an instant, and then as if in
7180  the courage of despair, to leap forward with a jerk into the very midst
7181  of the flood as if she would force her way through its tons of swinging
7182  sea-water.
7183  
7184  Jack went to the deck under the tremendous blow of the on-rushing wave
7185  as if he had been struck down by a thunderbolt. He felt the shock, the
7186  biting cold of the water, and then it seemed as if a giant had gripped
7187  him with hands of ice and were trying to wrench him from his hold. He
7188  clung on, drenched, bewildered, desperate, until he wondered if his arm
7189  would be pulled out of its socket. He had a stifling sensation of having
7190  been for hours without air; he felt as if he were being dragged by some
7191  terrible power swiftly through the sea miles below the surface. On a
7192  sudden he again felt the deck under him, and opened his eyes. The Merle
7193  had forced her way through the wave, and they were again free. He
7194  gasped, spluttered, and rose to his feet, the water streaming from him.
7195  Inside the bulwarks to starboard the green, foam-mixed brine washed
7196  about knee-deep, and was pouring with a hoarse gurgling out of the
7197  scuppers forward. The "anchor" had been swept bodily aft as far as the
7198  foremast, and there was jammed between the mast itself and the
7199  weather-shrouds. Drenched and cursing, the men squelched their way aft,
7200  dislodged the structure, and dragged it forward again. Luckily the
7201  mishap, really a slight one of twenty seconds' duration, had wrought no
7202  damage which could not be easily repaired, and so the crew took up their
7203  work where they had left it.
7204  
7205  Jerry reappeared with the killock of the market-boat just as they got
7206  into place once more.
7207  
7208  "Did you get wet?" he asked cheerily, with a broad grin which showed
7209  that he saw what had happened.
7210  
7211  "What do you think?" burst out the captain hotly. "No; I got dry, damn
7212  it!"
7213  
7214  "Did you really, though! Well, I thought you looked damp."
7215  
7216  Jack paid this boyish jest with a word that was sharp and a look that
7217  was too near a grin not to take the sting from it. He took the killock
7218  that Jerry had brought, and had the men make it fast to the lower point
7219  of the kite-like frame where the short boat-booms met. To the ends of
7220  the long spinnaker-boom he fastened lengths of strong inch Manilla, and
7221  a piece somewhat shorter to the point where the killock was attached.
7222  The captain meant that the "sea-anchor," when in the water, should ride
7223  not exactly vertical, but that by the shorter line the weighted point
7224  should be lifted a little toward the yacht as the Merle dragged back on
7225  it. In the end of each of these lines a bow-line was bent, and through
7226  the bights of them he had the rode bent and made fast. The whole
7227  contrivance was then like a triangular kite weighted at the point made
7228  by the shorter sides, and held by lines from the three corners joined on
7229  the rode, which corresponded to the string. When the work was finished
7230  Jack inspected it all carefully, and examined the fastenings.
7231  
7232  "It's a rough enough concern," he said to Jerry; "but it's stanch, and
7233  if we have to use it, it'll do good service. Make it fast," he added to
7234  the men. "Put on a couple of strong gaskets for stoppers. Come on, Tab;
7235  I don't want another ducking."
7236  
7237  They went aft to the cockpit, and the captain started to go below.
7238  
7239  "I'll just take another look at that glass," he said. "It's well to keep
7240  a"--
7241  
7242  "Look!" cried Jerry suddenly, seizing him by the arm, and pointing away
7243  to the southward.
7244  
7245  Jack's eyes followed the mate's arm. Afar off on the gloomy horizon, the
7246  black sea below and the gray sky above were in one place welded together
7247  by a wall of impenetrable haze. It was not much more than a spot, but
7248  Jack at a glance took in its full significance, and knew that before the
7249  Merle was a struggle that would try her strength and his seamanship to
7250  the very utmost. He opened his mouth to speak, and closed his lips
7251  firmly without a word. He looked a moment at the inky mist, and then
7252  dashed below. In a couple of minutes he reappeared with a grim look on
7253  his usually genial face.
7254  
7255  "Jerry," he said hurriedly, "I've been down and tried the storm-card on
7256  the chart. If we keep on as she's going, we'll fetch up plumb in the
7257  centre of this mess. The Merle wouldn't live there half an hour."
7258  
7259  "Well?" questioned Jerry. His face was sober, and had about it a
7260  suggestion of a big, serious dog that watches its troubled master. "What
7261  can we do?"
7262  
7263  "There is only one thing to do," Jack responded quickly, but with
7264  absolute decision. "The centre bears southwesterly,--that's why our
7265  wind's hauled 'round. We've got to put about and run into the heart of
7266  that greasy streak yonder. It'll be a tough job, but not so bad as if we
7267  were farther westward. When we get the wind westerly, we'll lay to. If
7268  we do anything else, we'll be swept into the centre, sure's fate."
7269  
7270  "Can't we run it out?" Jerry asked desperately. "It'll be tremendous!
7271  That blow we had coming over'll be pale beside it. Think, man!"
7272  
7273  "I have," Jack said shortly. "Ready 'bout ship!" he shouted.
7274  
7275  The men sprang to their places, although Jack could see that they threw
7276  swift glances of surprise at him as they did so. The evidence, slight as
7277  it was, that he was acting alone, and that he must see farther and more
7278  wisely than the men under him, accustomed as they were to the sea,
7279  imparted a new ring of command to his voice as he gave the necessary
7280  orders. With some difficulty and with much uproar of booming canvas and
7281  slatting ropes, the schooner came about, and Jack had her headed
7282  straight for the black spot on the horizon.
7283  
7284  Jack hurried on preparations for the storm before them. He had sail
7285  taken in and double-reefed; the "spitfire" jib set in place of the
7286  larger forestaysail, and tarpaulins battened over the skylights. He put
7287  the yacht as completely as possible in heavy-weather trim, to meet the
7288  gale scudding along over the black sea toward them.
7289  
7290  He was none too soon, for the storm was not long in coming. The gray sky
7291  above the yacht grew darker and darker, the sea about her more and more
7292  "cobbly." The wind freshened rapidly, and veered more toward the west.
7293  The Merle sailed on gallantly, the green waves breaking against her
7294  weather shoulder, and the spindrift flying down the decks as she
7295  slashed her way to windward. The tops of the great seas, as they heaved
7296  themselves skyward, were snatched off by the gale, and sped in white
7297  sheets down the wind.
7298  
7299  Jack was standing in the cockpit with Jerry. He was watching the weather
7300  narrowly, and now and then, with a brief word or two, gave the
7301  steersmen--for the wheel needed two of them--a command or a warning. The
7302  force of the gale so increased that at the end of an hour and a half the
7303  mainsail, though triple-reefed, was got down and furled, and the
7304  forestaysail, which had been unbent to give place to the spitfire, was
7305  set on the boom as a trysail.
7306  
7307  It had come on to rain, and the big drops were driven along almost in
7308  horizontal lines. When they struck the face Jack felt as if he had been
7309  pelted with hailstones. Mixed with the flying spindrift they filled the
7310  air as if with a mist, blinding and fierce.
7311  
7312  Suddenly, as the yacht was dipping into the trough of a long sea, a
7313  strong gust listed her over so that aft the green water rose on the
7314  decks to within a fathom of the cockpit combings. A sharp report burst
7315  out above all the roaring of the wind and the multitudinous clamor of
7316  the waters. Jack looked up to see the trysail streaming out in tattered
7317  ribbons, writhing and twisting like pale snakes in mad fury. The sight
7318  inflamed him like a personal insult flung at him by the storm. He broke
7319  out with a cry, and with a great oath swore he would see the Merle
7320  through in spite of everything.
7321  
7322  "Tab," he shouted in the mate's ear, "get along forward on that
7323  sea-anchor! Stand by to launch it. We don't want any more of this!"
7324  
7325  He saw Jerry gather the port watch,--for all the men had been on deck
7326  for two hours past, clinging to whatever was nearest and alternately
7327  watching the storm and the captain,--and with them scrabble forward,
7328  making way by the help of whatever could be grasped. Their difficulty in
7329  getting forward was to Jack like a sudden realization of the danger they
7330  were in, and made him for the moment think of the men, whereas he had
7331  before been conscious of nothing but of the yacht herself. He saw the
7332  men gather about the "sea-anchor," swaying and pitching with the motion
7333  of the bow, and Jerry turn to look for his signal. The yacht was
7334  carrying such a strong lee-helm that the steersmen could not keep her
7335  head to the wind, and Jack shouted and gesticulated frantically to Jerry
7336  to get down the storm-jib, while at the same time he ordered the
7337  starboard watch to unstop the mainsail. He was in deadly fear lest the
7338  vessel should get clean broadside to the wind and that the decks would
7339  be swept.
7340  
7341  "Unstop the mainsail!" he roared. "Show the peak! Douse the jib!"
7342  
7343  Again he motioned to Jerry, knowing that his voice would not be heard
7344  forward. He saw Tab pause a moment, and then wave his arm in reply. To
7345  his utter dismay, however, he saw the mate and the men with him stoop,
7346  get hold of the "sea-anchor," and, tugging and stumbling, begin to haul
7347  it up to the weather side. It flashed on Jack that his gestures had been
7348  misunderstood, and his order to get down the jib mistaken for a command
7349  to launch the "anchor." With a sickening plunge the Merle at that moment
7350  coasted down a mighty wave, fell off, and lay broadside to the seas. For
7351  a second he felt as if everything was lost.
7352  
7353  "Smartly!" he roared to the starboard watch, who were working for their
7354  lives upon the main-boom.
7355  
7356  He gave them one glance, and started to rush forward, running recklessly
7357  along, and feeling for his sheath-knife as he went. A quick lurch of the
7358  yacht to port flung him off his feet, and shot him forward and to his
7359  right. He instinctively flung out his hand, and clutched something
7360  metallic.
7361  
7362  "'Ware water!" he mumbled, half stunned.
7363  
7364  A green shadow curled over him. There was a crashing roar to leeward. He
7365  felt the yacht stagger and tremble, and suddenly and with an odd mental
7366  twist he remembered vividly an earthquake shock he had once felt at
7367  Patras. The shadow disappeared, a little water came slap! on his oilskin
7368  jacket between the shoulders. The rest of the wave--tons and tons of
7369  green water--had curled itself over him, and crashed on the decks to
7370  leeward.
7371  
7372  He got to his feet unsteadily, and with a queer singing in his ears ran
7373  forward. He threw a quick look to port as he ran. The force of the sea
7374  had evidently been heaviest amidships, for he saw that for thirty feet
7375  on the lee beam the rail had been burst out between the fore and main
7376  rigging; two boats were gone, and the skylights, broken, yawned blackly.
7377  Jack groaned inwardly, but did not stop. Pitching and staggering, he
7378  made his way to the foremast. A sudden fling of the yacht threatened to
7379  make him, as he afterward put it, "overshoot the mark" and tumble past
7380  the halyards. Fortunately, however, he checked himself by catching at
7381  the foretopsail-clewline as he was being pitched by, and he clung to it
7382  desperately. He laid hold of the spitfire halyard. One quick glance at
7383  the turns about the pin in the rack told him how much time he should
7384  save by cutting the rope, and with a swift backdrawing of the sharp
7385  sheath-knife he severed it. The fall of the halyard flew up aloft,
7386  playfully dealing him a smart rap on the chin as it went; the sail ran
7387  down in thunder, and blew away in shreds. The Merle began to rise, and
7388  Jack felt a thrill of joyful relief to see that she was coming up into
7389  the wind. The men aft had showed the peak of the mainsail, and the
7390  schooner was feeling its effects.
7391  
7392  A few yards forward, Jerry and the port watch were still toiling over
7393  the "sea-anchor." Twice they had tried to set it in position for
7394  launching, and each time wind and sea had overmastered them. Jack, in an
7395  agony lest the structure should be launched before the yacht was laid
7396  about on the other tack, or at least so near the wind that the awkward
7397  contrivance could be got over the bows to port, stumbled forward
7398  shouting.
7399  
7400  "To port!" he roared. "Get it over to port!"
7401  
7402  He gripped Jerry by the arm.
7403  
7404  "The wrong tack!" he bellowed in the mate's ear. "Run it over to
7405  leeward, and put it over when I wave my arm. Watch sharp!"
7406  
7407  "Aye!" shouted Tab, but Jack was already gone.
7408  
7409  Castleport stumbled aft much as he had gone forward, now climbing
7410  laboriously up hill, now leaning back and struggling to keep himself
7411  from rushing headlong down the sloping deck with an impetus that would
7412  have carried him overboard. When he reached the cockpit, he dropped
7413  inside almost spent.
7414  
7415  "Back the helm every time she rises!" he called to the men at the wheel.
7416  "We want her to fall over!"
7417  
7418  "Aye, aye, sir."
7419  
7420  "Now, then,--over with her!" he cried, as the yacht rose.
7421  
7422  The men gave her all they dared. The effect was imperceptible.
7423  
7424  "Hold her!" shouted Jack.
7425  
7426  At the risk of their lives, the two helmsmen held her as the schooner
7427  slid down the big slope of the wave, shivering as she went. As she rose,
7428  the captain, with a laughing heart, saw that she would make it. He tore
7429  off his "sou'-wester," and waved it frantically to Tab forward. Jerry
7430  threw up his arm in reply; the big "sea-anchor" rose from the deck, and
7431  went out on the port side.
7432  
7433  "Helm amidships!" sang out Jack.
7434  
7435  "Aye, aye, sir."
7436  
7437  The Merle began to drift back.
7438  
7439  "Watch along!" the captain roared again. "Gaskets on the mainsail!"
7440  
7441  The starboard watch began to wrestle with the heavy canvas which they
7442  had partially freed from its bonds so short a time before. The sail was
7443  made snug, and the Merle dragged back on her "anchor," and though she
7444  plunged and tugged, pitched and rolled, still kept her sharp nose to the
7445  wind. Through the mist of the stinging brine which the wind drove down
7446  the decks in sheets, the captain saw the hands forward pay out some
7447  forty fathoms of scope, and then, man by man, work their way aft.
7448  
7449  "I'm awfully sorry I--I made such a mess," Tab shouted in the captain's
7450  ear as he reached him.
7451  
7452  "It's all right," returned Jack, aglow with a wild exultation. "It's all
7453  right! No matter."
7454  
7455  The ominous belt of opaque mist which they had so shortly before seen on
7456  the horizon was now all about them. The Merle and her crew were
7457  enveloped in a shroud of rushing rain. It drove before the blast in
7458  incredible torrents, and with a force that made them catch their breaths
7459  chokingly whenever they faced it. The seas increased to frightful size.
7460  Even to the sailors, bred on the sea, it seemed hardly possible that
7461  the schooner could live in such surges. The cockpit, although
7462  self-bailing, was kept flooded; in it the water, sloshing about with the
7463  motion of the schooner, was as high as the transoms. The uproar of the
7464  wind, singing on the ropes strung by its own force to tautness, was like
7465  the shrieking of an immense and untuned harp. The crash of the waves
7466  sounded like a continuous cannonade all about the yacht. The mingling of
7467  sea and air produced a vertigo, as if everything was resolving again
7468  into its original chaos. Yet in the midst of it all Jack felt his blood
7469  sing in his veins with pure joy of the battle.
7470  
7471  Suddenly the captain remembered the broken skylights. He splashed out of
7472  the cockpit, where he stood almost waist-deep in the jumping water,
7473  steadied himself by the combings, and started forward.
7474  
7475  "Pumps!" he shouted. "Come!"
7476  
7477  He waved his arm to the men, and the yellow-clad figures detached
7478  themselves in the mist and blurring rain from the points of vantage to
7479  which they had clung, and dumb, obedient, followed him.
7480  
7481  The pumps were just abaft the foremast, and were of the semi-rotary
7482  sort. The bars were fitted, and two of the men, swinging themselves
7483  back and forth, back and forth, with a dull and dreary monotony, began
7484  pumping as if they had become parts of a machine. A steady flow of water
7485  came from the waste-pipe in a continuous stream. It spread out over the
7486  deck to port and to starboard as the yacht swayed. It was full of
7487  bubbles and flecks of froth, and was a sickly yellow in hue.
7488  
7489  Jack set the rest of the men to stretch new tarpaulins over the gaping
7490  skylights, and then he went below to look at the glass. Drenched,
7491  bruised, cold from his long fight with the storm and the hours which had
7492  gone by without his having had food, he found himself, now that for the
7493  moment action was not imperative, seized with a sort of terror at the
7494  perils he had gone through. The instant reflection that worse might be
7495  yet to come restored his courage. He could face whatever might befall as
7496  long as he might act.
7497  
7498  The sight which met him in the once trig cabin was sufficiently
7499  dispiriting. A thin sheet of water swashed softly about over the Turkish
7500  carpet. It chuckled in dark places as if sentient and fully aware of the
7501  impropriety of its being there. A locker door had burst open, and was
7502  banging maddeningly. Farther forward, in the dark staterooms, similar
7503  noises could be heard, with sounds which suggested that all sorts of
7504  small things were being flung about. Everything was sopped with
7505  sea-water and drenched by the beating rain: the transom-cushions, two of
7506  which were skating about the cabin with the wicker deck-chairs; the
7507  books on their shelves; the lockers, the mirrors, the sheathing, down
7508  which large drops ran in dizzying zigzags,--in short, everything. The
7509  sight gave Jack a feeling of discouragement worse than anything on
7510  deck--even the tearing away of the bulwarks--had been able to produce.
7511  He felt as if the cruel old ocean were mouthing the schooner as a beast
7512  breaks the bones of its prey before devouring it. He drew in his breath
7513  with fierce resolution, all his combative spirit aroused to fight to the
7514  last gasp, and made his stumbling way to the barometer. He steadied it
7515  with his hand, and read it. It stood at 27.04. This was a drop of only
7516  .05 since his last observation, and the captain's face cleared a little.
7517  If the glass had practically stopped falling, as apparently it had, the
7518  hardest part of the gale would come soon, and be speedily over. The old
7519  weather saw came into his head,--
7520  
7521  
7522       Long foretold, long last;
7523       Short notice, soon past.
7524  
7525  
7526  The relief, slight as it was, affected him so strongly that he almost
7527  smiled. He reflected that the Merle was as well prepared to meet it as
7528  under the circumstances she could be, and he had no real doubt of her
7529  ability to ride it out, unless some unexpected accident disabled the
7530  "sea-anchor."
7531  
7532  When he came on deck he was greeted by Tab, who had taken charge in his
7533  absence, and who asked eagerly the state of the glass. Jack told him,
7534  and drawing him into the companionway, where they could escape the wind
7535  enough to talk, he added his reasons for thinking that a short time
7536  might see them through the worst.
7537  
7538  "How are things below?" asked the mate.
7539  
7540  "Look!" the captain answered, with a sweep of his hand.
7541  
7542  Tab bent down and peered into the dismantled cabin.
7543  
7544  "The devil!" he cried in dismay.
7545  
7546  "Precisely--but it might be worse," returned Jack; "but by George, Tab!"
7547  he burst out with sudden vehemence, "I--I'm glad I haven't got all this
7548  to do over again. You don't know--can't imagine the strain of this sort
7549  of thing."
7550  
7551  "Does your conscience get up like a cat with the wind?" laughed Jerry.
7552  
7553  "No, Tab," Jack answered soberly, "but the men, you know, and thinking I
7554  took them into this when I'd no right to. Oh, rot! No matter, only I'm
7555  jolly glad I ran off with the Merle before I realized all this. I
7556  couldn't bring myself to do it again for"--
7557  
7558  "Come on deck, Jacko," Tab said, after a brief silence in which with
7559  eyes cast down awkwardly he had waited for the captain to continue. "I
7560  know how you feel, but thank the Lord there's work to be done, and we'll
7561  fight through all right. Besides, Gonzague's forward getting a ration of
7562  some sort. We can't afford to miss that."
7563  
7564  He put out his hand, and Jack grasped it appreciatively, with a
7565  half-conscious thanksgiving for the comfort of a friend.
7566  
7567  "Right you are!" the captain said heartily. "We're both of us ready for
7568  a feed, I fancy."
7569  
7570  And out into the storm they went again, buoyant and ready.
7571  
7572  [Illustration: Decoration]
7573  
7574  
7575  
7576  
7577  Chapter Seventeen
7578  
7579  FACING THE MUSIC
7580  
7581  
7582  "Well," Tab said, "I'll see you as far as the door for fear you'll bolt.
7583  You're a sight nearer funking than I ever saw you, Jacko. You must have
7584  your nerve with you if you don't want to come out of the little end of
7585  the horn."
7586  
7587  "I feel small enough to go through it," Jack retorted.
7588  
7589  "Oh, that's all right. Just take a brace, and"--
7590  
7591  "Humph!" snorted the captain. "It's all well enough for you to snoozle
7592  round and give me advice, but if you had to face Uncle Randolph
7593  yourself, you wouldn't be so chipper, let me tell you!"
7594  
7595  The young men were crossing Atlantic Avenue not far from the East Boston
7596  Ferry. They had at last, sea-weary and glad of land, made harbor on the
7597  previous evening. Jack had hardly waited for the anchor to be down
7598  before he had sent off in haste for his European letters, intrusting the
7599  messenger to post a voluminous epistle on which he had written
7600  industriously at intervals all the way over; and for half the night he
7601  had read and reread Katrine's missives, giving Jerry tantalizing bits
7602  now and then, with messages from Mrs. Fairhew enjoining him not again to
7603  aid and abet Jack in any nefarious schemes. In the morning the crew had
7604  been paid off generously, and given passages on the City of Rockland.
7605  Then Gonzague had been left in charge of the yacht, and now, with
7606  feelings curiously mixed, the captain was bound for the office of his
7607  uncle for the inevitable reckoning with the owner of the stolen Merle.
7608  
7609  It was a bright, sharp morning, without a cloud in the sky. The air had
7610  a clean crispness which went to the head like wine. The streets were
7611  thronged and noisy. Heavy trucks rolled past the pair like batteries
7612  moving into action; the Elevated thundered overhead with its rumbling
7613  screech. The teamsters shouted profanely at their straining horses; a
7614  fat policeman at the crowded crossing waved his arms like semaphores,
7615  now holding up the traffic and again with commanding gesture sweeping it
7616  along. The shrill voices of the newsboys rang out in mechanical
7617  iteration of the leading sensations of the morning journals.
7618  
7619  "Oh," cried Tab, as they walked briskly up State Street, "how good it
7620  is, isn't it, Jacko?"
7621  
7622  Jack was too much absorbed in the interview before him to do more than
7623  nod mechanically. He could not at the moment bring himself up to the gay
7624  mood of his friend.
7625  
7626  "There's no place like it after all," Jerry ran on, his honest, homely
7627  face aglow with delight. "My word, you may talk about Italy and all the
7628  rest of it till the crack of doom, but they can't hold a candle to good
7629  old Boston! Blest if this isn't the best part of the whole cruise!"
7630  
7631  "Think so, do you?" asked Jack dryly. "It's funny, but the very reverse
7632  was in my head. What the deuce," he burst out, "what the deuce am I
7633  going to tell the President anyway?"
7634  
7635  "Oh, just give him the yarn off the reel," returned Tab, as if it were
7636  all the simplest thing in the world. "You've got the log with you,
7637  and--I say, do look at those pigeons! Aren't they jolly! Come, brace
7638  up!"
7639  
7640  "Oh, yes," said Jack. "Brace up, of course--in the very mouth of the
7641  lion's lair. Here's the building,--we're just about seventy feet under
7642  Uncle Randolph's den. Brace up! The very thing, of course! So glad you
7643  suggested it!"
7644  
7645  "Now, Jacko," protested Jerry, "you mustn't take things this way. Do
7646  put some spirit into it. I'll leave you here; but if you want, I'll face
7647  the music with you."
7648  
7649  "No, thank you," his friend said gravely; "I'll take the medicine
7650  alone."
7651  
7652  "Well, that's what we decided last night when we threshed things out. Go
7653  ahead. Bring the remains round to lunch, though. The Roundheads at one.
7654  It's eleven now, and you've got two hours for the job of placating the
7655  president. Come sure; for I shall be in a stew till I know how you two
7656  get on together."
7657  
7658  "All right," Jack responded dispiritedly.
7659  
7660  "Good luck," Jerry said, stretching out his hand.
7661  
7662  "Thank you," Jack returned, giving Tab a hearty grasp. "So long."
7663  
7664  "One o'clock," Jerry repeated; and with a buoyant wave of the hand, he
7665  went on his way up State Street.
7666  
7667  "Suppose he'll weep when he sees the Frog Pond," muttered Jack to
7668  himself with a wan smile. "Wish I felt half as chipper."
7669  
7670  He went to the elevator, and pressed the electric button. The big cage
7671  came down, the boy clashed the door, and Jack went in as he might have
7672  mounted the steps to a scaffold.
7673  
7674  "Mr. Drake's," he said briefly, moistening his lips, and wondering why
7675  they seemed so stiff and dry.
7676  
7677  Deposited on the proper floor, he tucked the brown log-book more tightly
7678  under his arm, and approached his uncle's office.
7679  
7680  "I must have time," he said to himself. "I haven't thought this business
7681  out for a cent."
7682  
7683  He turned on his heel, and walked slowly down the marble-flagged
7684  corridor past the glazed doors of half a dozen offices. Then he stopped
7685  with sudden resolution.
7686  
7687  "Damn it! Be a man!" he adjured himself. "This won't do."
7688  
7689  He walked resolutely up to the door, and entered his uncle's outer
7690  office. A typewriter was clicking busily at one desk, and various clerks
7691  were scratching away assiduously. Several people were seated about,
7692  evidently waiting to speak with Mr. Drake. Even as Jack entered, the
7693  door opened, and a man came out from the inner room. The head clerk
7694  nodded to Jack, but regarded him curiously.
7695  
7696  "How do you do, Mr. Castleport?" he said.
7697  
7698  "Can I see my uncle?" Jack asked, returning his salutation, and he added
7699  to himself, "He knows all about the Merle. I can tell by his looks."
7700  
7701  "He's pretty busy this morning," the clerk answered, "but I'll tell him
7702  you're here. Of course he'll see you as soon as he can."
7703  
7704  Jack took a seat and waited until the next man came out of the inner
7705  office. Then the head clerk went in, and in a moment returned with a
7706  queer look on his face. "Mr. Drake says these men are here by
7707  appointment," he reported, "and he cannot see you till they are gone."
7708
