7709  "All right," Jack answered, reflecting ruefully that he was not
7710  accustomed to be thus kept waiting in his uncle's office. "I am in no
7711  hurry."
7712  
7713  He settled himself in his chair, feeling that he could have borne
7714  anything better than this delay, and half tempted now to give it up, and
7715  beat a retreat. He saw one man after another go into the inner room, and
7716  after a time return and go away. He crossed and recrossed his legs with
7717  an impatient feeling that he had never sat in so uncomfortable a chair.
7718  He tried to beguile the time by reading the log, but first he opened to
7719  the account of the lifting of the Merle, and then to the story of how
7720  her bulwarks were torn away by the storm. He fell to thinking how good
7721  Uncle Randolph had always been to him, and every minute felt more and
7722  more like a wretch for having left the old gentleman stranded at North
7723  Haven. The time grew longer and longer, and every moment more
7724  intolerable as the second hour began to drag its slow length after the
7725  first. Then he noticed that only one man remained to delay his
7726  interview, and so completely was he demoralized that he felt that he
7727  would have given anything in the world to be excused from the trial
7728  before him. It seemed to him that the last man but one did his business,
7729  whatever it was, in an amazingly short time; and he all but bolted when
7730  the last went to his appointment. If he could get away and think things
7731  over once more, he might perhaps be able to devise some sort of excuse
7732  more plausible than anything he had to offer; and he all but started to
7733  his feet to fly when the door opened to let out the only visitor who had
7734  stood between him and the dreaded encounter with the president.
7735  
7736  "Mr. Drake will see you now, sir," said the office boy.
7737  
7738  Jack got to his feet as if by automatic action, and felt them drag him
7739  forward against his will. Another instant, and the door had closed
7740  behind him; he stood in the inner office. With a tremendous effort--an
7741  effort which was almost physical--to pull himself together, he looked up
7742  at his uncle.
7743  
7744  He saw a slight gentleman, dressed in a well-fitting suit of gray,
7745  looking out of one of the windows with his back to the door. The office
7746  was high enough to command a view of the harbor, shining blue in the sun
7747  beyond the clusters of roofs and chimneys. Mr. Drake stood for a moment
7748  as if examining the view for the first time, while Jack wondered whether
7749  this unconsciousness of his presence was real, or was of a piece with
7750  the infliction of the long wait. Then the President turned to him, and
7751  bowed formally, as if to a stranger. His face wore a curious look of
7752  weariness and patience which somehow reminded Jack of his father. The
7753  high forehead was wrinkled with a line or two that Jack did not
7754  remember, and the curly hair was surely more thickly streaked with gray.
7755  
7756  "Well, sir?" Mr. Drake said in a tone hard and even.
7757  
7758  "Well, Uncle Randolph," said Jack, confused, "I--I'm here."
7759  
7760  "So I see," remarked the President. "Is that what you came to say?"
7761  
7762  Jack felt that the interview promised to be even worse than he had
7763  feared. He shuffled his feet uncomfortably, and studied the figures in
7764  the rug. Then he looked up at the face of the elder man, and something
7765  in it smote him to the heart.
7766  
7767  "Uncle Randolph," he said suddenly, "I suppose it's pretty late to say
7768  anything of the sort, but--but something that happened on the way over
7769  made me see that--made me see what a blackguard I'd been to steal the
7770  Merle as I did. I don't think apologies are much good, anyway,
7771  especially after you've had all the fun. It's a good deal like trying to
7772  sneak out of consequences, but I--I really mean most sincerely that I'm
7773  beastly sorry."
7774  
7775  Mr. Drake did not move a muscle of his keen, well-bred face, but into
7776  his eyes came some faint glint of humor which made Jack stop in
7777  confusion.
7778  
7779  "Are you done, sir?" his uncle asked.
7780  
7781  "I'm not quite through, sir," Jack said in a sort of desperate humility.
7782  "I--I--that is"--He floundered for a moment, and then went on with a
7783  rush, "I may as well explain that I'm not sorry one way; that is--I
7784  can't honestly say I wish I hadn't taken the Merle, for I--you know I'm
7785  engaged to Miss Marchfield, and I never could have been except--that is,
7786  unless I'd got over there. I can't be sorry for that."
7787  
7788  "No?" queried Mr. Drake, raising his brows. "You are not thinking,
7789  perhaps, what is the price I have paid for the privilege of
7790  congratulating you on this engagement. I have no son, and from the day
7791  your father died I have made one of you. You deceive me, humiliate me in
7792  the eyes of my guests, make me the joke of my club, leave me high and
7793  dry at North Haven"--
7794  
7795  Sad and sorry as Jack really was, he could not help the impulse that
7796  made him see the chance, and murmur under his breath,--
7797  
7798  "I didn't think anything could be high and dry in the sort of fog we
7799  went off in."
7800  
7801  His uncle gave a slight cough, as if he were strangling an inclination
7802  to laugh, and then went on in the same even voice as before.
7803  
7804  "Of course I can't expect you to have any feeling about the way I felt
7805  about your tricking me, any more than of the anxiety I went through when
7806  the Merle disappeared, and I didn't know whether you were on top of the
7807  sea or under it."
7808  
7809  "I--I never thought of that," stammered Jack, feeling his cheeks grow
7810  hot.
7811  
7812  "No, I suppose not. Nor how I enjoyed the storm you must have been in on
7813  the way home. Lloyd's people sent me word of your giving them the slip
7814  at Plymouth."
7815  
7816  "But they let us," Jack put in eagerly, seizing with avidity at any
7817  point which seemed to afford him a chance to defend himself. "I didn't
7818  think, Uncle Randolph, and I'm afraid I've been a beastly cad to you. I
7819  am sorry to the very bottom of my heart."
7820  
7821  The President took a quick stride forward and clapped one hand on his
7822  nephew's shoulder, while with the other he grasped warmly the hand Jack
7823  put out swiftly to meet him.
7824  
7825  "There, Jack," he said, "that's all I want. You don't know what we old
7826  fools go through worrying over you young ones. Perhaps it's just as well
7827  you don't."
7828  
7829  He gave Jack's hand a vigorous shake, and then turned away to blow his
7830  own nose with equal violence. Jack himself felt hot in the eyes, but he
7831  had no words which seemed adequate to the situation.
7832  
7833  "Sit down," his uncle said, waving him to a chair, and then going to his
7834  desk. He took from a pigeon-hole some letters and papers. "I have
7835  several things to say to you. Mrs. Fairhew writes a very spicy letter
7836  when she wants to."
7837  
7838  "I should think she might, sir. She can be spicy when she talks."
7839  
7840  "She says I didn't know you were grown-up, Jack."
7841  
7842  Jack blushed at the remembrance, vivid and sharp, of his declaration to
7843  Jerry that he would make his uncle realize that he had come to man's
7844  estate.
7845  
7846  "Oh, ho," said Mr. Drake, regarding him keenly, but with humorous eyes,
7847  "you thought so too, did you? Of course you did! Well, I know it now,
7848  and I've been an old fool. I congratulate you, Jack, with all my heart.
7849  If Miss Marchfield is like her mother"--He broke off as if his thought
7850  had got the better of his speech. "If she is all that Mrs. Fairhew says
7851  she is, you have a treasure, my boy. Don't ever run off with her yacht."
7852  
7853  "I never mean to repeat that performance with anybody," Jack declared
7854  stoutly, again shaking hands fervently. "You've always been awfully good
7855  to me, Uncle Randolph, and I've never done anything for you."
7856  
7857  "Hum, perhaps not that you know of," the other replied, with a humorous
7858  lift of his eyebrows; "but we sometimes do good when we think we're
7859  doing harm. Read this."
7860  
7861  He held out a long blue envelope, much stamped and written upon, and
7862  provided with both American and English postage-stamps. Jack knew it at
7863  a glance as the one he had taken from the messenger that foggy night at
7864  North Haven, had found in the pocket of his coat at Nice, and had after
7865  much cogitation remailed at Plymouth. In the upper left-hand corner was
7866  the notice to return to R. B. Tillington, if not delivered in five
7867  days, and the Boston address written in his own hand. He drew out the
7868  letter and read:--
7869  
7870  
7871       MY DEAR DRAKE,--You and I have known the ins and outs of the market
7872       for so many years that we ought to appreciate both the danger of
7873       getting into an unsound stock and the foolishness of letting the
7874       real thing go by for the want of a little courage. I think you are
7875       not likely to have forgotten what Orrington said in the club last
7876       week about Orion Copper, or that I told you I meant to sift that
7877       thing to the very bottom. Well, I have been looking it up with a
7878       microscope ever since. I enclose three or four copies of
7879       letters,--this is all confidential, of course; you would know that
7880       without my saying so, but the thing's too important not to be
7881       particular about. I write to you because I've got to have somebody
7882       share the thing, and I think you can raise the money without
7883       putting anybody on the scent. Besides that, we have always got on
7884       well together, I believe in your luck, and I want somebody to stand
7885       with me in running the whole thing. There's nothing less than
7886       millions in it if we can get control at once. Sell anything,--I'm
7887       selling _everything_ myself,--and get in on the ground floor of
7888       Orion. If I had known just where to hit you, I'd have got you to
7889       town to investigate for yourself; but I've wasted a small fortune
7890       already telegraphing to every damned port on the coast I could
7891       think of. You'll find wires waiting at every place you put into.
7892       Orion's bound to be the coming financial constellation. B. B.,
7893       Mellington, Foster, and two or three others have blundered into it
7894       just by bull luck, but they haven't got enough stock to hurt us if
7895       you'll stand by me.
7896  
7897       Yours for Orion,
7898       R. B. T.
7899  
7900  
7901  Jack read in steadily increasing consternation.
7902  
7903  "Good heavens!" he said. "Did I make you lose the chance? Did you get
7904  the telegrams?"
7905  
7906  "I got them, but they referred me to the letter, and I was too upset
7907  about the Merle to pay much attention. Then I went over to the island,
7908  and stayed there three or four days; so that by the time I did get a
7909  letter--a second one--the whole thing was over."
7910  
7911  "Was that what broke Tillington?" Jack asked, feeling as if his escapade
7912  had destroyed half the financial world.
7913  
7914  "It saved me from going with him," Mr. Drake returned, with a smile.
7915  "See here." He extended a lot of newspaper cuttings, and then drew them
7916  back. "Never mind, though," he went on. "There's no need of going into
7917  the particulars. The whole thing was a trap from beginning to end. If
7918  you made a fool of me, Jack, by running off with the Merle, it isn't a
7919  circumstance to the fool I'd have made of myself if I'd got that letter.
7920  If it hadn't been for that perfectly heartless and entirely inexcusable
7921  performance of yours, we'd both of us be beggars at this blessed moment.
7922  We came so near it that I can't read that sign downstairs, 'Beggars and
7923  Peddlers not Allowed,' without thinking how near I was to having it
7924  forbid me my own office."
7925  
7926  "Do you really mean it, Uncle Randolph?" Jack asked half breathlessly.
7927  
7928  "I do mean it, my boy, though I'm afraid the moral of it all's pretty
7929  crooked. I had been led in with a cleverness that gives me cold shivers.
7930  That talk at the club that I'd heard as if by accident had all been
7931  planned out, and so on for a lot more things I won't go into.
7932  Mellington's blown his brains out, and poor old Foster isn't up to
7933  anything but cadging for drinks at the club, and telling how he was
7934  roped in when he was drunk, poor old fellow! I was so sure of Orion that
7935  I'd have put in the last dollar of yours or mine I could have laid
7936  hands on! I feel like a humbug when men congratulate me on knowing
7937  enough to keep out of the mess."
7938  
7939  "And I saved you?" cried Jack, bending forward with boyish eagerness.
7940  
7941  "Yes, you rascally jackanapes; but small credit to you!"
7942  
7943  Jack sent the log up into the air, and, bounding to his feet, caught it
7944  as it fell.
7945  
7946  "Whoop!" he shouted. "Oh, how glad I am old Tillington wrote that letter
7947  and I carried it off!"
7948  
7949  The President laughed with responsive joyousness, but reminded his
7950  ebullient nephew that there were clerks in the other room. He began to
7951  ask questions about the voyage, but the clock struck one and Jack
7952  recalled the fact that Taberman was waiting for him at the Roundheads,
7953  and probably was on tenterhooks for his news.
7954  
7955  "You'll come to luncheon, won't you, sir?" he pleaded.
7956  
7957  "That'll look well," retorted his uncle with humorous derision.
7958  "Everybody knows about your running off with the Merle--Bardale couldn't
7959  hold his tongue--and I shall be accused of condoning a felony."
7960  
7961  Nevertheless they set out arm in arm for the club, and as they went the
7962  President informed his secretary that he should not be back at the
7963  office that afternoon.
7964  
7965  "We shall want to run over the log," he explained to Jack as they waited
7966  for the elevator. "I've no doubt it will make you blush to have me read
7967  it, but I'm going to."
7968  
7969  "I brought it for you," Jack answered, with a grin of pure joy. "Do you
7970  mind waiting a minute, while I send a cable to Katrine? She was awfully
7971  anxious to know how hard you'd be on me."
7972  
7973  "Now she'll think I've no backbone at all. Well, when you played me that
7974  trick, Jack, I felt terribly old and alone; but I think I am a little
7975  bit younger now you're back, and prepared to behave yourself."
7976  
7977  "Wait till you've read the log," laughed Jack, "and you'll think you're
7978  in your teens!"
7979  
7980  [Illustration: Decoration]
7981  
7982  
7983  
7984  
7985  Chapter Eighteen
7986  
7987  EPILUDE
7988  
7989  
7990  Jack, who had been dining at Mrs. Fairhew's, was taking leave of Katrine
7991  one evening a few weeks before the day set for the wedding. The farewell
7992  had all the characteristic deliberateness which has marked the unwilling
7993  separation of engaged couples from time immemorial, and was to-night
7994  prolonged more than usual by his teasing refusal to answer a question.
7995  
7996  "Do tell me what the great secret is between you and Mr. Drake, Jack,"
7997  she begged. "I think you are perfectly horrid!"
7998  
7999  He looked down into her face and laughed softly.
8000  
8001  "You're not," he returned. "You're perfectly stunning to-night."
8002  
8003  "Of course I am," she retorted, laughing and pouting; "but you can't put
8004  me off with a compliment. If you hadn't meant to tell me, you wouldn't
8005  have spoken about it at all; and I think you've teased me enough. What
8006  is it about the President and you?"
8007  
8008  She touched the tips of her fingers to his cravat, as if she were
8009  straightening it, whereas she was probably only exerting instinctively
8010  her privilege of proprietorship in Jack and his belongings.
8011  
8012  "Well," he laughed, "you have borne it beautifully, and I've had you
8013  crazy with curiosity till I don't dare put off telling you. But you'll
8014  probably lie awake half the night thinking about it."
8015  
8016  "That depends upon how important it is."
8017  
8018  "I expect to be paid for telling you," he declared with a look that made
8019  her flush.
8020  
8021  "I should think you might be generous enough to tell me for nothing,"
8022  she responded; but her dimples deepened.
8023  
8024  He stooped forward quickly, and kissed her. Then he took both her hands
8025  in his, and stood caressing them while he went on.
8026  
8027  "The news is this," he said. "We've got to change our plans for the
8028  wedding journey from stem to stern."
8029  
8030  "Why, Jack! What do you mean?"
8031  
8032  "It's a fact, dear," he went on, assuming an expression of profound
8033  regret which was too obviously artificial to be depressing.
8034  
8035  "But why?"
8036  
8037  "Because--Are you ready for a great shock? Wouldn't you like me to
8038  support you in case you couldn't bear it?"
8039  
8040  "Don't be silly," she urged, with an adorable smile. "Because what?"
8041  
8042  "Because Uncle Randolph has given us the Merle as a wedding present. He
8043  told me this afternoon, so that we should have time to shape our plans
8044  accordingly."
8045  
8046  "Oh, dear Jack!"
8047  
8048  "Splendid of him, isn't it? How would it strike you to have the Merle
8049  sent over and to take a whole year in her on the Mediterranean?"
8050  
8051  "Oh, that would be too beautiful!" Katrine cried.
8052  
8053  She clasped her hands, and looked up at him with loving brave eyes. Her
8054  first thought was of his pleasure, and instantly followed the reflection
8055  that she was making her first sacrifice; for her quick mind foresaw that
8056  Jack on a yacht, with duties in which he delighted, would probably be
8057  less wholly hers than in the travel by land which they had arranged. She
8058  smiled wonderfully, and for the first time in their engagement she bent
8059  forward of her own accord, and offered him her lips.
8060
